Talaq — Divorce
advanced
10 min
Dispute on Dowry and Iddah After Judge's Separation
Intra-Hanafi dispute on dowry and iddah after remarriage in iddah following judicial separation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the views of the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) and Imam Muhammad alone on the dowry and iddah in a case of non-compatible marriage, judge's separation, and remarriage in iddah.

Core Idea
The Sahibayn require full dowry and new iddah by istihsan; Muhammad requires half dowry and completion of first iddah.

This lesson analyzes the fourth scenario in the list, which involves a woman who marries a non-compatible match (ghayr kuf'), the judge separates them, then the man remarries her during her iddah, and they are separated again before consummation. The two companions and Imam Muhammad disagree on the dowry and iddah obligations.

Guided Path
1
State the base facts
Woman married a non-compatible man, consummated marriage, then judge separated at guardian's request. Then the same man remarries her in iddah with a new dowry, and judge separates them again before consummation.
The first marriage was considered invalid due to incompatibility.
2
Identify the dispute on dowry
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) say the man must pay the full second dowry. Imam Muhammad says only half the second dowry is due.
The difference stems from whether the second marriage is considered a valid marriage that ended by judicial divorce before consummation (half dowry) or something else.
3
Identify the dispute on iddah
The Sahibayn say a new iddah is required for the second marriage. Muhammad says she completes the first iddah (the one from the ba'in divorce).
This depends on whether the second marriage is considered to have created a new relationship requiring iddah.
4
Understand the usul basis
The Sahibayn rely on istihsan, treating the second marriage as a valid but defective marriage that ends by judicial separation, thus requiring new iddah. Muhammad treats it as a continuation of the first invalid marriage, so the first iddah suffices.
Istihsan is a Hanafi principle of juristic preference overriding qiyas.

Source Text

Fifth: He married her as a minor and consummated, then divorced her irrevocably, then married her during the iddah, then she apostatized (God forbid), then she became Muslim, then he married her during the iddah, then divorced her before consummation. Seventh: He married her and consummated, then divorced her irrevocably, then married her during the iddah, then she apostatized, then became Muslim, then he married her during the iddah, then divorced her before consummation. Commentary
This passage is a continuation of a list of specific cases regarding a woman divorced irrevocably (ba'in) who remarries during her iddah period. The author presents cases involving apostasy, a minor reaching puberty and choosing herself, and a slave woman being freed and choosing herself. The commentary critically examines textual variants found in Fath al-Qadir and other works, pointing out inconsistencies and correcting the number of marriages in each scenario. The key point is that the rulings depend on the sequence of events, and the author clarifies that only two marriages are needed in some cases, not three.

Key Terms
كفء (kuf'): Compatibility or equality in marriage, particularly in lineage, religion, and financial status.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; a method of ijtihad that allows a ruling to be based on a stronger evidence or equitable consideration, overriding qiyas.
فرق القاضي (farraqa al-qadi): The judge separated them, i.e., annulled the marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi (Sahibayn), what is the dowry and iddah in the fourth scenario?
A) Full dowry and new iddah
B) Half dowry and complete first iddah
C) Full dowry and no iddah
D) Half dowry and new iddah

Key Takeaway
Istihsan can override qiyas in determining dowry and iddah when a marriage is judicially separated twice.

Textual Criticism in Fath al-Qadir: Reconstructing the Sixth Scenario
Textual criticism of the sixth scenario in Fath al-Qadir

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the textual variant in the sixth scenario and reconstruct the correct version based on the author's argument and al-Tatarkhaniyya.

Core Idea
The sixth scenario in some copies is a distortion; the correct version involves consummation, not lack thereof.

This lesson focuses on the author's critical refinement regarding the sixth scenario in the list. The author notes textual variants in Fath al-Qadir and other sources, and corrects the description of the sixth scenario involving a minor wife. The correct version, supported by al-Tatarkhaniyya, includes consummation, which explains why iddah is required.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
The author is reviewing a list of ten scenarios. The sixth scenario is missing or distorted in some manuscripts. The author discusses variants found in different copies.
This is a common issue in classical texts; copyist errors can affect legal understanding.
2
Identify the variant
In some copies, the sixth scenario reads: 'He married her as a minor and did not consummate, then she attained puberty and chose herself, then he married her during the iddah, then divorced her before consummation.'
The key issue is 'did not consummate' (فلم يدخل بها).
3
Analyze the author's objection
The author argues that if there was no consummation, how can iddah be incumbent? Therefore, the correct version must have consummation.
Iddah is only required after consummation or valid seclusion. Without consummation, no iddah, so the scenario would be contradictory.
4
Accept the correction from al-Tatarkhaniyya
The author cites al-Tatarkhaniyya which says the correct wording is 'and he consummated' (ودخل بها). The sixth scenario should be: 'He married her as a minor and consummated, then she attained puberty and chose herself, then he married her during iddah...'
This correction resolves the inconsistency.

Talaq — Divorce

Ihdad: Obligation and Prohibitions for the Widow and Divorcée
Definition, ruling, proof, and list of prohibitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of ihdad for a woman in iddah of final divorce or death, list the prohibited adornments, and identify the dalil that makes it wajib.

Core Idea
Ihdad is wajib: a woman must avoid all adornment during iddah after final divorce or husband's death.

This lesson examines the obligation of ihdad (mourning) for a woman whose marriage has ended permanently, either by a final (ba'in) divorce or by her husband's death. The Hanafi school holds that refraining from adornment, perfume, and beautification is wajib, based on an authentic hadith. The commentary from Kanz al-Daqaiq and al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies the specific prohibitions and the underlying evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The text defines ihdad as leaving adornment and related items. Determine the hukm level: is it wajib, sunnah, or something else? Look for the phrase 'يجب' or the hadith indicating obligation.
2
List the prohibitions
The author explicitly states what must be left: adornment (zinah), perfume, kohl, oil (unless excused), henna, and wearing safflower- or saffron-dyed clothes. Note the exception for oil when there is an excuse.
3
Understand who must observe ihdad
The text specifies that the ruling applies to a Muslim adult woman who is in iddah from a final divorce (ba'in) or widowhood. The author generalizes it to cover all such women.
4
Analyze the dalil
The proof is an authentic hadith: 'It is not permissible for a woman who believes in Allah and the Last Day to mourn for more than three days except for her husband, for four months and ten days.' Consider how this hadith implies an obligation.
The hadith uses 'لا يحل' (not halal), which indicates prohibition, hence the opposite (mourning the husband) becomes wajib.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الإحداد
فيه لغتان أحدت إحداداً فهي محد ومحدة إذا تركت الزينة لموته، وحدت المرأة على زوجها تحد وتحد حداداً بالكسر فهي حاد بغيرها. وأنكر الأصمعي الثلاثي واقتصر على ؛ كذا في المصباح. وفي القاموس والحاد والمحد تاركة الزينة للعدة حدت تحد وتحد الرباعي ؛ هـ. : حداداً وأحدت ا وفي الشريعة ترك الزينة ونحوها من معتدة بطلاق بائن أو موت قوله : (تحد معتدة البت والموت بترك الزينة والطيب والكحل والدهن إلا بعذر والحناء ولبس المزعفر والمعصفر إن كانت مسلمة بالغة أي تحد المبانة والمتوفى عنها زوجها بترك ما ذكر . أطلقه فشمل الطلاق واحدة أو أكثر والفرقة كما في الخانية. وعبر بالإخبار عن فعلها لإفادة أنه واجب عليها للحديث الصحيح لا يحل لامرأة تؤمن بالله واليوم الآخر أن تحد فوق ثلاث إلا على زوج أربعة
والسابعة والثامنة صورة واحدة فالصور ثمانية كما ذكرها في النهر. ثم إن الذي في الفتح في آخر السابعة ثم ارتدت قبل الدخول بدل قوله ثم طلقها قبل الدخول. وقد اقتصر في التتارخانية على تسع مسائل وذكر منها الثامنة المذكورة هنا وذكر بدل السادسة والسابعة المذكورتين هنا ما عبر عنه بقوله : الخامسة تزوج امرأة ودخل بها ثم ارتدت والعياذ بالله تعالى ووقعت الفرقة بينهما ثم أسلمت فتزوجها في العدة ثم ارتدت قبل أن يدخل بها .
فصل في الإحداد
قوله : (قيل أراد بذلك فيما زاد على الثلاث قال في النهر وأقول : وينبغي أن يقيد عدم حل
Chapter on Ihdad. There are two pronunciations: ihdad and ihdad, she is muhidd or muhiddah when she leaves adornment for his death. And a woman ihdada on her husband, she ihdaddu and tahiddu hidada (with kasra), she is haad without ha. Al-Asma'i denied the triliteral and limited to; thus in al-Misbah. And in al-Qamus: al-haad and al-muhidd is one who leaves adornment for iddah. She hadda, tahaddu, tahaddu (quadriliteral), and ihdad. And in Shariah: leaving adornment and the like by a woman in iddah from a final divorce or death. He said: (A woman in iddah of final separation and death performs ihdad by leaving adornment, perfume, kohl, oil except for an excuse, henna, and wearing safflower-dyed and saffron-dyed garments, if she is Muslim and adult) i.e., the woman divorced finally and the one whose husband died performs ihdad by leaving what was mentioned. He made it absolute, so it includes one divorce or more and separation as in al-Khaniyya. And he expressed it as a report of her action to indicate that it is obligatory upon her, due to the authentic hadith: 'It is not permissible for a woman who believes in Allah and the Last Day to mourn for more than three days except for her husband, for four months and ten days.' And the seventh and eighth are one picture, so the pictures are eight as mentioned in al-Nahr. Then what is in Fath al-Qadir at the end of the seventh: 'then she apostatized before consummation' instead of his saying 'then he divorced her before consummation'. And al-Tatarkhaniyya restricted to nine issues, mentioning the eighth mentioned here, and instead of the sixth and seventh mentioned here, he said: 'The fifth: He married a woman and consummated, then she apostatized (God forbid) and separation occurred between them, then she became Muslim, so he married her in the iddah, then she apostatized before he consummated.' Chapter on Ihdad. He said: (It was said he intended by that regarding what exceeds three) He said in al-Nahr: And I say: It should be restricted that it is not permissible...
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This chapter defines ihdad (mourning) as a woman refraining from adornment during her iddah after a final divorce (ba'in) or death of her husband. The ruling is derived from the authentic hadith that prohibits a woman from mourning any deceased person for more than three days except for her husband, for whom she must observe a four-month and ten-day mourning period. The commentary clarifies that this ruling applies to Muslim adult women and includes prohibitions on wearing perfume, kohl, oil, henna, and dyed garments unless there is an excuse. The discussion then extends to various cases, including apostasy, establishing that the eight scenarios mentioned earlier are actually reduced to one picture, and textual variants in Fath al-Qadir and al-Tatarkhaniyya are examined.

Key Terms
إحداد (ihdad): The state of a woman refraining from adornment during iddah after a ba'in divorce or death of husband.
عدة (iddah): Waiting period after dissolution of marriage.
زينة (zinah): Adornment, including perfume, kohl, makeup, and colorful clothes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Consensus of scholars
B) Quranic verse
C) Authentic hadith
D) Rational reasoning

Key Takeaway
Ihdad is a wajib act of worship requiring a woman to abstain from all adornment during her iddah after a final divorce or husband's death, based on the authentic hadith.

The Obligation of Ihdad for the Mu'tidda
Defining ihdad and its obligatory prohibitions with evidence and conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ihdad, identify its prohibitions, and explain the proof from hadith, including the intra-Hanafi dispute on the migrant case.

Core Idea
Ihdad is wajib for a woman in 'iddah of death or irrevocable divorce; mourning for others is prohibited beyond three days.

Ihdad (mourning) is a legal requirement for a woman whose marriage has ended either by death or irrevocable divorce. This lesson unpacks the definition, the prophetic evidence that makes it wajib, and the specific prohibitions involved. You will also encounter a notable intra-Hanafi disagreement regarding a woman migrating from dar al-harb without her husband.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage defines ihdad as abandoning adornment by a woman in the 'iddah of death or irrevocable divorce. The ruling is wajib (obligatory).
The Hanafi school distinguishes between wajib and fard; ihdad is wajib because its proof is zanni al-thubut.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is the hadith: 'It is not permissible for a woman who believes in Allah and the Last Day to mourn for more than three days except for a husband, for four months and ten days.'
A second narration explicitly prohibits dyed clothes, kohl, and perfume, strengthening the obligation.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
In Hanafi usul, a command (amr) indicates obligation (wujub) unless there is a counter-indication. The hadith uses prohibitive language to define ihdad, establishing its obligatory nature.
The author notes that some argued the first version only implies permissibility, but the second removes doubt.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
An intra-Hanafi dispute arises concerning a woman who migrates from dar al-harb without her husband. Abu Hanifa holds she has no 'iddah; the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold she does.
The opposite opinion of the Sahibayn is considered mufta bihi in later fatwa.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Later Hanafi authorities (e.g., in Fath al-Qadir) preferred the view of the Sahibayn due to caution (ihtiyat). The standard ruling on ihdad for death and irrevocable divorce is agreed upon.
This tarjih is based on the principle that 'iddah is a right of Allah not dependent on domicile.

ولو أخر الاستثناء عن الجميع لكان أولى لجواز ما زاد على الثلاث بما إذا لم يرض الزوج بذلك، فإن رضي فقد أسقط حقه منها. أما غير ذات الزوج إذا لم تكن معتدة فينبغي أن يحل لها ذلك بقي هل له منعها في الثلاث؟ Chapter on Ihhad: There are two linguistic forms: 'ahaddat' (to mourn) and 'haddat'. In sharia it is the abandonment of adornment and the like by a woman in the waiting period of an irrevocable divorce or death. He said: 'The mu'tidda of irrevocable divorce and death observes ihdad by abandoning adornment, perfume, kohl, oil except with an excuse, henna, and wearing safflower and saffron-dyed clothes if she is a Muslim and adult.' This includes both one and three divorces, and separation. He expressed it as a report of her action to indicate that it is obligatory upon her, due to the authentic hadith: 'It is not permissible for a woman who believes in Allah and the Last Day to mourn for more than three days except for a husband for four months and ten days.' It was objected that there is no evidence of obligation in this, so the better proof is the other narration: '...except for her husband, she mourns four months and ten days, and does not wear a dyed garment except 'asb cloth, and does not apply kohl or touch perfume.' So the prohibition is explicit in detailing the meaning of abandoning ihdad. There is no disagreement that it is not obligatory for a woman due to someone other than a husband among relatives. Is it permissible? Muhammad said in al-Nawadir: 'Ihdad is not permissible for one whose father, son, brother, or mother dies; it is only for a husband specifically.' It was said: He meant that beyond three days, due to the hadith permitting it for Muslim women for three days for others. In al-Tatarkhaniyya: Abu al-Fadl was asked about a woman whose husband or father or other relatives die, and she dyes her black clothes and wears them for two, three, or four months grieving the deceased; is she excused? He said: No. And Ali ibn Ahmad said: She is not excused and is sinful, except the wife for her husband, she is excused for up to three days. Its apparent meaning is prevention of wearing black out of grief for a husband beyond three days. It is restricted by irrevocable divorce because the revocably divorced woman has no ihdad. It is deduced that if she wants to mourn for a relative for three days and has a husband, he can prevent her, because beautification is his right, even to the extent of hitting her if she refuses and he desires it, and this ihdad is permissible but not obligatory for her, and it wastes his right. In al-Tatarkhaniyya: It is recommended for her to abandon it. Since ihdad is obligatory in death to show grief for the loss of the blessing of marriage, it is also obligatory for the irrevocably divorced woman by analogy, even more so because death is more definitive than separation; hence she washes him dead before separation but not after. He did not specify regarding abandoning perfume, so she should not attend its preparation nor trade in it even if she has no other source of livelihood. Adornment includes combing with a fine-toothed comb, not a wide one, and wearing silk of all types and colors even black, and all types of jewelry of gold, silver, and gems. Al-Tatarkhaniyya adds metallic thread. The exception 'except with an excuse' applies to all, not just oil, so she may wear silk for itching or lice, and she may use kohl for necessity. If the exception were placed after all, it would be better to allow what exceeds three days if the husband does not consent; if he consents, he has waived his right. As for a woman without a husband who is not in 'iddah, it should be permissible for her. The question remains: can he prevent her in the three days? The implication of the hadith is that he does not have that, but it is mentioned in Shafi'i books that he does, and our principles do not reject it. Therefore, the permission in the hadith is interpreted as not preventing him. This last point comes shortly from Fath al-Qadir, and it is apparent because although it is permissible for her, it compromises his right to beautification, so he may prevent her just as he may prevent her from eating something with a foul smell and the like.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion of the text of Kanz al-Daqa'iq on the rulings of ihdad (mourning). The author begins by defining ihdad both linguistically and legally: it is the abandonment of adornment and beautification by a woman observing the 'iddah after an irrevocable divorce (bain) or the death of her husband. The ruling is wajib (obligatory) based on the hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) which prohibits a woman from mourning more than three days except for her husband, for whom she must mourn four months and ten days. The author notes that the first version of the hadith only establishes permissibility (ibaha) by exception, but the second version explicitly prohibits adornment, establishing obligation. The ruling applies to both Muslim and non-Muslim women? Actually, the text restricts it to a Muslim adult woman; for non-Muslim women, there is a later discussion about whether they are addressed with our laws. The author clarifies that ihdad is not obligatory for relatives other than the husband; rather, it is prohibited for them beyond three days, citing Muhammad al-Shaybani's view in al-Nawadir. However, some scholars permit it for three days for non-husband relatives based on the hadith. The text also addresses the case of a revocably divorced woman: she does not observe ihdad because the marriage is not yet fully dissolved. For a woman who wishes to mourn a relative beyond three days while having a husband, the husband may prevent her because beautification is his right. The passage then details what constitutes adornment: perfume, kohl, oil, henna, dyed clothes, silk, jewelry, etc., with exceptions for genuine necessity such as illness or insects. The exception applies to all prohibitions. There is also a discussion about the woman whose husband migrated from dar al-harb; Abu Hanifa holds that she does not have to observe 'iddah because she is not addressed with our laws, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say she must. This is a known intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf. The author also mentions that wearing black clothes out of grief for more than three days is prohibited based on the fatwa of Abu al-Fadl and Ali ibn Ahmad. Finally, the author notes a subtle point: if a woman observes ihdad for a relative for three days, the husband can prevent her because it clashes with his right to intimacy, which requires beautification.

Key Terms
الإحداد (al-ihdad): The legal ruling requiring a woman in 'iddah of death or irrevocable divorce to abstain from adornment, perfume, kohl, oil, henna, and colored clothing.
معتدة البت (mu'tiddat al-batt): A woman in the 'iddah of an irrevocable divorce (talaq bain).
الحناء (al-hinna'): Henna, which is prohibited for the mourning woman to apply.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as explained in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, for whom is ihdad (mourning) wajib?
A) Any woman who has lost a close relative, including father or brother.
B) A woman in the 'iddah of a revocable divorce (raj'i).
C) A woman in the 'iddah of an irrevocable divorce (bain) or death.
D) Only the woman whose husband has died, not divorce.

Key Takeaway
Ihdad is wajib on the mu'tidda of death or irrevocable divorce, based on the Prophet's prohibition of adornment, and the husband's right to intimacy gives him authority over her beautification.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate

The Obligation of Ihdad: Evidence and Scope
Establishes obligation of ihdad for widow and ba'in divorcée

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the hadith evidence for ihdad, explain how qiyas extends it to the ba'in divorcée, and describe the ruling for relatives.

Core Idea
Ihdad is wajib for the widow by hadith and for the ba'in divorcée by a fortiori qiyas; for relatives, it is permissible only for three days.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the author establishes the obligation of ihdad (mourning) for a widow and extends it to the irrevocably divorced woman (ba'in) through qiyas. We will examine the primary proofs and understand the scope of this obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary evidence
The hadith that prohibits adornment beyond three days for anyone except the husband. Note the author's preference for the explicit prohibitory narration over one that only shows permissibility.
2
Analyze the objection
Some argue the hadith only indicates permissibility, not obligation. The author counters that the explicit prohibitions in the other narration establish obligation.
3
Examine the qiyas
The obligation is extended to the ba'in divorcée using qiyas ula (a fortiori): since death is a more complete severance, if mourning is required in death, it is even more fitting in divorce.
4
Determine the ruling for relatives
Ihdad is not obligatory for relatives; it is permissible for up to three days. Note the dissenting view of Imam Muhammad in al-Nawadir that it is not permissible at all beyond three days.
5
Understand the husband's right
The passage notes that a husband may prevent his wife from mourning for relatives during those three days because adornment is his right.

مطيباً كالزيت الخالص منه والشيرج والسمن. وفي المجتبى : ولو اعتادت الدهن فخافت وجعاً فإن كان أمراً ظاهراً يباح لها ا هـ . ويستثنى من المعصفر والمزعفر الخلق الذي لا رائحة له فإنه جائز كما في الهداية. وقيد بإسلامها مع بلوغها لأنه لا حداد على كافرة ولا صغيرة وقدمنا معنى وجوب العدة عليهما. ولم يقيد بالعقل مع أنه لا حداد على مجنونة للاكتفاء بما يخرج الصغيرة لأن عدمه عليها ليس إلا لعدم تكليفها والمجنونة مثلها في ذلك ولهذا قال الأسبيجابي رحمه الله تعالى : الأصل أن كل معتدة مخاطبة فارقت فراش زوج حلال يجب عليها الإحداد وإلا فلا ا هـ . ولم يقيد بالحرية لوجوبه على الأمة المنكوحة لكونها مكلفة بحقوق الشرع ما لم يفت به حق العبد ولهذا لا يحرم عليها الخروج إلا إذا كانت في بيت الزوج وقت الطلاق ولم يخرجها المولى ويحل إن أخرجها والمدبرة والمكاتبة والمستسعاة كالقنة، ولو أسلمت الكافرة في العدة لزمها الإحداد فيما بقي من العدة; كذا في الجوهرة. وينبغي كذلك لو بلغت الصغيرة أو أفاقت المجنونة إذ لا فرق، واقتصاره على ترك ما ذكر يفيد جواز دخول الحمام لها. ونقل في المعراج أن عندهم لها أن تدخل الحمام وتغسل رأسها بالخطمي والسدر، وفيه أن الحداد حق الشرع حتى لو أمرها الزوج بتركه لم يحل لها .
Book of Divorce, Chapter on Ihdad: 'Four months and ten' (1) but it is objected that there is no proof of obligation in it, because its essence is an exception from the negation of permissibility, thus it establishes permissibility and there is no dispute about that. So the better proof is the other narration: 'Except for her husband, for she should observe ihdad for four months and ten days, and she should not wear a dyed garment except a 'asb garment, nor apply kohl, nor touch perfume.' So it explicitly prohibits in detailing the meaning of leaving ihdad. There is no disagreement about it not being obligatory on a woman due to a relative other than the husband. Is it permissible? Muhammad said in al-Nawadir: Ihdad is not permissible for one whose father, son, brother, or mother dies; rather it is only for the husband specifically. It was said: He meant that beyond three days, because of the hadith permitting it for Muslim women for relatives other than their husbands for three days; thus in Fath al-Qadir. In al-Tatarkhaniyyah: Abu al-Fadl was asked about a woman whose husband or father or other relatives die, and she dyes her garment black and wears it for two, three, or four months in grief for the deceased; is she excused? He said: No. And Ali ibn A was asked about her; he said: She is not excused and she is sinful, except a wife for her husband, for she is excused for up to three days, end. Its apparent meaning is prevention from wearing black in grief for the death of her husband beyond three days. It is restricted by 'ba'in' because the revocable divorcée has no ihdad, and it is suggested that if she wants to observe ihdad for a relative for three days while she has a husband, he may prevent her because adornment is his right, to the extent that he may beat her for leaving it if she refuses while he desires it; and this ihdad is permissible for her, not obligatory, and thereby his right is lost; thus in Fath al-Qadir. In al-Tatarkhaniyyah: It is recommended for her to leave it. Since ihdad is obligatory in death to show grief over loss of the blessing of marriage, so it becomes obligatory for the ba'in divorcée by analogy (a fortiori) to the one whose husband has died, because death is more final than separation; for this reason one washes him dead before separation, not after. He also [said] regarding leaving perfume: So she should not attend its preparation nor trade in it, even if she has no livelihood except in it. Included in adornment is combing with a tight-toothed comb, not a wide one, as in al-Mabsut. And it includes wearing silk of all types and colors, even black, and all types of jewelry of gold, silver, and jewels; al-Tatarkhaniyyah added embroidery. His statement 'except for an excuse' is connected to all, not only oil; so she may wear silk for itching or lice, and she may apply kohl for necessity; if the exception were postponed after all, it would be better for permissibility of what is beyond three days when the husband does not consent; but if he consents, he has waived his right. For a woman without a husband who is not in waiting period, it is suggested that it is permissible for her; then, may he prevent her during the three days? The apparent meaning of the hadith is that he cannot, and what is mentioned in Shafi'i books is that he can, and our principles do not reject it; then the permission in the hadith is interpreted as not preventing him, end. This latter comes soon from Fath al-Qadir, and it is apparent because, although it is permissible for her, there is in it a loss of his right to adornment, so he may prevent her just as he may prevent her from eating something with a bad smell and the like. Remaining: (1) Narrated by al-Bukhari... Page 254 Book of Divorce, Chapter on Ihdad: Wearing safflower-dyed and saffron-dyed clothes when she finds nothing else due to the obligation of covering the private parts. Mentioning oil after perfume indicates its prohibition even if not perfumed, like pure sesame oil and clarified butter. In al-Mujtaba: If she is accustomed to oil and fears pain, if it is an apparent thing, it is permissible for her, end. Exception is made from safflower-dyed and saffron-dyed the worn-out garment that has no smell, for it is permissible, as in al-Hidayah. It is restricted by her being Muslim and having reached puberty, because there is no ihdad for a disbeliever nor for a minor, and we have already discussed the meaning of 'iddah being obligatory on them. He did not restrict by sanity, although there is no ihdad for an insane woman, because the exclusion of a minor suffices, since the absence of ihdad on her is only due to her not being obligated, and the insane woman is like her in that; so al-Asbijabi said: The principle is that every woman in 'iddah who is addressed (by law) and has left the lawful bed of her husband must observe ihdad, otherwise not, end. He did not restrict by freedom because it is obligatory on the married slave woman since she is obligated by the rights of the law, unless the right of the master is thereby lost; for this reason, leaving the house is not prohibited for her except if she was in the husband's house at the time of divorce and the master did not take her out, but it is permissible if he takes her out; and the mudabbarah, mukatabah, and mustas'ah are like the female slave. If a disbelieving woman becomes Muslim during the 'iddah, she must observe ihdad for the remainder of the 'iddah; thus in al-Jawhara. And likewise if a minor reaches puberty or an insane woman regains sanity, for there is no difference. His restriction to leaving what was mentioned indicates that entering the bathhouse is permissible for her. It is narrated in al-Mi'raj that according to them she may enter the bathhouse and wash her head with khitmi and sidr; and therein that ihdad is a right of the law, so even if the husband orders her to leave it, it does not become permissible for her.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with the ruling (hukm) of ihdad (mourning period) for women. It is a ruling-with-proof passage where the author discusses the obligation of ihdad and its details.

The passage begins by discussing the evidence for the obligation. The primary proof is the hadith: 'A woman who believes in Allah and the Last Day should not mourn for anyone beyond three days except for her husband, for four months and ten days.' The author notes that there is a debate about whether this hadith indicates obligation (wujub) or permissibility. He argues that the better evidence is the other narration that explicitly prohibits certain actions: not wearing dyed clothes, not applying kohl, not touching perfume, etc. This prohibition indicates that these actions are forbidden during the period of ihdad, thus establishing its obligatory nature.

There is consensus (ijma') that ihdad is not obligatory for relatives other than the husband. However, Imam Muhammad reported in al-Nawadir that it is not permissible to observe ihdad for relatives at all, meaning beyond three days. The hadith permits Muslim women to mourn for anyone for three days, so beyond three days is only for the husband. Thus, a woman may observe three days of ihdad for any relative.

The author then discusses the case of a woman wearing black clothes in grief for her husband beyond three days. The apparent ruling is that she is sinful for doing so, except a wife for her husband who is excused for up to three days. This is because excessive grieving beyond the prescribed limit is not allowed.

Next, the passage addresses the ba'in (irrevocably divorced) woman. The author uses qiyas ula (a fortiori analogy) to extend the obligation of ihdad from the widow (whose husband died) to the ba'in divorcée. The reasoning is that death is a more complete severance than divorce, so if ihdad is obligatory in death, it is even more so in divorce. This is because in death, the woman even washes the deceased's body, which is not done after divorce.

The passage then details the prohibitions during ihdad: perfume (attending its preparation or trading in it), adornment items such as tight-toothed combing, silk clothing, jewelry, etc. Exceptions are made for necessity: wearing silk if needed for itching or lice, applying kohl if necessary, and wearing safflower-dyed clothes if nothing else is available to cover the 'awrah. Also, if the husband consents to her wearing certain things beyond three days, he may waive his right.

A key discussion is whether a husband can prevent his wife from observing ihdad for a relative during the three-day period. The Hanafi position is that he can prevent her because her adornment is his right. Even though the hadith permits her to mourn for three days, it doesn't mean he cannot stop her. The Shafi'i view is that he cannot prevent her, but Hanafi principles do not reject the idea of prevention.

The author then lists conditions for the woman: she must be Muslim and have reached puberty. There is no ihdad for a disbeliever or a minor. It is not restricted by sanity because the same principle applies: an insane woman is not obligated just like a minor. Also, a female slave (umm walad, mudabbarah, mukatabah, etc.) must observe ihdad unless it conflicts with her master's right. If a disbelieving woman converts during the 'iddah, she must observe ihdad from that point onward.

Finally, the passage notes that entering the bathhouse is permissible, and washing the head with certain plants is allowed. The ihdad is a legal right (haqq al-shar') that cannot be overridden even by the husband's order.

Key Terms
الإحداد (al-ihdad): Abstention from adornment and perfume during the 'iddah period due to the death of a husband or irrevocable divorce, as a sign of mourning.
المبتوتة (al-mabtutah): A woman who has been given an irrevocable divorce (ba'in talaq), after which she must observe 'iddah and ihdad according to Hanafis.
العدة (al-'iddah): Waiting period after divorce or death before a woman may remarry.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the evidence that ihdad is wajib for a widow?
A) Consensus of scholars
B) The hadith that explicitly prohibits adornment during mourning period
C) Qiyas with divorce
D) The Quranic verse

Key Takeaway
Ihdad is wajib for the widow by explicit hadith and for the ba'in divorcée by qiyas ula; for other relatives it is permissible only for three days, and the husband may prevent it.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
10 min
Prohibitions and Conditions of Ihdad
Details prohibitions and conditions of ihdad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the acts prohibited during ihdad, identify exceptions for necessity, and state the conditions for the obligation.

Core Idea
During ihdad, a woman must avoid perfume, dyed clothes, kohl, silk, jewelry, and even combing with a tight-toothed comb, except for illnesses or when the husband consents.

After establishing the obligation, the passage details what a woman must abstain from during ihdad and who must observe it. It also revisits the issue of the husband's permission to mourn for relatives, highlighting a difference with the Shafi'i school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited acts
The passage lists: perfume (including trade), dyed clothes (except 'asb), kohl, silk, jewelry, and tight-toothed combing.
2
Note the exceptions
These prohibitions are lifted for necessity (e.g., silk for itching, kohl for eye treatment). Also, worn-out safflower/saffron clothes without smell are allowed.
3
Understand the husband's consent
If the husband allows, she may wear things that would otherwise be impermissible beyond three days. His consent waives his right.
4
Examine the Shafi'i difference
The passage notes that according to Shafi'is, the husband cannot prevent three-day mourning; Hanafis allow it because his right over adornment is superior.
5
List the conditions for obligation
The woman must be Muslim, adult (baligha), and in valid 'iddah. No ihdad for a disbeliever, minor, insane, or if it conflicts with a slave woman's master's right.

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with the ruling (hukm) of ihdad (mourning period) for women. It is a ruling-with-proof passage where the author discusses the obligation of ihdad and its details.

The passage begins by discussing the evidence for the obligation. The primary proof is the hadith: 'A woman who believes in Allah and the Last Day should not mourn for anyone beyond three days except for her husband, for four months and ten days.' The author notes that there is a debate about whether this hadith indicates obligation (wujub) or permissibility. He argues that the better evidence is the other narration that explicitly prohibits certain actions: not wearing dyed clothes, not applying kohl, not touching perfume, etc. This prohibition indicates that these actions are forbidden during the period of ihdad, thus establishing its obligatory nature.

There is consensus (ijma') that ihdad is not obligatory for relatives other than the husband. However, Imam Muhammad reported in al-Nawadir that it is not permissible to observe ihdad for relatives at all, meaning beyond three days. The hadith permits Muslim women to mourn for anyone for three days, so beyond three days is only for the husband. Thus, a woman may observe three days of ihdad for any relative.

The author then discusses the case of a woman wearing black clothes in grief for her husband beyond three days. The apparent ruling is that she is sinful for doing so, except a wife for her husband who is excused for up to three days. This is because excessive grieving beyond the prescribed limit is not allowed.

Next, the passage addresses the ba'in (irrevocably divorced) woman. The author uses qiyas ula (a fortiori analogy) to extend the obligation of ihdad from the widow (whose husband died) to the ba'in divorcée. The reasoning is that death is a more complete severance than divorce, so if ihdad is obligatory in death, it is even more so in divorce. This is because in death, the woman even washes the deceased's body, which is not done after divorce.

The passage then details the prohibitions during ihdad: perfume (attending its preparation or trading in it), adornment items such as tight-toothed combing, silk clothing, jewelry, etc. Exceptions are made for necessity: wearing silk if needed for itching or lice, applying kohl if necessary, and wearing safflower-dyed clothes if nothing else is available to cover the 'awrah. Also, if the husband consents to her wearing certain things beyond three days, he may waive his right.

A key discussion is whether a husband can prevent his wife from observing ihdad for a relative during the three-day period. The Hanafi position is that he can prevent her because her adornment is his right. Even though the hadith permits her to mourn for three days, it doesn't mean he cannot stop her. The Shafi'i view is that he cannot prevent her, but Hanafi principles do not reject the idea of prevention.

The author then lists conditions for the woman: she must be Muslim and have reached puberty. There is no ihdad for a disbeliever or a minor. It is not restricted by sanity because the same principle applies: an insane woman is not obligated just like a minor. Also, a female slave (umm walad, mudabbarah, mukatabah, etc.) must observe ihdad unless it conflicts with her master's right. If a disbelieving woman converts during the 'iddah, she must observe ihdad from that point onward.

Finally, the passage notes that entering the bathhouse is permissible, and washing the head with certain plants is allowed. The ihdad is a legal right (haqq al-shar') that cannot be overridden even by the husband's order.

Key Terms
الإحداد (al-ihdad): Abstention from adornment and perfume during the 'iddah period due to the death of a husband or irrevocable divorce, as a sign of mourning.
المبتوتة (al-mabtutah): A woman who has been given an irrevocable divorce (ba'in talaq), after which she must observe 'iddah and ihdad according to Hanafis.
العدة (al-'iddah): Waiting period after divorce or death before a woman may remarry.
الحرير (al-harir): Silk, prohibited for women in ihdad unless for necessity like itching.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a condition for ihdad to be obligatory?
A) The woman must be a free person
B) The woman must be Muslim and adult
C) The woman must have children
D) The woman must be a virgin

Key Takeaway
The prohibitions during ihdad (perfume, dye, silk, kohl, jewelry) are subject to exceptions for necessity; the obligation requires the woman to be Muslim, adult, and in valid 'iddah, with nuances for slaves and converts.

Women Exempt from Ihdad: Seven Categories
Lists seven types of women exempt from ihdad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the seven categories of women exempt from ihdad and explain the rationale for each exemption.

Core Idea
Seven types of women are exempt from ihdad because they lack the cause: a valid marriage blessing to grieve over.

Ihdad is the period of mourning during 'iddah, but not all women in 'iddah are required to observe it. The Hanafi school identifies seven categories exempt from this obligation. In this lesson, we examine the list and the legal reasoning behind each exemption.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the seven categories
Learn the seven exempt women: disbeliever, minor, insane, freed slave, invalid marriage (fasid from inception), intercourse under shubha, and revocable divorce.
Use the mnemonic 'Kafir, Saghir, Majnun, 'Atiqa, Fasid, Shubha, Raj'i'.
2
Understand the cause of exemption
For each category, determine whether the exemption is due to lack of obligation (minor, insane, disbeliever) or absence of a valid marriage blessing (invalid marriage, shubha).
The cause of ihdad is to show grief over losing a valid marriage blessing (ni'mah al-nikah).
3
Distinguish between types of invalid marriage
Differentiate between a marriage invalid from inception (fasid aslan) and one that becomes invalid later (fasid after being sahih). Only the former exempts; the latter requires ihdad.
Example from al-Khaniyyah: a man marries his own slave after having a child with her.
4
Analyze the objection and response
Review why the invalid marriage and revocable divorce are exempt despite apparent grief. The response points out that there is no blessing to grieve in an invalid marriage, and for revocable divorce, ihdad would discourage reconciliation.
This demonstrates the hikmah (wisdom) behind the rulings.
5
Apply the ruling to a new scenario
Given a scenario, identify which category applies and state whether ihdad is required.
Practice with examples like a woman who discovers her marriage was invalid due to fosterage.

قوله: (لا معتدة العتق والنكاح الفاسد أي لا حداد على أم الولد إذا أعتقت بإعتاق سيدها أو موته، ولا على المعتدة من نكاح فاسد وهو مفهوم من اقتصاره على البت والموت. وفي الخانية : لو تزوج أمة وملكها بعد الدخول وقد ولدت منه فسد النكاح بينهما ولا حداد عليها، ولا يجوز لغيره أن يتزوجها حتى تحيض حيضتين، فإن أعتقها كان عليها عدتان : عدة فساد النكاح وفيها الحداد وعدة العتق ولا حداد فيها فتحد في حيضتين دون الثالثة، ولو أعتقها بعد حيضتين كان عليها أن تعتد بثلاث ا.هـ. وبهذا ظهر أن النكاح إذا فسد بعد صحته يوجب الحداد بخلاف ما إذا كان فاسداً من أصله لأنه إنما وجب اظهاراً للتأسف على فوات نعمة النكاح وسببه النكاح الصحيح فلا يتأسف على الفاسد، واستفيد عدم وجوبه على المعتدة من وطء شبهة بالأولى كما في المعراج فالحاصل لا إحداد على كافرة ولا صغيرة ولا مجنونة ولا معتدة عن عتق ولا معتدة عن نكاح فاسد ولا على معتدة عن وطء شبهة ولا معتدة عن طلاق رجعي، فهن سبع لا حداد عليهن. فإن قلت: إن العلة لوجوده أعني إظهار التأسف موجودة في الفاسد لأنها تتأسف على فراق الزوج وأيضاً في الرجعي لوجود النكاح إلى آخر العدة قلت: لا نسلم أنها تتأسف على الفاسد لأنه لا نعمة له فيتأسف عليها بل أمر يريد الخلاص منه، وأما الرجعي فالزوج موجود ويمكنه الرجعة وهي تشتاق إليه فتتزين كي يراجعها ففي التكليف بالحداد تنفير له عن المراجعة ولأنها لو تبرجت كان أدعى إلى رغبته عند النظر إليها. قوله: (وجوب سترة العورة ينبغي أي يقيد بقدر ما تستحدث ثوباً غيره إما ببيعه والاستخلاف بثمنه أو من مالها إن كان لها مال؛ كذا في النهر عن الفتح قوله : (ونقل في
His statement: (Not the woman in 'iddah from freedom or from an invalid marriage) meaning there is no ihdad for a slave woman (umm walad) when she is freed by her master's manumission or death, nor for the woman in 'iddah from an invalid marriage; this is understood from his restriction to the irrevocable divorce and death. In al-Khaniyyah: If a man marries a slave woman and then comes to own her after consummation and she has borne him a child, the marriage becomes invalid between them and she has no ihdad; and it is not permissible for another man to marry her until she completes two menstrual periods. Then if he frees her, she has two 'iddahs: the 'iddah of the invalid marriage, in which there is ihdad, and the 'iddah of freedom, in which there is no ihdad; so she observes ihdad in two periods but not the third. If he freed her after two periods, she must observe three 'iddahs. End. From this it appears that if marriage becomes invalid after its validity, it requires ihdad, unlike when it was invalid from the beginning, because ihdad is prescribed to show grief over loss of the blessing of marriage, and its cause is a valid marriage, so one does not grieve over an invalid one. It is also understood that ihdad is not required for a woman in 'iddah due to intercourse under a mistaken belief (shubha), a fortiori, as in al-Mi'raj. The conclusion: There is no ihdad for a disbeliever, a minor, an insane woman, a woman in 'iddah due to freedom, a woman in 'iddah due to an invalid marriage, a woman in 'iddah due to intercourse under shubha, and a woman in 'iddah from a revocable divorce. So these are seven on whom there is no ihdad. If you say: 'The cause for its obligation, namely showing grief, is present in the invalid marriage because she grieves over separation from her husband, and also in the revocable divorce because the marriage exists until the end of the 'iddah,' I reply: We do not accept that she grieves over the invalid marriage because there is no blessing in it to grieve over; rather it is something she wants to be rid of. As for the revocable divorce, the husband is present and he can take her back, and she longs for him, so she wants to adorn herself so that he will take her back; requiring ihdad would dissuade him from taking her back, and if she displays herself, it encourages his desire when looking at her.'
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement that identifies seven categories of women upon whom ihdad (mourning period) is not obligatory. The author comments on the text of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, which says 'not the woman in 'iddah from freedom or from an invalid marriage.' The commentary explains that this means a slave woman who becomes free (umm walad) does not observe ihdad upon freedom, nor does a woman in 'iddah from a marriage that was invalid from its inception. However, if a marriage was initially valid but later became invalid (e.g., a man marries his own slave), then ihdad is required because the marriage was valid and thus there is a blessing to grieve over.

A detailed case from al-Khaniyyah illustrates: A man marries a slave woman, then after having a child, he comes to own her. The marriage becomes invalid, and she must observe an 'iddah of two menstrual periods without ihdad. If he later frees her, she has two separate 'iddahs: the first for the invalid marriage (with ihdad) and the second for freedom (without ihdad). The ruling is practical: she combines the ihdad from the first 'iddah into the two periods, but not the third.

The passage then enumerates seven categories: 1) a disbeliever (during her 'iddah from a non-Muslim husband), 2) a minor (below puberty), 3) an insane woman, 4) a woman observing 'iddah due to being freed (from slavery), 5) a woman observing 'iddah from an invalid marriage (fasid), 6) a woman observing 'iddah due to intercourse under a mistaken belief (shubha), and 7) a woman observing 'iddah from a revocable divorce (talaq raj'i).

The text then addresses a potential objection: why is the invalid marriage not included, since she might still grieve? The answer is that there is no blessing (ni'mah) in an invalid marriage to grieve over. For a revocable divorce, the husband may take her back, so if she were required to avoid adornment, it would discourage him from reconciliation. Thus, the obligation of ihdad would be counterproductive.

The final part of the passage (cut off) relates to an exception regarding covering the 'awrah during ihdad, suggesting that if a woman has no other garment, she may wear something even if dyed, to cover herself.

Key Terms
الإحداد (al-ihdad): Abstention from adornment and perfume during the 'iddah period; not required for these seven women.
العدة (al-'iddah): Waiting period after termination of marriage or upon certain events like manumission.
النكاح الفاسد (al-nikah al-fasid): An invalid marriage that lacks a condition of validity from its inception, such as marrying within prohibited degrees without knowledge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A woman in 'iddah from a revocable divorce
B) A woman in 'iddah from an irrevocable divorce (ba'in)
C) A minor girl
D) A woman in 'iddah due to being freed

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school exempts seven categories from ihdad, based on the absence of a valid marriage blessing or lack of legal capacity.

Ihdad for the Woman in 'Iddah of Death
Explains obligatory mourning and prohibited adornments for woman in iddah of death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of ihdad for a woman in iddah of death and identify the seven categories of women exempt from ihdad.

Core Idea
Ihdad is wajib for a Muslim adult woman whose husband dies; exceptions include minors, non-Muslims, and those in iddah from invalid marriage.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the obligations and prohibitions of ihdad (mourning) for a woman in the iddah of death. The author details forbidden adornments, conditions for obligation, and seven categories of women who are exempt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Ihdad is an obligation (wajib) for a woman in iddah from the death of her husband. It involves refraining from adornment.
The ruling is derived from the Sunnah and is a right of Shari'a.
2
List the prohibited actions
The woman must avoid wearing safflower- or saffron-dyed clothes (unless necessary for covering), and using oil even if unperfumed (except for health reasons).
Old clothes with no scent are permitted.
3
Identify the conditions of obligation
Ihdad is obligatory upon a Muslim, adult, sane woman who was in a valid marriage and whose husband died. It is not required for minors, disbelievers, or the insane.
Slave women are also obligated as long as it does not conflict with the master's rights.
4
List the seven exempt categories
The author enumerates seven women who do not observe ihdad: disbeliever, minor, insane, woman in iddah from emancipation, invalid marriage (from origin), doubtful intercourse, and revocable divorce.
These are all cases where the wisdom of mourning does not apply.
5
Understand the legal cause and wisdom
The 'illa (cause) is the loss of a valid marriage; the wisdom is to display regret. The cause is pervasive, while the wisdom is not always present.
This explains why ihdad is not required in cases like invalid marriage from the start.

The wearing of safflower and saffron-dyed clothes if she does not find other clothes due to the obligation of covering the private parts. And he did not restrict it by intellect, although there is no ihdad for an insane woman, because he sufficed with what excludes the minor, since its absence regarding her is only due to lack of obligation, and the insane woman is like her in that regard. For this reason al-Asbijabi said: The principle is that every addressed woman in iddah who has separated from the bed of a lawful husband must observe ihdad, otherwise not. And he did not restrict it by freedom because it is obligatory upon a married slave woman as she is obligated by the rights of Shari'a, as long as the right of the master is not violated. For this reason, leaving the house is not prohibited for her unless she was in the husband's house at the time of divorce and the master did not take her out; it is permissible if he takes her out. His statement: (There is no ihdad for the woman in iddah of emancipation or invalid marriage) meaning there is no ihdad for umm walad if she is emancipated by her master's act or death, nor for a woman in iddah from an invalid marriage, and this is understood from his confining it to irrevocable and death. And in al-Khaniyya: If a man marries a slave woman and then owns her after consummation and she gives birth from him, the marriage becomes invalid between them and there is no ihdad on her. It is not permissible for another to marry her until she has two menses. If he emancipates her, she must observe two iddas: the iddah of invalid marriage in which there is ihdad, and the iddah of emancipation in which there is no ihdad, so she observes ihdad in two menses but not the third. If he emancipates her after two menses, she must observe three. And from this it appears that if a marriage becomes invalid after its validity, it requires ihdad, unlike if it was invalid from its origin. Because it was only made obligatory as a display of regret for the loss of the blessing of marriage, and its cause is a valid marriage, so one does not regret the invalid. And the non-obligation upon the woman in iddah from doubtful intercourse is deduced by priority, as in al-Mi'raj. So the conclusion is: there is no ihdad for a disbeliever, nor a minor, nor an insane woman, nor a woman in iddah from emancipation, nor a woman in iddah from invalid marriage, nor a woman in iddah from doubtful intercourse, nor a woman in iddah from revocable divorce. These are seven women on whom there is no ihdad. If you say: The cause for its obligation is the display of regret for the loss of the blessing of marriage. And if this is absent in the two issues of the book, there remains another (cause), namely the non-display of desire for what is prohibited, and these things are for desire. The answer is that this is wisdom and is not pervasive, while that is a cause that ceases with its cessation, as in al-Mi'raj.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling on ihdad (mourning period) for a woman in iddah of the death of her husband. The author explains the prohibitions: wearing safflower or saffron-dyed clothes unless necessary for covering oneself (and even then only if no other clothes exist), and using oil even if not perfumed (like pure sesame oil) is prohibited because it is a form of adornment. However, if a woman is accustomed to oil and fears pain from not using it, it may be permissible if the need is apparent. An exception is old clothes with no scent. The obligation of ihdad is for Muslim adult women, not for minors, disbelievers, or insane women. The passage also clarifies that ihdad is required even for slave women as long as it does not conflict with the master's rights, and that a woman who converts to Islam during iddah must observe ihdad for the remaining period. The commentary then discusses the case of invalid marriage: no ihdad if the marriage was invalid from the start, but if it becomes invalid after being valid, ihdad is required. The wisdom behind ihdad is to display regret for the loss of the marriage blessing, but this wisdom does not apply in certain cases. The author concludes by listing seven categories of women who do not observe ihdad.

Key Terms
إحداد (ihdad): Mourning period for a woman whose husband died, during which she avoids adornment.
معتدة (mu'taddah): A woman in her waiting period (iddah) after divorce or death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Which of the following women does NOT have to observe ihdad?
A) A Muslim adult woman whose husband died
B) A woman in iddah from an invalid marriage from its origin
C) A woman in iddah from a revocable divorce
D) A slave woman whose husband died

Key Takeaway
Ihdad is a right of Shari'a; it is wajib for the woman in iddah of death, but not for those in divorce or invalid marriage.

Prohibition of Explicit Proposals to a Woman in 'Iddah
Explains prohibition of explicit and implicit proposals to a woman in iddah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on proposing to a woman in iddah, distinguish between explicit (tasrih) and implicit (ta'rid) proposals, and cite the primary evidence from Quran and hadith.

Core Idea
Explicit proposals to women in iddah are haram; implicit proposals are permissible.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on marriage proposals (khutbah) to a woman during her iddah (waiting period). The passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that while explicit proposals are prohibited, implicit expressions of interest are allowed. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correct fiqh application in marriage practices.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine the act being discussed: proposing to a woman in iddah. State the ruling: explicit proposal is haram; implicit proposal is permissible.
The ruling applies to all types of iddah (divorce, death, etc.).
2
Locate the primary evidence
Find the dalil: Quran 2:235 (prohibition of resolving on marriage until term ends) and hadith (prohibiting proposing over a brother's proposal).
The hadith is applied by analogy to a woman in iddah.
3
Understand the distinction between explicit and implicit
Explicit (tasrih) is a direct request; implicit (ta'rid) is an indirect hint. The Hanafi school allows ta'rid but forbids tasrih.
Examples of ta'rid: saying 'I wish to marry someone like you'.
4
Note the scope and exceptions
The prohibition covers all women in iddah. For a woman not in iddah, both are allowed unless another man has already proposed and she consented.
If she remains silent after a proposal, there are two opinions.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Given a case, determine if a proposal is explicit or implicit and state the hukm.
Use the criteria: direct vs. indirect wording.

تختطب معتدة وصح التعريض ولا تخرج معتدة الطلاق من بيتها ومعتدة الموت تخرج على فوات نعمة النكاح وإن فاتت في مسألتي الكتاب بقيت أخرى أعني عدم اظهار الرغبة فيما هو ممنوع فيها وهذه الأشياء للرغبة . أجيب بأن هذه حكمة فلا تطرد وتلك علة يزول الحكم بزوالها كما في المعراج قوله: (ولا) تخطب معتدة أي تحرم خطبتها وهي بكسر الخاء مصدر بمنزلة الخطب مثل قولك إنه لحسن القعدة والجلسة تريد القعود والجلوس. وفي اشتقاقه وجهان: الأول أن الخطب هو الأمر والشأن يقال ما خطبك أي ما شأنك فقولهم خطب فلان فلانة أي سألها أمراً وشأناً في نفسها. والثاني أن أصل الخطبة من الخطاب الذي هو الكلام ؛ يقال خطب المرأة خطبة لأنه خاطب في عقد النكاح وخطب خطبة أي خاطب بالزجر والوعظ والخطب الأمر العظيم لأنه يحتاج فيه إلى خطاب كثير؛ كذا ذكر الإمام الرازي. أطلقها فشمل المعتدة عن طلاق بنوعية وعن وفاة وعن عتق وعن غير ذلك ولم أره صريحاً، وعلم منه حرمة خطبة المنكوحة بالأولى وتحرم تصريحاً وتعريضاً كما في البدائع . وقيد بالمعتدة لأن الخالية عن نكاح وعدة تحل خطبتها تصريحاً وتعريضاً لجواز نكاحها لكن بشرط أن لا يخطبها غيره قبله فإن خطبها فعلى ثلاثة أوجه : إما أن تصرح بالرضا فتحرم، أو بالرد فتحل، أو تسكت فقولان للعلماء ولم أر هذا التفصيل لأصحابنا وأصله الحديث الصحيح لا يخطب أحدكم على خطبة أخيه» (۱) وقيدوه بأن لا يأذن له. واستفيد من حرمة خطبة المعتدة حرمة نكاحها على غير المطلق بالأولى وهو ظاهر ولكن جعلوا دليله قوله تعالى ولا تعزموا عقدة النكاح حتى يبلغ الكتاب أجله ﴾ [البقرة : ٢٣٥] ووجهه أن المراد لا تعقدوا، وعبر عنه بالعزم لأنه سببه مبالغة في المنع عنه، وقيل هو باق على حقيقته والمراد به الإيجاب؛ يقال عزمت عليك أي أوجبت عليك والإيجاب سبب للوجود ظاهراً فكان مجازاً ويحتمل
A woman in iddah may be proposed to implicitly, and it is correct. And a woman in iddah of divorce does not leave her house, while a woman in iddah of death leaves. On the loss of the blessing of marriage, and if this is absent in the two issues of the book, there remains another (cause), namely the non-display of desire for what is prohibited, and these things are for desire. The answer is that this is wisdom and is not pervasive, while that is a cause that ceases with its cessation, as in al-Mi'raj. His statement: (And a woman in iddah is not to be proposed to) meaning her proposal is prohibited, and it (khatba) with kasr of kha is a verbal noun like 'khutb', like when you say he is good in sitting and sitting, meaning the act of sitting. And there are two aspects of its derivation: first, that 'al-khatb' is the matter or affair; it is said 'ma khatbuka' (what is your matter), so they say 'khataba fulaan fulanatan' meaning he asked her for a matter concerning herself. Second, that the origin of 'khutba' is from 'khitab' which is speech; it is said 'khataba al-mar'ata khutbatan' because he addresses her in the marriage contract, and 'khataba khutbatan' meaning he addressed with exhortation and preaching, and 'al-khatb' (the great matter) because it needs much speech; thus stated Imam al-Razi. He made it general so it includes the woman in iddah from divorce of both types, from death, from emancipation, and from others, and I have not seen it explicitly. And from this the prohibition of proposing to a married woman is understood by priority, and it is prohibited explicitly and implicitly as in al-Bada'i. And he restricted it to the woman in iddah because a woman without a husband and iddah may be proposed to explicitly and implicitly due to the permissibility of marrying her, but with the condition that no one else has proposed to her before. If he proposes, then there are three aspects: either she expresses consent, so it is prohibited; or she refuses, so it is permissible; or she remains silent, then there are two opinions among the scholars, and I have not seen this detail from our companions. Its origin is the authentic hadith: 'None of you should propose to a woman who has already been proposed to by his brother' and they restricted it by his not having given permission. And from the prohibition of proposing to a woman in iddah, the prohibition of marrying her to someone other than the one who divorced is understood by priority, and it is apparent, but they made its evidence the saying of Allah: 'And do not resolve on the knot of marriage until the book reaches its term' (al-Baqarah 2:235), and the explanation is that what is meant is 'do not contract', and it is expressed as 'resolve' because it is its cause as an exaggeration in prohibition; and it is said that it remains in its literal meaning and what is meant is obligation; one says 'azamtu alayka' meaning I made it obligatory upon you, and obligation is a cause for existence apparently, so it is metaphoric and possible.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage discusses the prohibition of proposing to a woman who is in her iddah (waiting period). The author first notes that a woman in iddah may be proposed to implicitly ('takhrib') but not explicitly. The ruling is that proposing to a woman in iddah is haram (prohibited). The term 'khatba' is defined linguistically. The prohibition applies to all types of iddah (divorce, death, emancipation, etc.). This includes both explicit and implicit proposals. The author contrasts with a woman not in iddah, who may be proposed to. The discussion also covers the case where a woman is already proposed to by another: if she consents, the second proposal is haram; if she refuses, it is permissible; if she remains silent, there are two opinions. The primary evidence is the hadith 'None of you should propose to a woman who has already been proposed to by his brother' and the Quranic verse prohibiting resolving on marriage until the iddah expires.

Key Terms
خطبة (khutbah): Marriage proposal, from the root 'khataba' meaning to address or to ask in marriage.
تعريض (ta'rid): Implicit or indirect expression of intent to marry.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Permissible (mubah)
B) Recommended (mustahabb)
C) Prohibited (haram)
D) Disliked (makruh)

Key Takeaway
Explicit proposals to women in iddah are prohibited by Quran and hadith; implicit proposals are permissible.

Prohibition of Proposing to a Woman in 'Iddah and Permissibility of Ta'rid for Widows
Explaining prohibition of khitbah and permissibility of ta'rid only for widows

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on proposing to women in 'iddah and distinguish between direct (khitbah) and indirect (ta'rid) proposal regarding permissibility.

Core Idea
Direct proposal to any woman in 'iddah is haram; indirect proposal is only allowed for widows.

This lesson from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the prohibition of khitbah (direct marriage proposal) to a woman in 'iddah and the exceptional permissibility of ta'rid (indirect proposal) only for widows. We will examine the textual proofs from the Quran and Sunnah and the rationale behind the distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what the act of 'khitbah' is and its ruling for a mu'taddah. The text states 'tukhtabu mu'taddah' meaning she is not to be proposed to directly.
Khitbah is the direct request for marriage.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the proofs cited: the hadith 'لا يخطب أحدكم على خطبة أخيه' and the Quranic verse 'ولا تعزموا عقدة النكاح حتى يبلغ الكتاب أجله'.
Both are from authentic sources.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the hadith indicates prohibition. The imperative 'لا يخطب' (do not propose) is a nahy that implies haram according to Hanafi usul.
The nahy is understood as a command to refrain.
4
Distinguish ta'rid from khitbah
Define ta'rid as indirect proposal and note its permissibility only for widows, not divorced women.
The Quranic verse about ta'rid is applied specifically to widows.
5
Note the consensus on ta'rid
The text cites al-Mi'raj stating that ta'rid is allowed only for widows by ijmā'. For divorced women, it is haram due to potential enmity.
This is a key distinguishing factor.

وهو

Book of Talaq, Section on Ihdad: A woman in iddah is proposed to, and indirect proposal is valid, and a divorced woman in iddah does not leave her house, and a widow leaves. ... He said: (And not) a woman in iddah is proposed to, meaning her proposal is prohibited...
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, discussing the rulings of 'iddah. The author first quotes the matn: 'A woman in iddah is proposed to' meaning she should not be proposed to directly. The commentary clarifies that proposing (khitbah) to a woman in 'iddah is haram. This includes all types of 'iddah: divorce, death, manumission, etc. The prohibition is established by the hadith 'No one should propose over the proposal of his brother' and the Quranic verse 'Do not resolve the marriage contract until the appointed term reaches its end.' The text then discusses ta'rid (indirect proposal), which is linguistically distinct from kinayah. Ta'rid is permissible only for a widow whose husband has died, because there is no enmity between the deceased and the suitor. For a divorced woman, ta'rid is not allowed as it may cause enmity. The Quranic verse 'There is no sin upon you concerning that which you offer to women of indirect proposal' is specifically applied to widows. The passage also includes a note about combing hair during 'iddah, where Shafi'i, Maliki, and Ahmad permit it absolutely, while the Hanafi position is silent due to lack of text, but the commentator implies it is not allowed.

Key Terms
خطبة (khitbah): Proposing marriage verbally to a woman directly or indirectly.
تعريض (ta'rid): Indirect proposal using ambiguous speech that hints at marriage without explicit mention.
معتدة (mu'taddah): A woman observing the waiting period ('iddah) after divorce or death of husband.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, to which type of mu'taddah is ta'rid (indirect proposal) permissible?
A) A divorced woman in 'iddah of a revocable divorce.
B) A divorced woman in 'iddah of an irrevocable divorce.
C) A widow in 'iddah of death.
D) All of the above.

Key Takeaway
Direct proposal to any woman in 'iddah is haram; indirect proposal (ta'rid) is only allowed for widows, not for divorced women.

Talaq — Divorce

Prohibition of a Divorced Woman Leaving Her Home During 'Iddah
States the ruling with Quranic proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a divorced woman leaving her house during iddah, identify the Quranic proof, and explain the exception for necessity.

Core Idea
A divorced woman in iddah may not leave her home; exit is haram except for a clear necessity.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq expounds the ruling that a woman observing iddah after divorce must remain in her home. The proof is the Quranic command in Surah At-Talaq (65:1). The commentator clarifies that the prohibition applies to both husband and wife, and that necessity permits exit. This lesson examines the ruling, its evidence, and its limits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is a divorced woman leaving her home during iddah. The ruling is haram, as stated explicitly in the text.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the Quranic verse from Surah At-Talaq (65:1): 'Do not expel them from their houses nor let them leave unless they commit a clear immorality.'
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The prohibitory forms 'la tukhrijuhunna' and 'la yakhrujna' indicate haram in Hanafi usul. The exception ('illa an ya'tina bifahishah') shows that exit is only permissible in extreme cases.
4
Note the exception
The verse mentions 'fahishah mubayyinah' (clear immorality). The commentator expands this to include other necessities, such as buying food or medical treatment. Without necessity, exit remains haram day or night.
5
Summarize the complete ruling
A divorced woman must remain in her iddah residence. Her husband may not expel her, and she may not leave. If the house is borrowed, the husband must provide another. Leaving without a valid necessity is haram.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولا) تخرج معتدة (الطلاق لقوله تعالى لا تخرجوهن من بيوتهن ولا يخرجن إلا أن يأتين بفاحشة مبينة [الطلاق: ١] أي لا تخرجوا المعتدات من المساكن التي كنتم تسكنون فيها قبل الطلاق فإن كانت المساكن عارية فارتجعت من الساكن كان على الأزواج أن يعينوا مساكن أخرى بطريق الشراء أو الكراء، وعلى الزوجات أيضاً أن لا يخرجن حقاً الله تعالى إلا لضرورة ظاهرة فإن خرجن ليلاً أو نهاراً كان حراماً. وقال ابن عباس رضي الله عنهما :
قوله : (وما قيل أن منه الخ قال في النهر : فيه نظر فقد أخرج البيهقي عن ابن جبير في قوله تعالى إلا أن تقولوا قولاً معروفاً) [البقرة: ٢٣٥] يقول إني فيك لراغب وإني لأرجو أن نجتمع. قال في الفتح : ونحوه إنك لجميلة أو صالحة فلا يصرح بنكاحها ولم يعول على ما في البدائع قوله :
He said: (And not) a divorced mu'taddah leaves (her home) due to the word of Allah Most High: 'Do not expel them from their houses nor let them leave unless they commit a clear immorality.' [At-Talaq:1] Meaning do not expel the mu'taddaat from the dwellings they inhabited before divorce...
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, now addressing the ruling that a divorced woman in 'iddah may not leave her house. The proof is the Quranic verse from Surah At-Talaq: 'Do not expel them from their houses nor let them leave unless they commit a clear immorality.' The commentator explains that this applies to both the husband (who must not expel her) and the wife (who must not leave). If the house was borrowed, the husband must provide another. Leaving without necessity is haram, whether day or night. The passage then includes a note about a statement attributed to Ibn 'Abbas regarding ta'rid, critiquing it and citing Al-Bayhaqi and Al-Fath.

Key Terms
عدة ('iddah): The waiting period a woman observes after divorce or death before remarrying.
خروج (khuruj): Leaving the house during 'iddah; generally prohibited except for necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the prohibition of a divorced woman leaving her home during iddah?
A) A hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him).
B) The Quranic verse 'Do not expel them from their houses nor let them leave'.
C) Consensus of the Companions.
D) A legal maxim.

Key Takeaway
A divorced woman in iddah is prohibited from leaving her home unless a manifest necessity arises.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
10 min
The Prohibition of Leaving the 'Iddah Residence and the Meaning of Fahisha Mubayyina
Explains the meaning of 'fahisha mubayyina' and rules for leaving during 'iddah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a woman's obligation to remain in her 'iddah residence and explain the intra-madhhab dispute over the definition of fahisha mubayyina.

Core Idea
Staying in the iddah residence is a divine right; the sole exception is a manifest lewd act, interpreted as adultery by most Hanafi scholars.

This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses one of the most practically significant rulings in family law: the requirement for a woman in 'iddah to remain in her residence. The key legal nuance lies in the Quranic exception for 'fahisha mubayyina' (manifest lewd act). Within the Hanafi school, there is a classic disagreement over the scope of this term. Understanding this dispute and the general prohibition will enable you to correctly advise on a wide range of scenarios involving divorced and widowed women.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Ruling and Its Basis
The passage establishes that a free Muslim woman in 'iddah of divorce must stay in her residence. This is a wajib obligation because it is a right of Allah, not a right of the husband. The evidence is from the Quranic verse (65:1) and a hadith that is zanni al-thubut.
The distinction between fard and wajib is critical: fard is proven by qati dalil, wajib by zanni dalil.
2
Define the Exception: Fahisha Mubayyina
The verse allows leaving only if she commits a manifest lewd act. Two interpretations exist: (1) it is specifically adultery (Ibn Mas'ud, adopted by Abu Yusuf), or (2) it includes any open disobedience like nushuz (Ibn Umar, adopted by Abu Hanifa). The passage discusses the linguistic elegance of the broader interpretation.
The phrase 'illa an' is treated as a boundary; if the act is the same as the prohibition, the exception would be self-referential.
3
Apply the Ruling to Different Categories of Women
The general prohibition applies to free Muslim women. Slave women (including umm walad, mukataba) are allowed to leave because service is the master's right. Minor and insane women are not obligated to observe ihdad but can be prevented by the husband. A kitabiyya (non-Muslim) is not obligated unless the husband prevents her.
The distinction is based on legal capacity (taklif) and rights of others.
4
Distinguish the Widow's 'Iddah
A widow may leave during the day for work because she has no maintenance (nafaqa), but she must return at night. This is a necessity-based exception, not a general permission. The divorced woman, who receives nafaqa, does not have this allowance.
The difference is rooted in financial need, not in the nature of 'iddah.
5
Conclude with the Mufta Bihi and Practical Consequences
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that fahisha mubayyina refers specifically to adultery. Thus, a woman can only be expelled for that specific crime. However, both interpretations agree that leaving without a valid excuse (necessity or fahisha) is prohibited. The husband's permission does not override the divine right.
The practical difference is limited because the criteria for 'necessity' are strict.
