Lineage and Mahr in Conditional Divorce: Six-Month Birth
Explaining the ruling on lineage when a woman gives birth six months after a conditional divorce marriage, and the dispute on the mahr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on lineage and mahr when a woman gives birth six months after a conditional divorce marriage, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding the mahr amount.

Core Idea
Istihsan over qiyas in conditional divorce: one mahr, not one and a half.

This lesson examines a classic Hanafi scenario: a man declares 'If I marry her, she is divorced', marries, and the spouse gives birth exactly six months later. The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses two interrelated issues: establishing the child's lineage and determining the mahr. While lineage is secured by the marriage bed (firash), the mahr amount sparks an internal Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, hinging on whether to apply analogy (qiyas) or juristic preference (istihsan). Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to analyze ikhtilaf within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core scenario
The man says 'If I marry her, she is divorced', then marries. The woman gives birth exactly six months from the marriage date. Determine what legal issues arise: lineage and mahr.
The six-month period is critical; less or more changes the ruling.
2
Analyze the lineage ruling
Why is the child's lineage established? Because the birth at six months from marriage means conception occurred during the marriage, making the woman a valid bed (firash). Marriage itself suffices, even without actual consummation.
The text discusses a conceptualization (tasawwur) of intercourse at the moment of marriage, but some scholars deem it unnecessary; firash alone is enough.
3
Understand the mahr dispute
Abu Hanifa: one full mahr (istihsan). Abu Yusuf: one and a half mahr (qiyas: half for pre-consummation divorce + full mahr for intercourse). Track the reasoning behind each.
The dispute is intra-Hanafi, so both views are within the school, but Abu Hanifa's is mufta bihi.
4
Evaluate the role of istihsan
Istihsan overrides qiyas here: the marriage contract is treated as equivalent to consummation, so only one mahr is due. This protects lineage and avoids hardship.
Istihsan is a distinctive Hanafi source that allows departure from strict analogy in favor of equity.
5
Apply the tarjih
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is Abu Hanifa's one mahr, because istihsan is stronger and the scenario assumes intercourse coincided with marriage.
Later Hanafi authorities like al-Marghinani and Ibn Abidin uphold this view.

Chapter of Establishing Lineage. Since it is from the effects of pregnancy, it is mentioned after the 'iddah. His statement: (And whoever said: If I marry her, she is divorced, then she gave birth at six months from the day he married her, then his lineage and her mahr are incumbent upon him.) As for lineage, because she is his bed (firash), since she brought the child at six months from the time of marriage, she brought it at less than that from the time of divorce, so conception was before it in the state of marriage. The conceptualization is established by him marrying her while he is intermixing with her, so ejaculation coincided with the marriage. Lineage is something that requires caution in establishing it. Marrying in this situation is either by both of them speaking and the witnesses hearing, or by both of them appointing an agent, and the agent marries them while they are in this state. The second is better as is not hidden. And it can be said: Interpreting this upon the case where he married her while he is intermixing with her imposes imputation of unlawful on a Muslim, which is not permissible. Hence some leading scholars avoided establishing this conceptualization and said: There is no need for this artificiality; rather the existence of the bed is sufficient, and possibility of intercourse is not considered because marriage takes its place, as in the case of a man from the East marrying a woman from the West with a year's distance between them, which the prohibited degree may prevent, as they said it is disliked to seclude with a young female in-law. Reflect on his statement: (And according to the Nihaya and others, return is determined.) Mentioned in al-Fath that it is what the author's absolute statement in the first issue requires, and that it is the most plausible because when she returns she becomes a resident, and when she goes she is a traveler until she reaches the destination. So if she can refrain from continuing travel during the 'iddah, it is determined upon her. Chapter of Establishing Lineage. His statement: (And Abu Yusuf said in al-Imla'...) He said in al-Fath: And Abu Yusuf's expression in al-Amali as: ... Then she brought a child at six months from the day he married her. But in Fath al-Qadir, the truth is that conceptualization is a condition, and hence if a boy's wife brings a child, his lineage is not established. And the conceptualization is established in the case of the Western woman due to the establishment of miracles of saints and divine uses, so he becomes the owner of a [special] step... and he did not answer what we mentioned. He restricted it to her giving birth at six months without increase or decrease, because if she gave birth at less than that, his lineage is not established because conception then was from a husband before the marriage; and if she gave birth at more than that, it is also not established due to the possibility of its occurrence after the divorce, and we have judged that where we judged the non-obligation of 'iddah for being before consummation and seclusion, and the nullity of this judgment is not clear. Fath al-Qadir objected to their denial of lineage here in a period in which it is possible to be from him, which is two years, contradicting caution in establishing it. The mentioned possibility is extremely remote, for the continuous custom is that pregnancy is six months, and perhaps epochs pass without having heard of birth at six months. So the apparent is that it did not occur, and its occurrence is a possibility. Then what caution is there in establishing lineage if we deny it for a weak possibility that requires its denial, and we leave the apparent that requires its establishment? Would that I knew which of the two possibilities is more remote: the possibility they assumed for conceptualizing conception from him to establish lineage, which is that he married her while having intercourse with her, and people heard their speech while they were in that state, then ejaculation coincided with the contract; or the possibility that the pregnancy if it exceeds six months by a day could be from other than him. As for the mahr, because when lineage is established from him, he is considered a partner by legal fiction, so the mahr is confirmed by it. And Abu Yusuf said in al-Imla': By analogy, one and a half mahr is due by intercourse after the divorce and before it. The answer is: If we assume that he married her in the state of intercourse, the intercourse was not after the divorce, so only one mahr is required of him. Ibn Bandar mentioned in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir, and by this is refuted what was said that the establishment of lineage does not necessitate his intercourse because pregnancy may occur by the insertion of water into the vagina without intercourse, though it is rare, and the apparent aspect is what is customary. And in Fath al-Qadir: Know that if the correct view in establishing this lineage is the possibility of consummation, and its conceptualization is only by what mentioned of marrying her in the state of his initiated intercourse before marriage, and the ruling is that one mahr is due in it. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth reported: By analogy, one and a half mahr should be due on the husband because divorce fell upon her, so half the mahr became obligatory, and another mahr by consummation. He said: But Abu Hanifa used istihsan and said: Only one mahr is obligatory because we deemed it equivalent to consummation in terms of legal ruling, so the dowry is strengthened and the obligation of increase is ambiguous. This expression for the contemplative does not necessitate his statement of the necessity of one and a half mahr; rather it is apparent in denying that because istihsan is given precedence over qiyas, so narration from him to that effect is not permissible. His statement: (While it is rare and the apparent aspect is what is customary). Al-Maqdisi said in his commentary: I say: It is not rarer than the Eastern man marrying the Western woman and attaching his lineage to her, so it is interpreted upon it and saved from imputing a Muslim with corruption, which is intercourse and contract with her. His statement: (And know that when the correct view) is a refutation of al-Zayla'i where he said: It should be that two mahr are due upon him: one mahr by intercourse and one mahr by marriage, just as if a man married a woman while having intercourse with her, two mahr would be due upon him. His statement: (It necessitates what was mentioned being absolute and relative) 'kawn' in nominative is the subject of 'yalzam'.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion of a ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the establishment of lineage and the mahr in a conditional divorce scenario. The author explains that if a man says 'If I marry her, she is divorced' and then marries the woman, and she gives birth exactly six months from the marriage date, the child's lineage is established and the full mahr is due. The reasoning is that since the birth occurs at six months from the marriage, it must have been conceived during the marriage, making the woman a valid bed (firash) for the husband. The author addresses an objection that this scenario assumes intercourse at the exact moment of marriage, which might be considered sinful, but responds that the marriage contract itself suffices to establish the bed, as exemplified by the case of a couple from distant lands where consummation is impossible yet lineage can still be established. The passage then discusses the dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding the mahr. Abu Hanifa holds that only one mahr is due, considering the intercourse as part of the marriage contract by legal fiction (istihsan). Abu Yusuf argues that by analogy (qiyas), one and a half mahr should be due: half for the divorce that occurred before consummation and a full mahr for the subsequent intercourse. The author supports Abu Hanifa's view, noting that istihsan takes precedence over qiyas. The passage also clarifies that the birth must be exactly at six months; less than six months indicates conception before marriage, and more than six months suggests conception after divorce. The author refutes a potential argument that pregnancy could occur without intercourse (e.g., by insemination without penetration) by stating this is rare and the customary ruling is based on the usual case. Finally, it addresses a critique by al-Zayla'i who argued for two mahr, and the author refutes this.

Key Terms
فراش (firash): The married woman considered as a 'bed' for the husband, implying that any child she bears is attributed to him if conception is possible during the marriage.
علوق (aluq): Conception; the beginning of pregnancy. The text uses it to determine timing relative to marriage and divorce.
مهر (mahr): The mandatory bridal gift from husband to wife in marriage. In this context, it discusses the amount due under a conditional divorce scenario.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; a method of legal reasoning where one sets aside qiyas (analogy) in favor of a more equitable ruling based on stronger evidence or public interest.
تصور (tasawwur): Conceptualization; the mental conception of the scenario. Here, it refers to the specific scenario of marrying during intercourse to make lineage plausible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the mahr due when a man says 'If I marry her, she is divorced', marries, and the woman gives birth exactly six months from the marriage?
A) Half a mahr
B) One full mahr
C) One and a half mahr
D) Two mahr

Key Takeaway
In conditional divorce with birth at six months, the child's lineage is established and only one mahr is due, as istihsan overrides qiyas.

Talaq — Divorce

Paternity and Mahr in Divorce and Marriage: Hanafi Rulings and Disputes
Rulings on paternity after divorce and mahr dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the six-month minimum and two-year maximum rules for paternity after divorce in Hanafi fiqh, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on mahr when marriage occurs during intercourse.

Core Idea
Paternity follows a strict six-month minimum and two-year maximum window; when marriage occurs during intercourse, istihsan reduces the mahr to one instead of one and a half.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq tackles two interconnected issues: establishing paternity after divorce and the calculation of mahr when a man marries a woman while having intercourse with her. The Hanafi school applies a minimum pregnancy period of six months and a maximum of two years to determine paternity, with special rules for revocable divorce. A key intra-Hanafi dispute arises regarding the dower: Abu Hanifa uses istihsan to rule that only one mahr is due, while Abu Yusuf argues by qiyas for one and a half. Understanding these rulings requires distinguishing between the apparent (zahir) and the exceptional, and recognizing the precedence of istihsan over qiyas in the Hanafi method.

Guided Path
1
Identify the paternity window after divorce
Note that paternity is established if the child is born at least six months and at most two years after the last possible intercourse (marriage or divorce). Birth less than six months negates paternity (conception before marriage). Birth more than two years also negates paternity unless in revocable divorce where the two-year maximum applies from the date of divorce.
The six-month minimum is derived from Quran (46:15) and hadith; the two-year maximum is based on Hanafi istihsan and common practice.
2
Apply the rules to revocable divorce (raj'ah)
For a revocably divorced woman, paternity is established if she gives birth within two years of divorce, as long as she does not admit the iddah has passed. If birth occurs after two years, it does not establish paternity because the waiting period would have ended earlier.
The rationale is that intercourse during iddah is permissible, so conception can occur up to two years from divorce.
3
Examine the mahr dispute when marriage coincides with intercourse
The scenario: a man marries a woman while having intercourse with her (he says 'I marry you' during intercourse, she accepts, and they continue). After the contract, he divorces her before consummation. How much mahr is due? Abu Hanifa says one mahr by istihsan; Abu Yusuf says one and a half by qiyas.
The source passage discusses this dispute extensively, with the author supporting Abu Hanifa's view because istihsan overrides qiyas in Hanafi usul.
4
Understand the Hanafi rebuttal to the qiyas argument
Abu Yusuf's qiyas: divorce incurs half mahr, intercourse incurs full mahr → one and a half total. Abu Hanifa's response: the intercourse is contemporaneous with the contract, so the entire act has a semblance of permissibility (shubhat al-hill). The single act of intercourse is treated as part of the marriage, so only one mahr is due.
The key distinction is that here the intercourse and contract are simultaneous, not sequential. If the intercourse were entirely before the contract (adultery), then two mahr would be due (one for adultery, one for marriage) – but that is not this case.
5
Recognize the practical consequence (thamar al-khilaf)
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa (mufta bihi), the husband pays one mahr. Under Abu Yusuf, he would pay one and a half. The fatwa today follows Abu Hanifa's view.
The tarjih authority is Ibn al-Humam in Fath al-Qadir and the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.

She came with a child at six months from the day he married her, but in Fath al-Qadir: the correct view is that conception is a condition, and therefore if a boy's wife brings a child, his lineage is not established, and the conception is confirmed in the Moroccan [case] due to the establishment of miracles of saints and the use of spirits, so he is a possessor of a step or he did not answer what we mentioned. He restricted it to her giving birth at six months without increase or decrease because if she gave birth to it less than that, his lineage is not established because conception then is from a husband before marriage, and if she gave birth to it more than that, it is also not established due to the possibility of its occurrence after divorce, and we have judged that where we judged the non-obligation of the waiting period because it was before entry and seclusion, and the invalidity of this judgment has not become clear. And he objected to it in Fath al-Qadir: their denial of lineage here in a period in which it is conceivable that it could be from him, which is two years, contradicts caution in establishing it, and the mentioned possibility is extremely remote, for the continuous custom is that pregnancy is six months, and perhaps ages pass without having heard of birth at six months, so the apparent is its non-occurrence and its occurrence is a possibility, so what caution is there in establishing lineage if we deny it for a weak possibility that necessitates its denial and we leave an apparent that necessitates its affirmation. I wish I knew which of the two possibilities is more remote: the possibility they assumed for conceiving from him to establish lineage, which is that he married her while having intercourse with her and people heard their speech while they were in that state, then the ejaculation coincided with the contract; or the possibility that if the pregnancy exceeds six months by a day, it is from someone else. As for the dower, because when lineage is established from him, he is considered an interceder by ruling, so the dower is affirmed by it. And Abu Yusuf said in al-Imla': by analogy, one and a half mahr is obligatory by intercourse after the occurrence of divorce and before it. The answer: if we estimate that he married her at the time of intercourse, the intercourse was not after divorce, so only one mahr is incumbent upon him. Ibn Bandar mentioned in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir: and with it is repelled what was said: the establishment of lineage from him does not necessitate his intercourse because pregnancy may occur by introducing water into the vagina without intercourse, although it is rare, but the apparent face is what is customary. And in Fath al-Qadir: know that if the most correct in establishing this lineage is the possibility of entry and its conceptualization is only by what was mentioned of marrying her during the intercourse that began before marriage, and a single mahr has been ruled in it. In what al-Faqih Abu al-Layth transmitted, it is appropriate by analogy that one and a half mahr is obligatory on the husband because divorce occurred upon her, so half the mahr is obligatory, and another mahr by entry. He said: but Abu Hanifa used istihsan and said: only one mahr is obligatory because we made it in the position of entry by way of ruling, so that dower was confirmed and the obligation of increase was dubious. This statement for the contemplator does not necessitate his saying of the obligation of one and a half mahr, rather its apparent is the negation of that because istihsan is given precedence over qiyas, so narration from him about that is not permissible. His statement: (although it is rare and the apparent face is what is customary). Al-Maqdisi said in his commentary: I say: it is not rarer than the marriage of the Moroccan to the Easterner and the attribution of her lineage to him, so it is interpreted accordingly and he is saved from accusing the Muslim of corruption, which is intercourse and the contract with her. His statement: (and know that if the most correct) is a refutation of al-Zayla'i where he said: it would be appropriate that two mahr be obligatory upon him, mahr by intercourse and mahr by marriage, just as if he married a woman while he was having intercourse with her, he would owe two mahr. His statement: (the necessity of what was mentioned being absolute and attributed) the word 'being' is nominative as the subject of 'necessitates'. And it is established: the lineage of the child of a revocably divorced woman is established even if she gives birth to it more than two years after divorce, as long as she does not admit the passage of the waiting period, and there is a return in the greater part of the two years not in the lesser, and a definitive divorce for less than that, otherwise no, except according to a clear report. The necessity of what he mentioned being absolute and attributed, and we presented it in the chapter on dower: if he married her while having intercourse with her, he owes two mahr: mahr for adultery because the hadd is dropped by marriage before its completion, and mahr for marriage because this is more than seclusion. This is problematic because it contradicts the explicit school view. Also, the act is one and has been characterized by the semblance of permissibility, so one mahr is obligatory, unlike the case where he said 'if I marry her, she is divorced' and he forgot, then married and had intercourse with her, where one and a half mahr is obligatory because divorce is before intercourse. As for here, divorce is with permissible intercourse in a single act, so the entire act has a semblance of permissibility, and the dower has been obliged, so no other dower is obligatory. So the words of the author indicate two issues: one is that whoever divorces his wife before consummation and she brings a child less than six months from the time he divorced her, it is incumbent upon him because we are certain of conception during the marriage; if she brings it at six months or more, it is not incumbent upon him due to lack of certainty, and in this ruling, women with monthly periods and women with months are equal. The second: that whoever marries a woman and she gives birth less than six months from the time of marriage, his lineage is not established, and its explicit mention will come. And it is mentioned in al-Nihayah that he is not considered muhsan (protected) by intercourse in the issue of the book. His statement: (and the lineage of the child of a revocably divorced woman is established even if she gives birth to it more than two years, as long as she does not admit the passage of the waiting period) there is a return in the greater part of the two years not in the lesser, meaning from the two years due to the possibility of conception during the waiting period because it is possible that she has an extended menstrual period. If she brings it less than two years, she becomes separated from her husband due to the expiration of the waiting period, and his lineage is established due to the existence of conception in marriage or in the waiting period, and he does not become a returner (muraji') because it is possible that conception occurred before divorce and after it, so he does not become a returner by doubt. If she brings it more than two years, there is a return because conception is after divorce, and it is apparent that it is from him due to the absence of adultery on her part, so by intercourse he becomes a returner. The principle is that the minimum duration of pregnancy is six months and the maximum is two years. So in every place where intercourse is permissible, it is determined by the minimum which is the nearest time, except if it necessitates establishing a return by doubt, or effecting a divorce by doubt, or entitlement to property. And 'was' is attached to its noun which is 'what' relative. And his statement: 'absolute and attributed' are two states of 'what', and the intended meaning is that it is mentioned sometimes not attributing to anyone and sometimes attributed. And his statement: 'we presented it' the pronoun refers back to 'what', and the waw is for the state, and the sentence is a parenthetical state between the noun of existence and its predicate which is 'problematic'. And his statement: 'due to its contradiction' is a cause for the necessity of the mentioned problem. This, and some scholars responded to the problem saying: the correct way to picture the issue is to say that he first said 'I marry you', then inserted and ejaculated, and she said 'I accept' at one time, so the intercourse occurred in the core of the contract, not preceding it nor following the occurrence of divorce. That is, contrary to if he first had illicit intercourse then performed the contract before withdrawal, because when the hadd is dropped by the contract, the dower of the first intercourse is obligatory, and the second dower is obligatory by the contract that occurred during his intercourse, and in that situation there is only the dower that came by the contract, so there is no basis for al-Zayla'i's statement, and one branch cannot be analogized to the other. His statement: (except) if it necessitates establishing a return by doubt, etc. We will mention from al-Fath the clarification of this when discussing.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, addresses the complex rulings on establishing paternity (nasab) in cases of divorce and new marriage. The author, Zayla'i, begins by discussing a key scenario: if a woman gives birth exactly six months after marrying a man, paternity is established, but this is conditional upon the possibility of conception. He refutes an objection that paternity should also be established for births up to two years, arguing that the standard pregnancy is six months, and extending it would lead to uncertainty. The passage then delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the dower (mahr) when a man marries a woman while having intercourse with her. Abu Hanifa rules that only one mahr is due, using istihsan (juristic preference) to treat the intercourse as part of the marriage contract. Abu Yusuf, by qiyas (analogy), argues for one and a half mahr. The commentator defends Abu Hanifa's view, emphasizing that istihsan overrides qiyas. Finally, the author summarizes two main issues from the text: (1) If a woman divorces before consummation and gives birth within six months, paternity is established; if later, it is not. (2) If a woman marries and gives birth within six months of marriage, paternity is not established. The passage also discusses the iddah of a revocably divorced woman: if she gives birth within two years, paternity is established and it counts as a revocable return (raj'ah); if after two years, it does not.

Key Terms
العلوق (al-'uluq): Conception or impregnation
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas in certain cases
الرجعة (al-raj'ah): Revocable return of a divorced wife during her iddah

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a man divorces his wife before consummation and she gives birth exactly six months after the divorce, what is the ruling on paternity?
A) Paternity is not established because birth after divorce is always suspect.
B) Paternity is established because six months is the minimum pregnancy period and conception likely occurred during marriage.
C) Paternity is established only if the wife admits the child is from the husband.
D) Paternity is not established because the divorce cut off any possible lineage.

Key Takeaway
Paternity after divorce hinges on a six-month minimum and two-year maximum window; when marriage coincides with intercourse, istihsan dictates a single mahr.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
Establishing Lineage of Child from Divorcée in Iddah
Fiqh ruling with expanded commentary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on establishing lineage for a child born to a woman in revocable and irrevocable iddah, and identify the exceptions when the woman admits the iddah has passed.

Core Idea
For revocable iddah, lineage is established regardless of time; for irrevocable, only within two years.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the critical distinction between revocable and irrevocable divorce when determining paternity of a child born during the iddah. The ruling hinges on the maximum pregnancy period of two years and the woman's admission about the end of her waiting period. Understanding these rules preserves the child's lineage and prevents accusations of zina.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of divorce
Determine whether the divorce was revocable (raj'i) or irrevocable (ba'in). This classification dictates the time limit for establishing lineage.
Revocable divorce allows the husband to return to the wife during iddah without a new contract.
2
Check if the woman admits the iddah has passed
If the woman admits that her iddah has ended, the ruling changes. For revocable divorce, lineage is not established unless the child is born within six months of her admission.
The minimum iddah period for those with menses is 39 days (Sahibayn) or 60 days (Abu Hanifa).
3
Apply the time limit
For revocable divorce, lineage is established even if the child is born after two years, unless she admitted the iddah passed. For irrevocable divorce, lineage is only established if the child is born within two years from the divorce.
The maximum pregnancy period is set at two years according to Hanafi school.
4
Determine if it constitutes a revocable return (ruj'ah)
If a child is born after two years in a revocable divorce, the husband's intercourse that led to the conception is considered a revocable return (ruj'ah), reviving the marriage.
This is because the conception must have occurred after the divorce, and the presumption is that it is from the husband.

محصناً بالوطء في مسألة الكتاب قوله : (ويثبت نسب ولد معتدة الرجعي وإن ولدته لأكثر من سنتين ما لم تقر بمضي العدة رجعة في الأكثر منهما لا في الأقل منهما أي من السنتين لاحتمال العلوق في حالة العدة لجواز بالشك، وإن جاءت به لأكثر من سنتين كانت رجعة لأن العلوق بعد الطلاق والظاهر أنه منه لانتفاء الزنا منها فيصير بالوطء مراجعاً والأصل أن أقل مدة الحمل ستة أشهر وأكثرها سنتان، ففي كل موضع يباح الوطء فيه فهي مقدرة بالأقل وهو أقرب الأوقات إلا أن يلزم إثبات رجعة بالشك أو إيقاع طلاق بالشك أو استحقاق مال وكانت مضاف إلى اسمه وهو ما الموصولة . وقوله مطلقاً» ومنسوباً» حالان من «ما» والمراد ذكر تارة غير معزة لأحد وتارة ذكر معزواً. وقوله وقدمناه الضمير عائد على «ما» والواو للحال والجملة حالية معترضة بين اسم الكون وبين خبره وهو قوله «مشكلاً». وقوله لمخالفته» تعليل للزوم إشكال المذكور. هذا وأجاب بعض الفضلاء عن الإشكال فقال : الصواب في تصوير المسألة أن يقال إنه قال أولاً تزوجتك ثم أولج وأمنى وقالت قبلت في وقت واحد فكان الوطء حاصلاً في صلب العقد غير متقدم عليه ولا متأخر عن وقوع الطلاق .اهـ. أي بخلاف ما إذا وطىء أولاً حراماً ثم أجرى العقد قبل النزع فإنه لما سقط الحد بالعقد وجب مهر الوطء الأول والمهر الثاني وجب بالعقد الجاري حال وطئه وليس في تلك إلا المهر الذي حصل بالعقد فلا وجه لكلام الزيلعي ولا يقاس أحد الفرعين على الآخر قوله : (إلا) أن يلزم إثبات رجعة بالشك الخ سنذكر عن الفتح توضيح هذا عند الكلام بالشك فحينئذ يستند العلوق إلى أبعد الأوقات وهو ما قبل الطلاق لأن هذه الأشياء لا تثبت بالشك، وفي كل موضع لا يباح الوطء فيه فمدة الحمل سنتان ويكون العلوق مستنداً إلى أبعد الأوقات للحاجة إلى إثبات النسب وأمره مبني على الاحتياط؛ كذا في غاية البيان. أطلق في الأكثر منهما فشمل عشرين سنة أو أكثر، وقيد بعدم إقرارها لأنها لو أقرت بانقضائها والمدة محتملة بأن يكون ستين يوماً على قول أبي حنيفة وتسعة وثلاثين يوماً على قولهما، ثم جاءت بولد لا يثبت نسبه إلا إذا جاءت به لأقل من ستة أشهر من وقت الإقرار فإنه يثبت نسبه للتيقين بقيام الحمل وقت الإقرار فيظهر كذبها. وإنما نفى الأقل بقوله «لا في الأقل منهما مع فهمه من التقييد بالأكثر لبيان أن حكم السنتين حكم الأكثر ولذا قال في الاختيار : وإذا جاءت به لسنتين أو أكثر كان رجعة ا هـ . وأطلق في المعتدة فشمل المعتدة بالحيض أو بالأشهر ليأسها، ولا فرق بينهما كما في البدائع إلا إذا أقرت بانقضائها بالأشهر لإياسها مفسراً بثلاثة أشهر فإنه يثبت نسب ولدها إذا جاءت به لأقل من سنتين من وقت الطلاق بائناً كان أو رجعياً لأنها لما ولدت تبين أنها لم تكن آيسة فتبين أن عدتها لم تكن بالأشهر فلم يصح إقرارها بانقضاء عدتها بالأشهر فصار كأنها لم تقر أصلاً قوله : ( والبت لأقل منهما) أي ويثبت نسب ولد معتدة الطلاق البائن إذا ولدته لأقل من سنتين من وقت الطلاق لأنه يحتمل أن يكون الولد قائماً وقت الطلاق فلا يتيقن بزوال الفراش فيثبت النسب احتياطاً قوله : (وإلا لا صادق بصورتين بما إذا أتت به لسنتين فقط، وبما إذا أتت به لأكثر منهما، واقتصر الشارح على الثاني. وصرح في المجتبى والنقاية بأن حكم السنتين كالأكثر وهو المختصر، أما إذا أتت به لأكثر منهما فظاهر لأن الحمل حادث بعد الطلاق فلا يكون منه لحرمة وطئها في العدة بخلاف الرجعي، وأما إذا أتت به لتمام السنتين فمشكل فإنهم اتفقوا ظاهر على شرح قول المصنف «فلو نكح أمة فطلقها قوله : (ولا فرق بينهما كما في البدائع إلا إذا أقرت الخ) أقول : عبارة البدائع هكذا فإن كانت آيسة فجاءت بولد فإن كانت لم تقر بانقضاء العدة فحكمها حكم ذوات الأقراء سواء كان الطلاق رجعياً أو بائناً، وإذا جاءت بولد إلى سنتين من وقت الطلاق ثبت نسبه من الزوج لأنها لما ولدت علم أنها ليست بآيسة بل هي من ذوات الأقراء، وإن كانت أقرت بانقضاء عدتها فإن كانت أقرت به مفسراً بثلاثة أشهر فكذلك لأنه لما تبين أنها ليست بآيسة تبين أن عدتها لم تكن بالأشهر فلم يصح إقرارها بانقضاء عدتها بالأشهر فالتحق إقرارها بالعدم وجعل كأنها لم تقر أصلاً، وإن كانت أقرت به مطلقاً في مدة تصلح لثلاثة إقراء، فإن ولدت لأقل من ستة أشهر منذ أقرت ثبت النسب وإلا فلا لأنه لما بطل اليأس تعذر حمل إقرارها على الإقرار بالانقضاء بالأشهر لبطلان الاعتداد فيحمل على الإقرار بالانقضاء بالأقراء حملاً لكلام العاقلة المسلمة على الصحة عند الإمكان اهـ.
And the lineage of the child of a woman in revocable divorce iddah is established even if she gives birth after more than two years, as long as she does not admit the passing of the iddah, and it becomes a revocable return in the case of more than two years, not in less than two years. And for irrevocable divorce, lineage is established if she gives birth within less than two years, otherwise not, except according to explicit narration... etc.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the Hanafi ruling on establishing the paternity of a child born to a divorced woman during her iddah. The author distinguishes between revocable and irrevocable divorce. For revocable iddah, the child's lineage is established from the husband regardless of when the child is born (within or beyond two years), provided the woman does not confirm that her iddah has ended. If she gives birth after more than two years, the husband's intercourse that led to the conception becomes a revocable return. For irrevocable divorce, lineage is only established if the child is born within two years from the divorce, because after that the pregnancy is presumed to have occurred after the marriage was fully dissolved. The passage also discusses exceptions when establishing lineage would lead to establishing a revocable return by doubt, or other consequences. Additionally, it covers the case of a woman who is menopausal and claims her iddah has ended; if she later gives birth, her claim is invalidated and the lineage is established if the child is born within two years from the divorce. The ruling is based on the principle that the minimum period of pregnancy is six months and the maximum is two years, and lineage is established on the basis of caution.

Key Terms
عدة (iddah): The waiting period a divorced woman must observe before remarrying.
رجعة (ruj'ah): Revocable return; the husband takes back his wife during the iddah without a new marriage contract.
بائن (ba'in): Irrevocable divorce; the marriage ends immediately and cannot be resumed without a new contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a woman in revocable iddah gives birth three years after the divorce and does not admit that the iddah has ended, what is the ruling on the child's lineage?
A) The child's lineage is established from the husband and it is considered a revocable return.
B) The child's lineage is not established because the maximum pregnancy period is two years.
C) The child's lineage is established only if the woman admits the child is from the husband.
D) The child's lineage is established but the husband does not become a revocable returnee.

Key Takeaway
For revocable divorce, lineage is always established unless the woman admits iddah passed; for irrevocable, lineage is only established within two years.

Lineage of a Bā'in Woman's Child Within Two Years: The General Rule
Explains the general rule and proof for bā'in divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that the child of a bā'in woman is attributed to the husband if born within less than two years of divorce and explain the proof based on precaution (ihtiyat).

Core Idea
Lineage of a bā'in woman's child is established up to two years because possibility preserves the marital bed.

This lesson examines the core principle from al-Bahr al-Raiq: when an irrevocably divorced woman gives birth, her child's lineage is attributed to the former husband if the birth occurs within two years. The ruling is built on the possibility that the child could have existed at the time of divorce, and the Sharī‘ah's precaution in preserving lineage. We will unpack the proof and the underlying rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the act is the birth of a child by a bā'in woman after divorce. The ruling is that lineage is established (thubūt al-nasab) from the husband under specific conditions.
The ruling is declaratory (hukm wad‘ī), not taklīfī.
2
Locate the dalīl
The text states the proof: because it is possible that the child existed at the time of divorce, so we are not certain that the marital bed (al-firāsh) is removed.
The proof is rooted in the maxim 'al-walad li al-firāsh' and the principle of ihtiyāt.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalālah
The possibility of conception before divorce creates doubt about the separation. Certainty is not overridden by doubt, so the precautionary approach is to affirm lineage.
Hanafi usul: certainty is not removed by doubt.
4
Note the condition of no admission
The rule applies only if the woman has not validly admitted that her iddah has expired in a way that contradicts the possibility of pregnancy.
Admission can change the ruling, as explored in the next lesson.
5
Identify the muftā bihī
The relied-upon position is that lineage is established if the child is born within less than two years (including exactly two years), as supported by al-Mujtabā and al-Niqāyah.
The author (Ibn Nujaym) adopts this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

فمدة الحمل سنتان ويكون العلوق مستنداً إلى أبعد الأوقات للحاجة إلى إثبات النسب وأمره مبني على الاحتياط؛ كذا في غاية البيان. أطلق في الأكثر منهما فشمل عشرين سنة أو أكثر، وقيد بعدم إقرارها لأنها لو أقرت الطلاق بائناً كان أو رجعياً لأنها لما ولدت تبين أنها لم تكن آيسة فتبين أن عدتها لم تكن بالأشهر فلم يصح إقرارها بانقضاء عدتها بالأشهر فصار كأنها لم تقر أصلاً قوله : ( والبت لأقل منهما) أي ويثبت نسب ولد معتدة الطلاق البائن إذا ولدته لأقل من سنتين من وقت الطلاق لأنه يحتمل أن يكون الولد قائماً وقت الطلاق فلا يتيقن بزوال الفراش فيثبت النسب احتياطاً قوله : (وإلا لا صادق بصورتين بما إذا أتت به لسنتين فقط، وبما إذا أتت به لأكثر منهما، واقتصر الشارح على الثاني. وصرح في المجتبى والنقاية بأن حكم السنتين كالأكثر وهو المختصر، أما إذا أتت به لأكثر منهما فظاهر لأن الحمل حادث بعد الطلاق فلا يكون منه لحرمة وطئها في العدة بخلاف الرجعي، وأما إذا أتت به لتمام السنتين فمشكل فإنهم اتفقوا على شرح قول المصنف «فلو نكح أمة فطلقها قوله : (ولا فرق بينهما كما في البدائع إلا إذا أقرت الخ) أقول : عبارة البدائع هكذا فإن كانت آيسة فجاءت بولد فإن كانت لم تقر بانقضاء العدة فحكمها حكم ذوات الأقراء سواء كان الطلاق رجعياً أو بائناً، وإذا جاءت بولد إلى سنتين من وقت الطلاق ثبت نسبه من الزوج لأنها لما ولدت علم أنها ليست بآيسة بل هي من ذوات الأقراء، وإن كانت أقرت بانقضاء عدتها فإن كانت أقرت به مفسراً بثلاثة أشهر فكذلك لأنه لما تبين أنها ليست بآيسة تبين أن عدتها لم تكن بالأشهر فلم يصح إقرارها بانقضاء عدتها بالأشهر فالتحق إقرارها بالعدم وجعل كأنها لم تقر أصلاً، وإن كانت أقرت به مطلقاً في مدة تصلح لثلاثة إقراء، فإن ولدت لأقل من ستة أشهر منذ أقرت ثبت النسب وإلا فلا لأنه لما بطل اليأس تعذر حمل إقرارها على الإقرار بالانقضاء بالأشهر لبطلان الاعتداد فيحمل على الإقرار بالانقضاء بالأقراء حملاً لكلام العاقلة المسلمة على الصحة عند الإمكان اهـ.
He claims and the girl approaching puberty for less than nine months, otherwise no, and death for less than both, and the woman who admits its passing with doubt, then the attachment is attributed to the furthest time, which is before divorce, because these things are not established by doubt. And in every place where intercourse is not permissible, the period of pregnancy is two years, and the attachment is attributed to the furthest time due to the need to establish lineage, and its matter is based on precaution; thus in Ghayat al-Bayan. It was unqualified in the greater of the two, so it included twenty years or more, and it was qualified by her not admitting, because if she admitted its expiry and the period is probable, being sixty days according to Abu Hanifa and thirty-nine days according to them both, then she brings a child, his lineage is not established unless she brings him within less than six months from the time of admission, then his lineage is established due to certainty of the existence of pregnancy at the time of admission, so her lie becomes apparent. And he only negated the lesser by his statement 'no in the lesser of the two' with understanding from the qualification by the greater to show that the ruling of two years is the ruling of the greater, and therefore he said in al-Ikhtiyar: And if she brings him at two years or more, it is a return (of marriage). And it was unqualified in the woman in iddah, so it included the woman in iddah by menstruation or by months for her menopause, and there is no difference between them as in al-Bada'i except if she admitted its expiry by months for her menopause specifically as three months, then the lineage of her child is established if she brings him within less than two years from the time of divorce, whether it was a bā'in or raj'ī divorce, because when she gave birth it became clear that she was not menopausal, so it became clear that her iddah was not by months, so her admission of the expiry of her iddah by months was not valid, so it becomes as if she did not admit at all. His statement: (And the irrevocably divorced woman for less than them) meaning the lineage of the child of the irrevocably divorced woman in iddah is established if she gives birth to him within less than two years from the time of divorce, because it is possible that the child existed at the time of divorce, so it is not certain that the marital bed has been removed, so lineage is established as a precaution. His statement: (And otherwise no) is true in two cases: when she brings him at exactly two years, and when she brings him at more than them, and the commentator limited to the second. And it is explicitly stated in al-Mujtaba and al-Niqaya that the ruling of two years is like the greater, and it is the summary. As for if she brings him at more than them, it is apparent because the pregnancy occurred after divorce, so it cannot be from him due to the prohibition of intercourse during iddah, unlike the raj'ī. And as for if she brings him at exactly two years, it is problematic, for they agreed on the explanation of the statement of the author: 'If he marries a slave woman and divorces her...' His statement: (And no difference between them as in al-Bada'i, except if she admits, etc.) I say: the wording of al-Bada'i is as follows: If she was menopausal and gave birth to a child, if she did not admit the expiry of the iddah, then her ruling is the ruling of women with menstruation, whether the divorce was raj'ī or bā'in. And if she gave birth to a child within two years from the time of divorce, his lineage is established from the husband, because when she gave birth it became known that she was not menopausal, but rather she is one of the women with menstruation. And if she admitted the expiry of her iddah, if she admitted it specifically as three months, then likewise, because when it became clear that she was not menopausal, it became clear that her iddah was not by months, so her admission of the expiry of her iddah by months was not valid, so her admission is attached to non-existence and made as if she did not admit at all. And if she admitted it absolutely in a period suitable for three menstruations, then if she gives birth within less than six months since she admitted, lineage is established, otherwise not, because when menopause was invalidated, it became difficult to interpret her admission as an admission of expiry by months due to the invalidity of the waiting period, so it is interpreted as an admission of expiry by menstruation, interpreting the speech of the sane Muslim woman as correct when possible. End.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq is a commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq, addressing the chapter of establishing lineage (nasab) after divorce. The passage type is a ruling with proof, expanding on the matn's statement about the bā'in (irrevocably divorced) woman. The author explains that if a bā'in woman gives birth within less than two years from the time of divorce, the child's lineage is established from the husband. This is because we cannot be certain that the marital relationship (farash) has ended at the time of divorce; it is possible that the pregnancy existed before the divorce. The ruling is based on precaution (ihtiyat) to preserve lineage. The text then contrasts with the raj'ī (revocably divorced) woman, whose iddah ends with three menstrual cycles, but here the focus is on the bā'in. A key condition is that the woman has not admitted that her iddah has expired. If she admits it, the ruling changes: if she admits explicitly by months (because she claims menopause) and then gives birth within two years, the admission is disregarded because the birth proves she was not menopausal, so lineage is still established. If she admits absolutely, then if she gives birth within six months of the admission, lineage is established (certainty of pregnancy at admission), otherwise not. The text also addresses the case of exactly two years: some scholars view it as still within the maximum pregnancy period and thus lineage is established, while others differ. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that two years is like the greater period, so lineage is established. The authority weight is from Zahir al-Riwayah as the text cites al-Bada'i and other core works.

Key Terms
البائن (al-bā'in): The irrevocably divorced woman whose husband cannot take her back except with a new marriage and new dower.
الفراش (al-farāsh): The marital bed or relationship; used here as the basis for establishing lineage (al-walad li al-firāsh).
العلوق (al-talūq): The attachment or conception; the time when the pregnancy began.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A bā'in woman gives birth 1 year and 10 months after divorce. She has not admitted the expiry of her iddah. According to the Hanafi ruling in this text, the child's lineage is:
A) Not established because she is irrevocably divorced.
B) Established from the husband because of the possibility of conception before divorce.
C) Established only if the husband acknowledges paternity.
D) Not established because the birth is after the typical nine-month pregnancy.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school establishes lineage for a bā'in woman's child if born within two years of divorce, based on precaution (ihtiyat) and the possibility of conception during the marriage.

Exceptions: The Woman's Admission of Iddah Expiry and the Case of Exactly Two Years
Examines the effect of admission and the exactly-two-years case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a bā'in woman's admission of iddah expiry prevents lineage establishment, and state the relied-upon Hanafi position on the child born exactly two years after divorce.

Core Idea
A valid admission of iddah expiry blocks lineage; but exceptions exist when the admission is contradicted by birth within six months.

This lesson delves into two important refinements of the general rule. First, if the bā'in woman admits that her iddah has expired, the presumption of continued firāsh is broken, and lineage is generally not established unless the birth occurs within six months of that admission (proving the pregnancy existed before). Second, there is a known scholarly difference regarding whether a child born exactly two years after divorce is included: the relied-upon view (muftā bihī) treats two years like the greater period, thus establishing lineage. We will examine the reasoning behind both exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the effect of admission
If the woman admits her iddah has expired, the possibility of continued marital connection is weakened. The text details two types: specific admission by months (due to menopause) and absolute admission (suitable for three menstruations).
The admission must be valid and not contradicted by later birth.
2
Apply the rule for specific admission
If she admits expiry by months (claiming menopause) and then gives birth within two years, the admission is disregarded because the birth proves she was not menopausal. Lineage is still established.
This is because her admission becomes false.
3
Apply the rule for absolute admission
If she admits expiry absolutely in a period suitable for three menstruations, then if she gives birth within six months of the admission, lineage is established (certainty of pregnancy at admission). Otherwise, not.
The six-month window is key: the shortest possible pregnancy.
4
Analyze the dispute on exactly two years
Some scholars exclude birth at exactly two years (because the text says 'less than'), but the author cites al-Mujtabā and al-Niqāyah that two years is like the greater, so lineage is established.
The text explicitly states 'البت لأقل منهما' but the commentary expands it.
5
Identify the muftā bihī
The relied-upon position includes exactly two years, supported by the majority and by the need for caution in lineage.
This is the preferred view in the Hanafi school.

He claims and the girl approaching puberty for less than nine months, otherwise no, and death for less than both, and the woman who admits its passing with doubt, then the attachment is attributed to the furthest time, which is before divorce, because these things are not established by doubt. And in every place where intercourse is not permissible, the period of pregnancy is two years, and the attachment is attributed to the furthest time due to the need to establish lineage, and its matter is based on precaution; thus in Ghayat al-Bayan. It was unqualified in the greater of the two, so it included twenty years or more, and it was qualified by her not admitting, because if she admitted its expiry and the period is probable, being sixty days according to Abu Hanifa and thirty-nine days according to them both, then she brings a child, his lineage is not established unless she brings him within less than six months from the time of admission, then his lineage is established due to certainty of the existence of pregnancy at the time of admission, so her lie becomes apparent. And he only negated the lesser by his statement 'no in the lesser of the two' with understanding from the qualification by the greater to show that the ruling of two years is the ruling of the greater, and therefore he said in al-Ikhtiyar: And if she brings him at two years or more, it is a return (of marriage). And it was unqualified in the woman in iddah, so it included the woman in iddah by menstruation or by months for her menopause, and there is no difference between them as in al-Bada'i except if she admitted its expiry by months for her menopause specifically as three months, then the lineage of her child is established if she brings him within less than two years from the time of divorce, whether it was a bā'in or raj'ī divorce, because when she gave birth it became clear that she was not menopausal, so it became clear that her iddah was not by months, so her admission of the expiry of her iddah by months was not valid, so it becomes as if she did not admit at all. His statement: (And the irrevocably divorced woman for less than them) meaning the lineage of the child of the irrevocably divorced woman in iddah is established if she gives birth to him within less than two years from the time of divorce, because it is possible that the child existed at the time of divorce, so it is not certain that the marital bed has been removed, so lineage is established as a precaution. His statement: (And otherwise no) is true in two cases: when she brings him at exactly two years, and when she brings him at more than them, and the commentator limited to the second. And it is explicitly stated in al-Mujtaba and al-Niqaya that the ruling of two years is like the greater, and it is the summary. As for if she brings him at more than them, it is apparent because the pregnancy occurred after divorce, so it cannot be from him due to the prohibition of intercourse during iddah, unlike the raj'ī. And as for if she brings him at exactly two years, it is problematic, for they agreed on the explanation of the statement of the author: 'If he marries a slave woman and divorces her...' His statement: (And no difference between them as in al-Bada'i, except if she admits, etc.) I say: the wording of al-Bada'i is as follows: If she was menopausal and gave birth to a child, if she did not admit the expiry of the iddah, then her ruling is the ruling of women with menstruation, whether the divorce was raj'ī or bā'in. And if she gave birth to a child within two years from the time of divorce, his lineage is established from the husband, because when she gave birth it became known that she was not menopausal, but rather she is one of the women with menstruation. And if she admitted the expiry of her iddah, if she admitted it specifically as three months, then likewise, because when it became clear that she was not menopausal, it became clear that her iddah was not by months, so her admission of the expiry of her iddah by months was not valid, so her admission is attached to non-existence and made as if she did not admit at all. And if she admitted it absolutely in a period suitable for three menstruations, then if she gives birth within less than six months since she admitted, lineage is established, otherwise not, because when menopause was invalidated, it became difficult to interpret her admission as an admission of expiry by months due to the invalidity of the waiting period, so it is interpreted as an admission of expiry by menstruation, interpreting the speech of the sane Muslim woman as correct when possible. End.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq is a commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq, addressing the chapter of establishing lineage (nasab) after divorce. The passage type is a ruling with proof, expanding on the matn's statement about the bā'in (irrevocably divorced) woman. The author explains that if a bā'in woman gives birth within less than two years from the time of divorce, the child's lineage is established from the husband. This is because we cannot be certain that the marital relationship (farash) has ended at the time of divorce; it is possible that the pregnancy existed before the divorce. The ruling is based on precaution (ihtiyat) to preserve lineage. The text then contrasts with the raj'ī (revocably divorced) woman, whose iddah ends with three menstrual cycles, but here the focus is on the bā'in. A key condition is that the woman has not admitted that her iddah has expired. If she admits it, the ruling changes: if she admits explicitly by months (because she claims menopause) and then gives birth within two years, the admission is disregarded because the birth proves she was not menopausal, so lineage is still established. If she admits absolutely, then if she gives birth within six months of the admission, lineage is established (certainty of pregnancy at admission), otherwise not. The text also addresses the case of exactly two years: some scholars view it as still within the maximum pregnancy period and thus lineage is established, while others differ. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that two years is like the greater period, so lineage is established. The authority weight is from Zahir al-Riwayah as the text cites al-Bada'i and other core works.

Key Terms
البائن (al-bā'in): The irrevocably divorced woman whose husband cannot take her back except with a new marriage and new dower.
الفراش (al-farāsh): The marital bed or relationship; used here as the basis for establishing lineage (al-walad li al-firāsh).
العلوق (al-talūq): The attachment or conception; the time when the pregnancy began.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A bā'in woman admits her iddah has expired absolutely (without mentioning months). She gives birth 5 months later. According to the Hanafi ruling in this text, the child's lineage is:
A) Not established because she admitted iddah expiry.
B) Established because she gave birth within six months of the admission.
C) Established only if the ex-husband agrees.
D) Not established because the birth is after three months of iddah.

Key Takeaway
A bā'in woman's admission of iddah expiry can block lineage unless the birth occurs within six months (absolute admission) or unless the admission is specifically by months and contradicted by birth within two years.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
Maximum Pregnancy Period and Lineage of Twins After Divorce
Ruling on maximum pregnancy and twins' lineage after divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the maximum pregnancy period and apply the intra-madhhab disagreement to determine lineage for twins born after divorce.

Core Idea
The maximum pregnancy in Hanafi fiqh is two years; twins born within that span are both attributed to the husband.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the Hanafi position that the maximum pregnancy period is two years and clarifies the dispute about twins born after a bā'in divorce. The author presents a strong analogy (qiyas) with a sold slave girl to support the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf that both twins are attributed to the ex-husband, even if one is born after two years. Understanding this ruling is crucial for correctly assigning paternity and inheritance rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maximum pregnancy period
The text states that the maximum period of pregnancy is two years. Note that the Hanafi school includes exactly two years as within this maximum, so lineage is established for a child born at two years.
This is based on hadith and the practice of the early scholars, though the text focuses on the legal application.
2
Understand the dispute about twins
If a divorced woman gives birth to twins, one within two years and the other after two years, the Hanafi masters differ: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf attribute both to the husband; Muhammad attributes only the first.
The dispute hinges on whether the second twin could be from the same conception as the first.
3
Analyze the qiyas (analogical reasoning)
The text provides an analogy from a slave girl sold: if she later gives birth to twins and the seller claims the first, both are attributed to him. Similarly, in divorce, if she has twins, both can be attributed to the ex-husband.
The common 'illa' (effective cause) is that both children could have been conceived from a single act of intercourse during the marriage.
4
Evaluate the opposing view and the response
Muhammad argues that the second twin must be from a later conception, which implies the first is also post-divorce. The majority responds that the qiyas holds because it is possible that both were conceived before divorce, and possibility is sufficient for lineage.
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that if both are attributed, they inherit; if only one, the second has no lineage.
5
Apply the ruling to related cases
The text also discusses partial birth (part within two years, part after) and the necessary compensation, as well as the iddah ending. However, the core lesson is the twins' case.
For partial birth, there is liability only if the part emerging within two years is at least half the body, measured by a valuable part like the head or both legs.

الحكم كذلك. أو نقول : يمكن أن يفرق بينهما بأن البائع التزمه قصداً بالدعوة والزوج لم يدع حتى لو أدعى الزوج الأول كان مثله، ولو خرج بعضه لأقل من سنتين وباقية لأكثر من سنتين لا يلزمه حتى يكون الخارج لأقل من سنتين نصف بدنة أو يخرج من قبل الرجلين أكثر البدن لأقل والباقي لأكثر ؛ ذكره محمد ولم يذكر المصنف رحمه الله أن عدتها انقضت بوضع الحمل أو قبله. قالوا: فيما إذا ولدته لأكثر يحكم بانقضاء عدتها قبل ولادتها بستة أشهر عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد فيجب أن ترد نفقة ستة أشهر حملاً على أنه من غيره بنكاح صحيح وأقل مدة الحمل ستة أشهر فقد أخذت مالاً لا تستحقه في هذه الستة أشهر فترده. وقال أبو يوسف: لا تنقضي إلا بوضع الحمل بدليل جواز عدم تزوجها بالغير قبل وضعه فيحمل على الوطء من قوله : (وأما إذا أتت به لتمام السنتين (فمشكل) قال في النهر : وأما إذا جاءت به لتمام سنتين فعدم ثبوته منه كما هو ظاهر كلامه مخالف لما العبارة مقلوبة وحقها بدليل عدم جواز تزوجها
The maximum period of pregnancy is two years, and they attached the two years to the lesser of them, so that they established lineage if she brings him at exactly two years. And the answer is by distinction: for in the case of the permanently separated woman, if she brings him at two years from the time of divorce, if we established lineage from him, then the attachment would have to be before the divorce so that intercourse becomes permissible, then the child would have been in his mother's womb for more than two years, and in the hadith, the child does not stay more than two years in his mother's womb, unlike the non-permanently separated woman due to the permissibility of intercourse after divorce. And the author did not mention in the case of the permanently separated woman the condition that he mentioned in the revocably divorced woman, namely not admitting the expiry of her iddah, although it is a condition in both, as stated in al-Bada'i. And his statement 'otherwise no' is qualified by the case where she did not give birth to a child before him within less than two years and between them is less than six months, such that if she gives birth to twins, one within less than two years and the other at more than them, their lineage is established from him according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, like the slave girl who gives birth to two children after her sale, then the seller claims the first, their lineage is established from him because they were created from... And Muhammad said: Their lineage is not established because the second is from a new attachment, and by necessity the first is also like that, unlike the case of the slave girl because it is possible that the first attached while he was in his ownership due to the possibility, so even if she gives birth to one within less than two years and the other at more, the ruling should be similar. Or we can say: A distinction can be made between them in that the seller undertook it intentionally by claim, and the husband did not claim, until if the husband claimed the first, it would be similar. And if part of it comes out within less than two years and the rest at more than two years, he is not liable unless the part coming out within less than two years is half a badanah [sacrificial animal] or the man's part [of the child] is more of the body within less and the rest at more; mentioned by Muhammad. And the author (may Allah have mercy on him) did not mention whether her iddah ends by giving birth or before it. They said: In the case where she gives birth at more [than two years], it is ruled that her iddah ends before her birth by six months according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, so she must return the maintenance of six months, on the basis that it is from someone else by valid marriage, and the minimum period of pregnancy is six months, so she took money she does not deserve in these six months, so she returns it. And Abu Yusuf said: It does not end except by giving birth, based on the permissibility of her not marrying someone else before the birth, so it is interpreted as from intercourse. From his statement: (And as for if she brings him at exactly two years, it is problematic) He said in al-Nahr: And as for if she brings him at exactly two years, his non-establishment from him as apparent from his statement contradicts what will come that the maximum period of pregnancy is two years, and according to the narration of al-Idah, al-Isbijabi, and al-Aqta', it is established if she brings him at two years, and therefore the commentator decided to interpret his statement as the first. His statement: (Then it would necessitate the child being in his mother's womb for more than two years) He said in al-Nahr: It is denied by interpreting the attachment at the time of divorce because then it is before the removal of the marital bed, as Qadikhan stated, and it is good. And in al-Jawhara, the statement of al-Quduri that lineage is not established when she brings him at two years is a mistake, and what is mentioned in other books is that it is established, and the correct view is to interpret it as a difference of narrations, because the texts concur on its non-establishment as al-Quduri said, since the author followed that here and in al-Wafi, and likewise Sadr al-Shari'ah and the author of al-Majma', and they are more knowledgeable of the narration. His statement: (Based on the permissibility of her not marrying) the wording is inverted; its correct form is: based on the impermissibility of her marrying...
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of lineage after divorce, now focusing on the maximum pregnancy period of two years and a dispute about exactly two years. The author clarifies that the relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that two years is included in the maximum, so lineage is established. The text then addresses a specific case: if a divorced woman gives birth to twins, one within two years and the other after two years, what is the ruling? According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, both twins' lineage is established from the husband, analogizing to the case of a slave woman sold who later gives birth to twins, where the seller's claim establishes lineage for both. Muhammad disagrees, arguing that the second twin must be from a later conception, which necessarily implies the first is also post-divorce. The text then discusses the scholarly disagreement on when a bā'in woman's iddah ends if she gives birth after two years: according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the iddah is deemed to have ended six months before birth (the minimum pregnancy period), so she must return maintenance for that period; Abu Yusuf says iddah ends only with the birth itself. The passage ends with a correction of a wording error in the matn about marriage permissibility during iddah. The authority weight is a mix of Zahir al-Riwayah (from the core texts) and later tarjih (like al-Nahr, al-Jawhara). The passage type is a ruling with proof, as it provides rational and textual arguments.

Key Terms
التوأمان (al-taw'āmān): Twins; two children born from the same pregnancy.
البدنة (al-badanah): A large sacrificial animal (camel or cow) used as expiation in certain cases.
العدة (al-ʿiddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of her husband before remarrying.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a divorced woman (bā'in) gives birth to twins, one at 1 year and 11 months after divorce, and the other at 2 years and 1 month, what is the ruling on the lineage of the second twin?
A) Lineage is not established because it was born after two years.
B) Lineage is established because the maximum period is two years and it is not certain it was conceived after divorce.
C) Lineage is established only if the father claims it.
D) Lineage is not established because the second twin must be from a new conception.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the maximum pregnancy period is two years, and twins born within that span are both attributed to the husband even if one is born after two years, based on the possibility of a single conception before divorce.

Inheritance and Nafaqa in Marad al-Mawt Divorce: Abu Hanifa vs. Abu Yusuf
Intra-Hanafi dispute on inheritance and nafaqa return.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad versus Abu Yusuf on inheritance and nafaqa return in a specific marad al-mawt divorce case, and explain the basis of the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Core Idea
A divorced wife's inheritance depends on whether her iddah ended before the husband's death, even in marad al-mawt.

The Hanafi school holds that a divorced woman may inherit only if the divorce occurred during the husband's fatal illness (marad al-mawt) and she is still in her iddah at his death. But what if the iddah is unusually long? In a case where the husband is terminally ill, divorces his wife, both the illness and the iddah drag on for two years, and then he dies, the wife giving birth a month later, the imams differ on whether she inherits and whether she must return nafaqa. This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq records the dispute and invites us to examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the facts of the case
Restate the scenario: a husband divorces his wife while suffering from a fatal illness. His illness continues for two years, and her 'iddah also lasts two years. He then dies. One month after his death, she gives birth. He had already paid her nafaqa up to his death.
The two-year period is unusual; it may indicate a woman whose 'iddah is prolonged (e.g., a pregnant woman or one with irregular cycles).
2
State the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the wife does not inherit from her deceased husband, and she must return five months' worth of the nafaqa she received during the 'iddah.
Five months likely corresponds to the period after her 'iddah is deemed to have ended before his death.
3
State the position of Abu Yusuf
Abu Yusuf holds that she does inherit from her husband and does not have to return any nafaqa.
He considers her to have been in 'iddah at the time of death, thus she is a widow entitled to inheritance and deserving of nafaqa.
4
Analyze the basis of the ikhtilaf
The key point of divergence is whether her 'iddah had already ended before the husband's death. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad apparently conclude that the 'iddah ended earlier (perhaps because the two-year maximum elapsed, or because the pregnancy was not linked to the divorce). Abu Yusuf argues that the birth a month after death proves the 'iddah was still ongoing (since 'iddah for a pregnant woman ends only at delivery).
The text implies that the wife was pregnant at the time of divorce? But the birth after death suggests conception after divorce. This ambiguity is part of the dispute.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the wife loses both inheritance and part of her nafaqa. Under Abu Yusuf, she gains inheritance and keeps all nafaqa. This affects the financial rights of the woman and the heirs.
The mufta bihi position is not specified in this text; later scholars may have preferred one view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكر القاضي الاسبيجابي وكذلك إذا طلق الرجل امرأته في حال المرض فامتد مرضه إلى سنتين وامتدت عدتها إلى سنتين ثم مات ثم ولدت المرأة بعد الموت بشهر وقد كان أعطاها النفقة إلى وقت الوفاة فإنها لا ترثه ويسترد منها نفقة خمسة أشهر عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد : قاله. وقال أبو يوسف ترث ولا يسترد منها شيئاً .اهـ.
Al-Qadi al-Isbijabi mentioned: Likewise, if a man divorces his wife while he is ill, and his illness extends to two years, and her 'iddah extends to two years, then he dies, and then the woman gives birth a month after his death, and he had given her the nafaqa up to the time of death, then she does not inherit from him, and five months' worth of nafaqa is taken back from her according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. He said: And Abu Yusuf said: She inherits and nothing is taken back from her. End.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning a specific case: a man divorces his wife while terminally ill, and both the illness and the 'iddah last two years. The husband gives her nafaqa until his death. After his death, she gives birth within a month. The question is whether she inherits from him and whether she must return some nafaqa. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, she does not inherit (because the divorce occurred during marriage but the death after 'iddah expiration?), and she must return nafaqa for five months because the 'iddah likely ended before his death, so the nafaqa after that was undeserved. Abu Yusuf holds that she does inherit and does not return any nafaqa. The reliance (mufta bihi) on this issue may vary; the text leaves it unresolved.

Key Terms
نفقة (nafaqah): Maintenance provided by the husband to the wife during marriage and 'iddah.
ميراث (mirath): Inheritance; the right of a widow to inherit from her deceased husband if the marriage was valid at the time of death.
عدة (iddah): The waiting period after divorce or death before a woman can remarry, which affects financial rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) She inherits and keeps all nafaqa.
B) She does not inherit and must return five months' nafaqa.
C) She inherits but returns five months' nafaqa.
D) She does not inherit but keeps all nafaqa.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a divorced wife's inheritance and nafaqa rights in marad al-mawt hinge on whether her iddah has ended before the husband's death, leading to a legitimate intra-madhhab difference between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf.

Paternity for Irrevocably Divorced Women: Marriage During Iddah and the Husband's Claim
Rulings on paternity for irrevocably divorced women, including marriage during 'iddah and the exception of the husband's claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on paternity for a child born to an irrevocably divorced woman after two years and identify the exception of the husband's claim based on intercourse with shubha.

Core Idea
A husband can claim paternity of a child born after two years if he had intercourse with shubha during iddah.

This passage delves into the complexities of paternity for women who have received an irrevocable divorce. It covers scenarios where the woman marries another during her iddah, and the critical exception of the husband's claim even after the normal timeframe. Understanding these rulings is essential for family law practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify general rule for paternity after irrevocable divorce
Determine the standard timeframes: within two years from divorce if no remarriage, and within six months from a valid second marriage.
2
Recognize scenario of marriage during iddah
Analyze the case from al-Khaniyya where the woman marries another during iddah and the child's paternity depends on birth timings relative to both marriages.
3
Analyze husband's claim exception
Note that even if the child is born after two years, the ex-husband can claim paternity if he had intercourse with shubha during iddah.
4
Trace intra-Hanafi dispute
Follow the criticism from al-Tabyin that shubha fi al-fil does not establish paternity, and the response distinguishing pure shubha from non-pure shubha.
5
Determine mufta bihi position
Conclude that the relied-upon view is that with a claim of shubha, paternity is established, as per al-Nahr and al-Fath.

أشهر من وقت النكاح ولم يثبت من الأول ولا من الثاني فإن النكاح صحيح خلافاً لأبي يوسف بناء على تزوج الحامل من الزنا. هذا إذا لم يعلم أنها كانت معتدة وقت النكاح، فإن علم وقع الثاني فاسداً فإن جاءت بولد فإن النسب يثبت من الأول إن أمكن إثبات منه بأن جاءت به لأقل من سنتين منذ طلقها الأول أو مات ولستة أشهر فأكثر منذ تزوجها الثاني، فإن جاءت به لأكثر من سنتين من وقت الطلاق ولستة أشهر من وقت التزوج فهو للثاني؛ كذا في البدائع قوله : (إلا) أن يدعيه استثناء من النفي يعني إذا جاءت به المبتوتة لأكثر وادعاء الزوج يثبت نسبه منه لأنه التزمه وله وجه بأن وطئها بشبهة في العدة؛ كذا في الهداية وغيرها. وتعقبه في
