And he made absolute regarding the irrevocable (al-batt) so it includes one and three [divorces] as in al-Bada'i, and includes the free woman and the slave woman, but on condition that he does not own her after the divorce. So if he married a slave woman, then consummated, then divorced her once, then [later] owned her, her child is binding upon him if she gives birth at less than [the time of] ownership, and not binding if she gives birth at six months or more as will come at the end of the chapter. And know that the establishment of paternity regarding what is mentioned of the child of the revocably and irrevocably divorced woman is restricted by what will come of testimony of birth or the husband's acknowledgment of pregnancy or apparent pregnancy. And in al-Khaniyya: The woman in 'iddah from an irrevocable divorce, if she marries another husband during the 'iddah and later gives birth: if she gives birth at less than two years from the time of the first divorce and less than six months from the time of the second marriage, the child belongs to the first; if she gives birth at more than two years from the first divorce, it is not binding on the first; then it is considered: if she gives birth at six months from the second marriage, the child belongs to the second, otherwise not. End. By this it is known that what is in al-Mukhtasar includes the case where the irrevocably divorced woman marries during 'iddah or not, and it is not clarified in al-Khaniyya regarding when she brings it at less than the time of the first divorce and six months from the time of the second marriage. And in al-Bada'i: It is for the second, and the marriage is valid because her proceeding to marry indicates the end of her 'iddah from the first. Likewise, if she brings it at more than the time of divorce and less than six months from the time of marriage, and it is not established from the first nor the second, then the marriage is sound, contrary to Abu Yusuf based on the marriage of a pregnant woman from fornication. This is when it is not known that she was in 'iddah at the time of marriage; if it is known, the second marriage is invalid (fasid). If she brings a child, then paternity is established from the first if possible: if she brings it at less than two years since he divorced her or died, and at six months or more since she married the second. If she brings it at more than two years from the divorce and six months from the marriage, it is for the second; as in al-Bada'i. His saying: (Except if he claims it) is an exception from the negation, meaning if the irrevocably divorced woman brings it at more [than two years] and the husband claims it, paternity from him is established because he accepted it, and it has a justification that he had intercourse with her by shubha during the 'iddah; as in al-Hidaya and others. And it was criticized in al-Tabyin that for the irrevocably divorced woman with three divorces, if the husband has intercourse with her by shubha, it is shubha in the act, and in that case paternity is not established even if he claims it, as stated in Kitab al-Hudud; so how can paternity be established here? End. And the answer is to concede that shubha in the act does not establish paternity when it is purely [shubha fi al-fil], otherwise it does, as in the case of the woman divorced thrice or on payment, where paternity is not established by claim because the shubha in them is not... His saying: (And the answer is to concede... etc.) He said in al-Nahr after mentioning this answer: And what is in al-Fath mentioned there is when he does not claim shubha, and what is mentioned here is to be interpreted as intercourse with shubha, and for the unrelated woman (ajnabiyya), paternity is established.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage covers several rulings and disputes regarding paternity for irrevocably divorced women (mabtutah). It begins by stating that the rules apply to both one and three divorces, and to free and slave women, provided the husband does not come to own her after divorce (if he later owns her, the child is his only if born within a short period). Paternity is conditional on evidence: testimony of birth, husband's acknowledgment, or visible pregnancy. The key section from al-Khaniyya addresses a woman who marries another during her 'iddah: if the child is born within two years from the first divorce and less than six months from the second marriage, it belongs to the first husband; if after two years, it does not; if exactly at six months from the second marriage, it belongs to the second. If the child is born more than two years from the first but less than six months from the second, paternity is unclear. Al-Bada'i clarifies that if she marries during 'iddah, the marriage is valid only if her 'iddah had likely ended; otherwise it is invalid. There is a dispute with Abu Yusuf regarding the validity of marriage during 'iddah. Finally, an exception is discussed: if the husband claims paternity after two years, it may still be established if he had intercourse with shubha (semblance) during the 'iddah. The commentator (in al-Tabyin) criticizes this, arguing that shubha fi al-fil does not establish paternity even if claimed. The response is that this is only when the shubha is purely in the act; otherwise paternity can be established. The passage ends with a note from al-Nahr confirming that paternity is established for the unrelated woman in some cases.

Key Terms
بت (batt): Irrevocable divorce (al-batt), which includes one or three pronouncements that make the divorce final.
شبهة (shubha): A semblance or doubt that makes an otherwise forbidden act acceptable; here, intercourse with shubha during 'iddah may establish paternity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Paternity for an irrevocably divorced woman's child can be established even after two years if the husband claims it based on intercourse with shubha during the iddah.

Divorce in Fatal Illness: Inheritance and Maintenance after Posthumous Birth
States a specific ruling and the intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad on inheritance and maintenance repayment in the scenario of divorce during fatal illness with post-mortem childbirth, and distinguish the position of Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Post-mortem childbirth after divorce in fatal illness: inheritance denied and maintenance recovered per Abu Hanifa.

This lesson examines a specific intra-Hanafi dispute about the financial consequences of a divorce pronounced during the husband's fatal illness, when the husband dies after an extended period and the wife gives birth after his death. The core question: does the wife inherit and must she repay maintenance received? Understanding this ruling clarifies the intersection of divorce, inheritance, and maintenance in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
Identify the key facts: divorce during fatal illness, illness lasts two years, wife's 'iddah extends two years, husband dies, wife gives birth one month after death, and she had received maintenance up to death.
The 'iddah of a divorced woman is normally three menstrual cycles or until childbirth if pregnant; here it extends due to pregnancy? Actually, the text says her 'iddah also lasted two years, then she gives birth after death.
2
Identify the main ruling (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad)
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: the wife does not inherit from the husband and must repay five months of maintenance from the estate.
The five months likely refers to the period between divorce and his death minus the first month? Not specified but given.
3
Identify the intra-Hanafi variant (Abu Yusuf)
Abu Yusuf holds that she does inherit and does not repay any maintenance.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.
4
Analyse the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Determine the financial impact: under Abu Hanifa, the estate recovers five months' maintenance and the wife gets no inheritance; under Abu Yusuf, the wife inherits and keeps the maintenance.
This affects the distribution of the estate.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
The text implies the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad is the relied-upon position, likely because it is more cautious and widely accepted in later fatwa.
The author does not explicitly tarjih but follows the majority of the Sahibayn.

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a specific ruling on divorce during fatal illness, as reported by al-Isbījābī. The ruling: if a man divorces his wife while terminally ill, the illness itself lasts two years, the wife's 'iddah is two years, then he dies, and she gives birth a month after his death (so within the 'iddah), then according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the wife does not inherit from him (because the divorce prevents inheritance if he died during the 'iddah? The reasoning is likely that the birth of the child after death indicates that the 'iddah had ended with the death, so no inheritance. Abu Yusuf holds she inherits and does not repay. Then the commentary expands: the term 'al-batt' (absolute divorce) in the ruling includes both one and three divorces, and applies to free and slave women, provided he does not own her after divorce. If he divorces a slave woman, then owns her, the child's paternity depends on timing relative to ownership. This is an intra-Hanafi dispute with Abu Hanifa and Muhammad on one side and Abu Yusuf on the other. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, as it is the view of the majority.

Key Terms
البت (al-batt): Absolute divorce, which makes the woman irrevocably separated (bā'in). It includes both one and three divorces.
النفقة (al-nafaqah): Maintenance paid by the husband to the wife during 'iddah.

Conditions and Timing for Establishing Paternity in Divorced Women
States general conditions for paternity and timing rules from al-Khaniyah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three general conditions for establishing paternity and apply the timing rules to determine which husband is the father when a divorced woman gives birth after remarriage during 'iddah.

Core Idea
Paternity after divorce depends on timing: within two years from divorce or six months from remarriage.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq lays the groundwork for establishing paternity after divorce. It first lists the necessary conditions—testimony, acknowledgment, or visible pregnancy—then presents a detailed scenario from al-Khaniyah showing how the timing of birth relative to divorce and remarriage determines the father. Understanding these rules is essential for both family law and inheritance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three general conditions for paternity
The text states that paternity depends on either testimony of childbirth, the husband's acknowledgment of pregnancy, or visible pregnancy. These are the prerequisites for any attribution.
These are conditions for considering paternity, not for the validity of the marriage.
2
Understand the maximum and minimum pregnancy periods
The Hanafi school sets the maximum pregnancy at two years and the minimum at six months. These are used to determine whether a child can be attributed to a particular husband.
Six months is the shortest possible viable pregnancy.
3
Apply the timing rules to the al-Khaniyah scenario
If the woman gives birth within two years of the first divorce and within six months of the second marriage, the child is the first husband's. If more than two years from the first, it cannot be his. If exactly six months from the second marriage, it is the second husband's.
The scenario assumes the woman remarried during 'iddah.
4
Identify cases where paternity is not established
If the birth occurs after two years from the first and not at six months from the second, the child is attributed to no one (neither husband).
This is the default when timing does not match any window.
5
Recognize the implication for marriage validity
The woman's act of remarriage is taken as evidence that she believed her 'iddah had ended, so the second marriage is considered valid unless it was known she was still in 'iddah.
This introduces the concept of shubhah (legal doubt).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن ثبوت النسب فيما ذكر من ولد المطلقة الرجعية والبائنة مقيد بما سيأتي من الشهادة بالولادة أو اعتراف من الزوج بالحبل أو حبل ظاهر. وفي الخانية : المعتدة طلاق عن بائن إذا تزوجت بزوج آخر في العدة وولدت بعد ذلك إن ولدت لأقل من سنتين من وقت طلاق الأول ولأقل من ستة أشهر من وقت نكاح الثاني كان الولد للأول، وإن ولدت لأكثر من سنتين من وقت طلاق الأول لا يلزم الأول، ثم ينظر ؛ إن ولدت لستة أشهر من وقت نكاح الثاني فالولد للثاني وإلا فلا .اهـ وبه علم أن ما في المختصر شامل لما إذا تزوجت المبتوتة في العدة أو لم تتزوج ولم يبين في الخانية فيما إذا أتت به لأقل من وقت طلاق الأول ولستة أشهر من وقت نكاح الثاني. وفي البدائع : إنه للثاني والنكاح جائز لأن إقدامها على التزوج دليل انقضاء عدتها من الأول، وكذلك إذا أتت به للأكثر من وقت الطلاق ولأقل من ستة أشهر من وقت النكاح ولم يثبت من الأول ولا من الثاني فإن النكاح صحيح خلافاً لأبي يوسف بناء على تزوج الحامل من الزنا. هذا إذا لم يعلم أنها كانت معتدة وقت النكاح، فإن علم وقع الثاني فاسداً فإن جاءت بولد فإن النسب يثبت من الأول إن أمكن إثبات منه بأن جاءت به لأقل من سنتين منذ طلقها الأول أو مات ولستة أشهر فأكثر منذ تزوجها الثاني، فإن جاءت به لأكثر من سنتين من وقت الطلاق ولستة أشهر من وقت التزوج فهو للثاني؛ كذا في البدائع
Know that the establishment of lineage regarding the child of a revocably and irrevocably divorced woman is conditioned by what will come: testimony of childbirth or the husband's acknowledgment of pregnancy or apparent pregnancy. In al-Khaniyah: The woman observing 'iddah due to an irrevocable divorce, if she marries another husband during the 'iddah and gives birth after that: if she gives birth before two years from the time of the first divorce and before six months from the time of the second marriage, the child belongs to the first husband. If she gives birth after more than two years from the time of the first divorce, it is not attributed to the first husband. Then it is considered: if she gives birth at six months from the time of the second marriage, the child belongs to the second husband; otherwise, not. End. And by this it is known that what is in al-Mukhtasar includes the case where the irrevocably divorced woman marries during the 'iddah or does not marry. It is not clarified in al-Khaniyah regarding the case where she gives birth before the end of the first husband's time and at six months from the second husband's marriage. In al-Bada'i': It belongs to the second husband and the marriage is valid because her action of marrying indicates the end of her 'iddah from the first. Similarly, if she gives birth after the maximum time of divorce and before six months from the marriage, and it is not established from the first nor from the second, then the marriage is valid contrary to Abu Yusuf, based on the marriage of a pregnant woman from adultery. This is if it is not known that she was in 'iddah at the time of marriage. If it is known, the second marriage is invalid. Then if she gives birth, paternity is established from the first if possible: that she gives birth before two years from the time he divorced her or died and at six months or more from the time she married the second. If she gives birth after more than two years from the time of divorce and at six months from the time of marriage, it is for the second. Thus in al-Bada'i'.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage explains the conditions for establishing paternity of a child born to a divorced woman (revocable or irrevocable). The key conditions are: testimony of birth, or the husband's admission of pregnancy, or visible pregnancy. Then it presents a detailed scenario from al-Khaniyah: if a woman in an irrevocable 'iddah marries another man while still in 'iddah and gives birth, paternity is determined by timing. If birth occurs within less than two years from the first divorce AND less than six months from the second marriage, the child belongs to the first husband. If more than two years from first divorce, it is not the first's. If exactly six months from second marriage, it is the second's. The commentary clarifies that al-Mukhtasar includes both cases of remarriage and no remarriage. Then al-Bada'i adds: if birth occurs at less than the two-year maximum from first husband but at six months from second, it is the second's. If it occurs after the two-year maximum from first but before six months from second, the marriage is valid (contrary to Abu Yusuf who would invalidate it). Finally, if the second marriage is known to have occurred during 'iddah, it is invalid, and paternity reverts to the first if the timing allows (within two years from divorce or death of first, and at least six months from second marriage). This is an intra-Hanafi dispute, with Abu Yusuf differing in some details regarding the validity of the second marriage. The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the Sahibayn).

Key Terms
المبتوتة (al-mabtūtah): A woman who has been irrevocably divorced (bā'in), whether by one or three divorces.
شبهة (shubhah): Appearance of legality that affects rulings; here, the husband's intercourse during 'iddah may create shubhah for paternity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the maximum period after divorce within which a child can be attributed to the first husband?
A) Six months.
B) One year.
C) Two years.
D) Three years.

Key Takeaway
Paternity after divorce is established through one of three proofs, and the timing of birth relative to divorce and any subsequent marriage determines attribution.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Second Marriage During 'Iddah and Paternity
Intra-Hanafi dispute on validity of second marriage during 'iddah when timing is ambiguous.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf regarding the validity of a second marriage performed during 'iddah when the woman's condition was unknown, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
A woman's action of remarriage is taken as evidence of her 'iddah's end, making the marriage valid unless her ongoing 'iddah was known.

This passage from al-Bada'i' presents a specific intra-Hanafi dispute. When a woman in 'iddah from an irrevocable divorce marries another man and later gives birth at a time that does not clearly match either husband, the validity of that second marriage becomes contested. Abu Yusuf invalidates it by analogy to the prohibition of marrying a pregnant adulteress, but the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) uphold it based on the woman's own reckoning. Understanding this ikhtilaf reveals how the Hanafi school balances textual analogy with practical considerations of certainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario in dispute
The woman was irrevocably divorced, entered 'iddah, remarried during what she thought was after 'iddah, but actually was still in 'iddah. She gives birth at a time that exceeds the two-year window from the first husband but is less than six months from the second marriage, making paternity unclear.
This is the case explicitly mentioned in al-Bada'i'.
2
Understand Abu Yusuf's position and his proof
Abu Yusuf analogizes this to a woman known to be pregnant from adultery: marrying her is invalid. He extends this analogy to the woman in 'iddah because the uncertainty is similar—the marriage may be to a woman carrying another man's child.
He uses qiyas to the prohibition of marrying a pregnant adulteress.
3
Analyze the Hanafi majority response
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad reject the analogy. They argue that the woman's action of remarriage demonstrates her belief that her 'iddah had ended, creating a valid excuse (shubhah). This shubhah prevents the analogy to the clear case of adultery.
The key distinction is knowledge: here the woman genuinely believed her 'iddah ended.
4
Recognize the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Yusuf's view, the second marriage is invalid (fasid), and if the birth occurs after two years from the first and before six months from the second, the child would be unattributed and the marriage void. Under the majority, the marriage is valid, and the child could be attributed to the second husband if born exactly at six months.
This affects lineage, inheritance, and marital rights.
5
Identify the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: the marriage is valid, and the child is attributed accordingly. This is supported by al-Bada'i' and later Hanafi works.
The majority view prevails because it aligns with the principle that a person's actions are interpreted as valid when possible.

The Shubhah Distinction: When a Husband's Claim Establishes Paternity After Maximum Period
Discusses the exception and the shubhah distinction in establishing paternity by claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between shubhah in the act alone and shubhah combined with a contract, and apply this distinction to determine when the husband's claim establishes paternity for a child born after the maximum period.

Core Idea
Shubhah in the act alone does not establish paternity; it must be combined with shubhah of a contract, like a prior marriage.

In the previous lesson, we learned that a child born to an irrevocably divorced woman after the maximum period (usually two years) is not attributed to the husband. However, there is an exception: if the husband claims the child, paternity is established. But on what basis? Al-Hidayah states that it is because he may have had intercourse with her under shubhah during the 'iddah. Al-Tabyīn objects: in the Book of Hudud, it says that shubhah in the act alone does not establish paternity. How can it here? The answer reveals a crucial distinction: the shubhah must involve not just the act but also the semblance of a valid contract. Let us explore this critical refinement.

Guided Path
1
1. State the General Rule and Exception
Recall that if an irrevocably divorced woman gives birth after the maximum period, the child is not attributed to the ex-husband. However, if he claims it, paternity is established. This is the exception clause 'إلا أن يدعيه'.
2
2. Understand al-Tabyīn's Objection
Al-Tabyīn argues that shubhah in the act alone (such as mistakenly thinking she is his wife) does not establish paternity, as ruled in the Book of Hudud. So how can it here? This objection challenges the reasoning in al-Hidayah.
3
3. Analyze the Hanafi Reconciliation
The author replies that the shubhah here is not pure (متمحضة) in the act; it is combined with shubhah of contract (شبهة عقد) because there was a prior valid marriage. The prior marriage creates a semblance of a valid relationship, which combined with the act establishes paternity.
4
4. Apply the Distinction to Scenarios
Contrast three cases: (a) A woman divorced by three irrevocable divorces – shubhah includes both act and contract, so claim establishes paternity. (b) A woman divorced in exchange for compensation (khul') – similarly, shubhah of contract exists. (c) A complete stranger mistakenly believed to be his wife – shubhah is only in the act, so no paternity by claim.
5
5. Conclusion and Wider Application
The passage also notes that this ruling extends to all forms of separation (not just divorce). The key: paternity by claim requires shubhah that includes a contractual element, such as a prior marriage, making the intercourse potentially lawful in appearance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (إلا) أن يدعيه استثناء من النفي يعني إذا جاءت به المبتوتة لأكثر وادعاء الزوج يثبت نسبه منه لأنه التزمه وله وجه بأن وطئها بشبهة في العدة؛ كذا في الهداية وغيرها. وتعقبه في التبيين بأن المبتوتة بالثلاث إذا وطئها الزوج بشبهة كانت شبهة في الفعل وفيها لا يثبت النسب وإن ادعاه نص عليه في كتاب الحدود فكيف أثبت به النسب هنا اهـ وجوابه تسليم أن شبهة الفعل لا يثبت النسب فيها وإن ادعاه إذا كانت متمحضة وإلا فلا كما في المطلقة ثلاثاً أو على مال فإنه لا يثبت النسب فيهما بالدعوة لأن الشبهة فيهما لم تتحض للفعل بل هي شبهة عقد أيضاً فلا يكون بين النصين تناقض، وهذا أولى من حمل بعضهم المذكور هنا على المبانة بالكنايات فإن الشبهة فيها شبهة المحل، وأما المطلقة ثلاثاً أو على مال فلا يثبت فيها النسب بالدعوة لأن المنصوص عليه هنا أعم من المبتوتة بالكنايات أو بالثلاث أو على مال وقد صرح ابن الملك في شرح المجمع أن من وطىء امرأة أجنبية زفت إليه وقيل له إنها امرأتك فهي شبهة في الفعل وأن النسب يثبت إذا ادعاه، فعلم أنه ليس كل شبهة في الفعل تمنع دعوى النسب وأطلق في المختصر فأفاد أنه لا يشترط تصديق المرأة وفيه روايتان كما في البدائع، والأوجه أنه لا يشترط لأنه ممكن منه وقد ادعاه ولا معارض ولذا لم يشترطه السرخسي والبيهقي فدل على ضعف رواية الاشتراط وغرابتها كغرابة ما نقله في المجتبى أن توقف ثبوت النسب فيما إذا جاءت به للأكثر على الدعوى إنما هو قول أبي يوسف، وأما عندهما فيثبت النسب بلا دعوة لاحتمال الوطء بشبهة في العدة اهـ. وفي البدائع : وكل جواب عرفته في المعتدة عن طلاق فهو الجواب في المعتدة من غير طلاق من أساب الفرقة اهـ.
His statement: '(unless) he claims it' is an exception from the negation, meaning that if the irrevocably divorced woman brings a child after the maximum period and the husband claims it, his paternity is established because he has undertaken it and it has a basis: that he had intercourse with her under shubhah during the 'iddah. This is in al-Hidayah and others. Al-Tabyīn objected: 'If a woman divorced by three revocable divorces is had intercourse with by the husband under shubhah, it is shubhah in the act, and in that case lineage is not established even if he claims it, as stated in the Book of Hudud. So how can lineage be established here by claim?' The answer is the admission that shubhah in the act does not establish lineage even if claimed when it is pure; otherwise it does establish, as in the case of a woman divorced three times or for compensation: lineage is not established in both by claim because the shubhah in them is not confined to the act but is also a shubhah of contract, so there is no contradiction between the two texts. This is better than the view of some who hold that what is mentioned here refers to separation by kināyāt, for the shubhah there is shubhah of the place. As for the woman divorced three times or for compensation, lineage is not established by claim because what is stated here is more general than the irrevocably divorced by kināyāt, or by three, or for compensation. Ibn al-Malik in Sharh al-Majma' stated that whoever has intercourse with a foreign woman who is presented to him and it is said to him that she is his wife, that is shubhah in the act, and lineage is established if he claims it. So it is known that not every shubhah in the act prevents a claim of lineage. And he (the author of al-Mukhtasar) used absolute terms, indicating that the woman's confirmation is not required. There are two narrations on this, as in al-Bada'i', and the correct view is that it is not required because he is capable of intercourse and has claimed it, and there is no opponent. For this reason, al-Sarakhsi and al-Bayhaqi did not require it, showing the weakness and strangeness of the narration requiring it, as strange as what is reported in al-Mujtabā that the condition of establishing lineage when the child is born after the maximum period upon claim is only the saying of Abu Yusuf; according to his two companions, lineage is established without a claim due to the possibility of intercourse under shubhah during the 'iddah. End. And in al-Bada'i': Every answer you know regarding the woman in 'iddah due to divorce is the same for the woman in 'iddah from other causes of separation. End.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hāshiyah) of the earlier ruling from al-Hidayah. It addresses the exception clause 'unless he claims it' (إلا أن يدعيه) in the context of a child born to an irrevocably divorced woman after the maximum period. According to al-Hidayah, if the husband claims the child, paternity is established because he has undertaken responsibility and there is a possibility that he had intercourse with her under shubhah during the 'iddah. However, al-Tabyīn raises an objection: In the Book of Hudud, it is stated that shubhah in the act alone does not establish paternity even if claimed. How can it be otherwise here? The response reconciles: If the shubhah is purely in the act (e.g., the woman is a complete stranger but mistakenly thought she was his wife), then paternity is not established. But if the shubhah also involves a semblance of a valid contract (as in the case of a woman divorced irrevocably where there was a prior valid marriage), then paternity is established by claim. This distinction resolves the apparent contradiction. The passage further discusses the requirement of the woman's confirmation of the claim. The majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) and later authorities like al-Sarakhsi and al-Bayhaqi hold that her confirmation is not required; his mere claim suffices when no one contests. There is a weak narration requiring her confirmation, and a strange report from al-Mujtabā that the need for a claim itself is only Abu Yusuf's view; according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), paternity is automatically established without claim due to the possibility of shubhah intercourse. The passage concludes by extending these rulings to all forms of separation, not just divorce.

Key Terms
شبهة الفعل (shubhah al-fi'l): Shubhah related to the act of intercourse, such as mistakenly thinking the woman is permissible.
شبهة العقد (shubhah al-'aqd): Shubhah related to the existence of a valid contract, e.g., a previous valid marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the reconciliation in the passage, what type of shubhah is present in the case of a woman divorced by three irrevocable divorces that allows the husband's claim to establish paternity?
A) Pure shubhah in the act.
B) Shubhah in the place (mahall) only.
C) Shubhah in both the act and the contract.
D) No shubhah at all.

Key Takeaway
A husband's claim to paternity for a child born after the maximum period is accepted by the Hanafi school because the shubhah includes both the act and the prior valid marriage contract, distinguishing it from cases of pure shubhah in the act alone.

Paternity of a Child Born to a Minor Divorcée: The 9-Month vs. 27-Month Dispute
States the intra-Hanafi dispute on the period for establishing paternity when a minor is divorced

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf on the paternity period for a minor divorcée, explain the reasoning behind each, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
A minor's 'iddah ends definitively at three months; birth after nine months means a new pregnancy.

When a minor girl (murāhiqah) is divorced and later gives birth, the paternity (nasab) of the child hinges on timing. This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq presents an intra-Hanafi ikhtilāf between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad on one side and Abu Yūsuf on the other. The divergence stems from whether the termination of the minor's 'iddah by months is a definitive legal end or subject to the possibility of prolonged gestation. Understanding this dispute clarifies how the Hanafi school prioritizes certain types of evidence (ḥukm shar‘ī) over others (iqrār) and how thamarah al-khilāf (practical consequence) directly affects the child's lineage and inheritance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling context
Recognize that the case involves a minor divorcée (murāhiqah) who does not menstruate. Her 'iddah is three months (Quran 65:4), not by menstruation. The husband had consummated the marriage. The question: within what period after divorce can a child be attributed to him?
This is a subcase of the general rules on nasab.
2
State the majority position (Abu Hanifa & Muhammad)
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold: if the child is born within 9 months of the divorce, paternity is established. If born at 9 months or later, it is not. This applies equally to raj'ī and bā'in ṭalāq.
The 9-month period is the normal maximum gestation.
3
State Abu Yūsuf's alternative
Abu Yūsuf argues for longer periods: 2 years for bā'in ṭalāq (like an adult woman) and 27 months for raj'ī ṭalāq (2 years plus 3 months 'iddah). He assumes the husband could have intercourse at the very end of the 'iddah, and then the child might be born after a prolonged pregnancy of up to 2 years.
27 months = 3 (months) + 24 (months).
4
Analyze the reasoning (talīl) of both sides
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad argue that the end of 'iddah by months is a definitive legal decree (ḥukm qaṭʿī), stronger than the woman's own admission. Once three months pass, the 'iddah is over conclusively. Any pregnancy appearing after that must begin after the 'iddah. Therefore, birth within 9 months of divorce shows conception occurred during 'iddah; otherwise it is a new pregnancy. Abu Yūsuf treats the possible gestation period as up to 2 years, and the 'iddah as a delay, not a definite end point.
The passage also notes a mistaken statement in al-Badā’i‘ and a reconciliation attempt.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (relied-upon position)
The author of al-Bahr al-Rā’iq supports Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, noting that their position is based on the definitive end of 'iddah. Therefore, the operative fatwa is the 9-month rule. The practical consequence: any child born after 9 months from divorce is not attributed to the ex-husband.
This means no inheritance, maintenance, or custody rights from that husband.

قوله : (والمراهقة لأقل من تسعة أشهر وإلا (لا أي ويثبت نسب ولد المطلقة المراهقة إذا أتت به لأقل من تسعة أشهر وقد كان دخل بها ولم تقر بانقضاء عدتها ولم تدع حبلاً، وإن جاءت به لتسعة أشهر فأكثر لا يثبت. وهذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد سواء، كان الطلاق رجعياً أو بائناً كما أطلقه المصنف. وقال أبو يوسف يثبت النسب إلى سنتين في الطلاق البائن كالكبيرة وإلى سبعة وعشرين شهراً في الطلاق الرجعي لأنه يجعل واطئاً في آخر العدة وهي الثلاثة الأشهر ثم تأتي به لأكثر مدة الحمل وهي سنتان. ولهما أن لانقضاء عدة الصغيرة جهة متعينة وهي الأشهر فبمضيها يحكم الشرع بالانقضاء وهو في الدلالة فوق إقرارها لأنه لا يحتمل الخلاف والإقرار يحتمله فإذا ولدت قبل مضي تسعة أشهر من وقت الطلاق تبين أن الحمل كان قبل انقضاء العدة، وإن ولدته لتسعة أشهر فأكثر فهو حمل حادث بعد انقضاء عدتها بالأشهر، وقد وقع في البدائع هذا غلط فاجتنبه فإنه قال : إذا لم تقر بوطئها يشبه فكيف بالمعتدة؟ فيجب الجمع مثلاً بأن يقال ينبغي أن يصرح بدعوى الشبهة المقبولة غير مجرد شبهة الفعل ثم قال : والوجه أن لا يشترط غير دعواه لأنه لم يشترط في الكتاب سواه ثم يحمل على مجرد الشبهة التي هي مجرد ظن الحل قوله : (كغرابة ما نقله في المجتبى الخ) لأنه قد أنه لا يثبت نسبه. إذا جاءت به لتمام السنتين للزوم أن يكون العلوق سابقاً على الطلاق فيلزم أن يكون مكث الولد أكثر من سنتين فكيف يثبت عندهما بلا دعوة إذا جاءت به للأكثر؟ قال . أقول : الظاهر أن حكمه بالغرابة مبني على أنه فهم من الأكثر أكثر من السنتين وهو غير متعين، بل المراد به أكثر مدة الحمل وهي السنتان وحينئذ يكون اختلاف عباراتهم مبنياً على اختلاف أبي يوسف صاحبيه ويرتفع التناقض فتأمل .اهـ. ويؤيده ما مر عن النهر من أن الحق حمله على اختلاف الروايتين .
His statement: '(and a minor girl, for less than nine months, otherwise not)' — meaning: The lineage of the child of a divorced minor girl is established if she brings it at less than nine months, provided he had intercourse with her and she did not acknowledge the end of her 'iddah nor claim pregnancy. If she brings it at nine months or more, it is not established. This according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, whether the divorce is revocable or irrevocable, as the author gave it absolutely. Abu Yusuf said: Lineage is established up to two years in the case of bā'in divorce, as for an adult woman, and up to twenty-seven months in the case of raj'ī divorce, because he considers him as having intercourse at the end of the 'iddah which is three months, then she brings the child at the maximum period of pregnancy which is two years. Their (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad's) reasoning: The end of the 'iddah of a minor has a determinate means, which is the months; with their passage, the law rules that the 'iddah is over, and this indication is stronger than her own admission because it does not admit of differing opinions, while admission does. So if she gives birth before the passage of nine months from the time of divorce, it is known that the pregnancy existed before the end of the 'iddah; if she gives birth at nine months or more, it is a new pregnancy after the end of her 'iddah by months. In al-Bada'i' there is a mistake on this, so avoid it, for he said: 'If she does not acknowledge his intercourse, how then for the woman in 'iddah?' So it is necessary to combine, for example, by saying: 'It should be clear that a claim of accepted shubhah, not merely shubhah in the act, is required.' Then he said: 'The correct view is that nothing beyond his claim is required, because in the book nothing else is required, and it is interpreted as mere shubhah which is mere assumption of permissibility.' His statement: '(like the strangeness of what he reported in al-Mujtabā)' — because it states that his lineage is not established. If she brings it at the completion of two years, then necessarily the conception would have occurred before the divorce, requiring the child to remain more than two years, so how can it be established according to them without a claim if she brings it after the maximum? He said: I say: It appears that his ruling of strangeness is based on his understanding that 'after the maximum' means more than two years, which is not established. Rather, the intended meaning is the maximum period of pregnancy which is two years; and then the difference in their expressions is based on the difference between Abu Yusuf and his two companions, and the contradiction is removed, so ponder. End. And what passed from al-Nahr supports this: the truth is that it should be taken as based on two different narrations.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage addresses the paternity of a child born to a minor wife (murāhiqah) who has been divorced. The ruling: if she gives birth before nine months from the divorce, paternity is established; if at nine months or more, it is not. This applies whether the divorce was revocable or irrevocable, according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf differs: for a bā'in divorce, he extends the period to two years (like an adult woman); for a raj'ī divorce, he extends to 27 months (2 years plus 3 months 'iddah). Their reasoning: for a minor, the 'iddah ends by the passage of three months (since she does not have menstruation). The end of 'iddah by months is a definite legal ruling, stronger than her own admission. Therefore, if pregnancy appears within nine months of divorce, it must have occurred during the 'iddah (since the gestation period is normally nine months). If birth occurs after nine months, the pregnancy began after the 'iddah ended, so the child is not from the former husband. The passage then warns about a mistaken statement in al-Bada'i' that seems to suggest otherwise, and attempts a reconciliation. The discussion of 'shubhah' and the requirement of the husband's claim is similar to earlier sections. The passage emphasizes that Abu Hanifa and Muhammad's position is based on the definitive legal termination of 'iddah by months, while Abu Yusuf's view considers the possibility of prolonged gestation. The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, as it is more consistent with the definitive nature of the 'iddah period for minors.

Key Terms
المراهقة (al-murāhiqah): A minor girl who has reached puberty but is still under the age of majority; here specifically one who does not menstruate.
العدة (al-'iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death before remarrying; for a minor who does not menstruate, it is three months.
الطلاق الرجعي (al-ṭalāq al-raj‘ī): Revocable divorce: the husband can take the wife back without a new contract during the 'iddah.
الطلاق البائن (al-ṭalāq al-bā’in): Irrevocable divorce: the husband cannot take the wife back without a new marriage contract.
النسب (al-nasab): Lineage/paternity: the legal attribution of a child to his father.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Two years.
B) Twenty-seven months.
C) Nine months.
D) Six months.

Key Takeaway
Paternity of a child born to a minor divorcée is established only if birth occurs within nine months of divorce, based on the definitive end of her three-month 'iddah.

Talaq — Divorce

Lineage of Child from Divorced Minor Girl: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on lineage time limits

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on lineage of a child born to a divorced minor, explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf, and identify the operative ruling (mufta bihi).

Core Idea
A child born to a divorced minor after nine months from divorce has no lineage; the iddah's completion by months is definitive.

This lesson examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the establishment of lineage (nasab) when a minor girl who has been divorced gives birth. The core issue is whether the firm legal determination of the iddah's end by months should override potential scenarios of intercourse at the end of the iddah. Understanding this helps clarify the Hanafi commitment to definitive legal indicators over speculative possibilities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling from the matn
Start by understanding what the matn (Kanz al-Daqaiq) states: for a divorced minor girl whose marriage was consummated, if she gives birth within nine months of the divorce, lineage is established; if at nine months or later, it is not.
This applies to both revocable and irrevocable divorce.
2
Recognize the point of agreement: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad
Both Imams agree on the nine-month limit. Their logic: the iddah of a minor is three months, a definitive legal period. Birth before nine months indicates pregnancy before the iddah ended. Birth at or after nine months indicates a new pregnancy after iddah.
The nine months = 3 months iddah + 6 months minimum gestation.
3
Examine Abu Yusuf's opposing view
Abu Yusuf holds that lineage can be established up to two years (ba'in divorce) or twenty-seven months (raj'i divorce). His reasoning: the husband may have had intercourse at the very end of the iddah, so the maximum pregnancy period should be counted from that point.
Two years is the maximum duration of pregnancy according to Hanafi school.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to Abu Yusuf
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad argue that the completion of the iddah by the passage of months is a definitive legal ruling (hukm qat'i). It does not admit doubt. The husband's hypothetical intercourse is speculative and cannot override the clear indication of the end of iddah.
This illustrates a usul principle: a definitive indicator prevails over a speculative one.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (operative position)
The commentary (Al-Bahr al-Raiq) corrects an error in Al-Bada'i' and confirms that the relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Textual authority and methodological strength favour their view.
Abu Yusuf's view is a valid ikhtilaf but not adopted for fatwa.

وهذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد سواء، كان الطلاق رجعياً أو بائناً كما أطلقه المصنف. وقال أبو يوسف يثبت النسب إلى سنتين في الطلاق البائن كالكبيرة وإلى سبعة وعشرين شهراً في الطلاق الرجعي لأنه يجعل واطئاً في قال : إذا لم تقر بانقضاء عدتها فإن جاءت به لأقل من ستة أشهر من وقت الطلاق يثبت النسب وإن جاءت لستة أشهر أو لأكثر لا يثبت. وصوابه ابدال الستة بالتسعة كما في المختصر أو إبدال قوله به من وقت الطلاق بقوله من وقت انقضاء العدة بالأشهر الثلاثة والعبارتان سواء. قيد المصنف بكونها مطلقة لأنها لو مات عنها زوجها ولم تقر بالحبل ولا بانقضاء العدة فعندهما إن ولدت لأقل من عشرة أشهر وعشرة أيام يثبت النسب لأنه تبين وقيدنا بكونه دخل بها لأنه لو لم يدخل بها وجاءت بولد فإن كان لأقل من ستة أشهر من وقت الطلاق يثبت نسبه، وإن جاءت به لأكثر منها لا يثبت لحصول العلوق وهي أجنبية كما في غاية البيان. وقيدنا بكونها لم تقر His saying: (And the minor [girl] [if she brings the child] in less than nine months, otherwise not) meaning: the lineage of the child of the divorced minor girl is established if she brings it in less than nine months, and he had consummated the marriage with her, and she did not acknowledge the completion of her waiting period, and she did not claim pregnancy. And if she brings it at nine months or more, it is not established. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, whether the divorce is revocable or irrevocable, as the author stated absolutely. Abu Yusuf said: Lineage is established up to two years in irrevocable divorce like the adult woman, and up to twenty-seven months in revocable divorce, because he considers him as having intercourse at the end of the waiting period, which is three months, then she brings it at the maximum duration of pregnancy, which is two years. And for them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is that the completion of the minor's waiting period has a specific means, which is the months; by its passage, the law judges the completion, and it is above her acknowledgment in indication because it does not admit dispute, while acknowledgment admits it. So if she gives birth before nine months from the time of divorce, it becomes clear that the pregnancy existed before the completion of the waiting period. And if she gives birth at nine months or more, then it is a new pregnancy after the completion of her waiting period by the months. And it has occurred in al-Bada'i' that there is an error in this, so avoid it. For he said: If she does not acknowledge the completion of her waiting period, and she brings it in less than six months from the time of divorce, lineage is established; and if she brings it at six months or more, it is not established. The correct is to replace 'six' with 'nine' as in al-Mukhtasar, or to replace his saying 'from the time of divorce' with 'from the time of completion of the waiting period by three months', and both expressions are the same. The author restricted it to her being divorced because if her husband died and she did not acknowledge pregnancy nor the completion of the waiting period, then according to them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), if she gives birth in less than ten months and ten days, lineage is established because it becomes clear that it existed before the completion of the death waiting period; otherwise it is not established because it is new after its completion. And according to Abu Yusuf, it is established up to two years like the adult woman. And if she acknowledged the completion of the waiting period after four months and ten days, then gave birth at six months or more, lineage is not established from him. And we restricted it to him having consummated the marriage because if he did not consummate and she brought a child, then if it is less than six months from the time of divorce, his lineage is established; if she brings it at more than that, it is not established because conception occurred while she was a foreign woman, as in Ghayat al-Bayan. And we restricted it to her not acknowledging its completion because if she acknowledged it after three months and did not claim pregnancy, then brought a child, then if it is less than six months from the time of acknowledgment, lineage is established; if she brings it at six months or more, lineage is not established due to the completion of the waiting period and the child's arrival after a full gestation period. And we restricted it to her not claiming pregnancy because if she claimed pregnancy, it is an acknowledgment from her of reaching puberty, so her statement is accepted, and she becomes like an adult woman regarding the establishment of his lineage, in that the completion of her waiting period is not confined to less than nine months. Then if the divorce is irrevocable, her child's lineage is established within less than two years; if it is revocable, his lineage is established if she brings it within less than twenty-seven months as in Ghayat al-Bayan, not absolutely, because for the adult woman, her child's lineage is established in revocable divorce for more than two years, and even if it extends to the age of menopause, due to the possibility of the extension of her purity and his intercourse with her at the end of the purity. And the author's expression 'minor' is preferable to many who say 'young girl' because the minor is the one who gives birth, not the one below that, and to al-Hidayah's expression 'young girl who is like one who can be copulated with', as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the establishment of lineage (nasab) for a child born to a divorced minor girl (mughallaqah). The matn from Kanz al-Daqaiq states that if a minor girl who has been divorced and whose marriage was consummated gives birth within nine months of the divorce, the child's lineage is established; if she gives birth at nine months or later, it is not. This applies whether the divorce is revocable (raj'i) or irrevocable (ba'in). Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani agree on this. Abu Yusuf differs: in a ba'in divorce, lineage is established up to two years (the maximum pregnancy duration), and in a raj'i divorce, up to twenty-seven months (three months iddah plus two years). His reasoning is that the husband is considered to have had intercourse at the end of the iddah, and then she gives birth at the maximum pregnancy term. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad argue that the iddah of a minor is determined by months (three months), and its completion is ruled by Islamic law. This legal ruling is stronger than her own acknowledgment because it is definitive. Therefore, if she gives birth before nine months from divorce, it shows the pregnancy preceded the iddah's end; if at nine months or later, it is a new pregnancy after iddah. The commentary then corrects an error in al-Bada'i', which had said six months instead of nine. The correct is to replace 'six' with 'nine' or to refer the time from the end of the iddah. Then the passage discusses conditions: the woman must be divorced (not widowed); if widowed, the ruling differs with a 10-month-10-day threshold. Other conditions include that the husband must have consummated the marriage, the woman must not have acknowledged the iddah's completion, and she must not have claimed pregnancy. If she claims pregnancy, she is treated as an adult woman, and the time limits change accordingly. The commentary prefers the term 'mughallaqah' (minor close to puberty) over 'saghirah' (young child) because the latter may not conceive.

Key Terms
مراهقة (murahiqah): A girl close to puberty who can become pregnant; in this context, a minor who has reached the age of possible conception.
عدة (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death before remarriage; for a minor, it is three months.
نسب (nasab): Lineage, paternity; the legal attribution of a child to its father.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), when is the lineage of a child born to a divorced minor established?
A) If the child is born within 9 months of the divorce
B) If the child is born within 2 years of the divorce
C) If the child is born within 27 months of the divorce
D) If the child is born at any time as long as the marriage was consummated

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school definitively limits the establishment of lineage for a divorced minor's child to births within nine months of divorce, based on the definitive completion of the iddah by months, rejecting speculative extensions.

Lineage of Child from Widow in Death Iddah
Explains ruling on lineage of child born to a widow within specific timeframes from death, including Zufar's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the timeframe for establishing lineage of a child born to a widow and explain Zufar's objection and the Hanafi response.

Core Idea
Lineage established up to 2 years because iddah can end by childbirth, not only by months.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a key question in family law: when is the lineage of a child born to a widow established? The Hanafi position is that the child's lineage is established if born within two years of the husband's death. Zufar objects, restricting it to the iddah period plus six months. The commentary refutes Zufar by noting that the death iddah can also end by childbirth, making the two-year period valid.

Guided Path
1
State the basic ruling
From the matn: lineage of a child born to a widow is established if born within less than two years from the time of death.
This is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.
2
Identify Zufar's objection
Zufar argues that if the child is born after completion of the death iddah (4 months 10 days) plus six months, lineage is not established because the law has definitively ended the iddah by months.
Zufar analogizes to the case of a minor who acknowledged completion of iddah.
3
Analyse the Hanafi response
The iddah can also end by childbirth, unlike the minor where pregnancy is impossible. Therefore, the possibility of extended pregnancy remains, supporting the two-year maximum.
The two-year maximum is based on the maximum known pregnancy duration.
4
Identify the scope and conditions
The ruling applies to an adult woman who does not acknowledge completion of her iddah. If she acknowledges, the issue is different. Also, the ruling covers both consummated and unconsummated marriages.
For a minor, the ruling differs as pregnancy is not presumed.
5
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Zufar's view, a child born after the death iddah plus six months would not have established lineage. Under the majority, it would if within two years.
This affects inheritance, custody, and other rights.

قوله : (والموت لأقل منهما) معطوف على الرجعي أي ويثبت نسب ولد معتدة الموت إذا جاءت به لأقل من سنتين من وقت الموت. وقال زفر : إذا جاءت به بعد انقضاء عدة الوفاة لستة أشهر لا يثبت النسب لأن الشرع حكم بانقضاء عدتها بالشهور لتعين الجهة فصار كما إذا أقرت بالانقضاء كما بينا في الصغيرة إلا أنا نقول : لانقضاء عدتها جهة أخرى وهو وضع الحمل بخلاف الصغيرة لأن الأصل فيها عدم الحمل لأنها ليست بمحل له قبل البلوغ وفيه شك. أطلق في معتدة الموت وهو مقيد بالكبيرة، وأما الصغيرة فقدمنا حكمها ومقيد بما إذا لم تقر بانقضاء عدتها، وأما إذا أقرت فهي داخلة في عموم المسألة الآتية عقيب هذه. وشمل كلامه المدخول به ساقط قوله : (لكن قيده في البدائع بأن تكون الخ) قال في النهر : هذا لم أجده في البدائع أقول : كأنه من نسخته فقد وجدته في النسخة التي عندي أيضاً قوله : (فحكمها في الوفاة ما هو حكمها في الطلاق) وهو أنها إذا كانت آيسة ولم تقر بانقضاء العدة فحكمها حكم ذوات الأقراء إذا جاءت
His saying: (And death [if less than them]) is conjoined to 'raj'i', meaning the lineage of the child of a woman in the waiting period of death is established if she brings it within less than two years from the time of death. And Zufar said: If she brings it after the completion of the death waiting period at six months, lineage is not established because the law has ruled the completion of her waiting period by the months due to the specific means, so it becomes like when she acknowledges the completion, as we explained for the minor. Except we say: For the completion of her waiting period, there is another means, which is giving birth, unlike the minor because the presumption in her is that there is no pregnancy, as she is not a place for it before puberty, and in it there is doubt. He made it absolute regarding the woman in death iddah, and it is restricted to the adult woman. As for the minor, we have already given her ruling, and it is restricted to when she does not acknowledge the completion of her waiting period. But if she acknowledges, then she enters into the generality of the following issue after this. And his speech includes the one with whom marriage was consummated. I say: It seems from his copy, for I found it in the copy I have as well. His saying: (So her ruling in death is the same as her ruling in divorce) meaning that if she is menopausal and does not acknowledge the completion of the waiting period, her ruling is the ruling of women with menstruation if she comes...
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage deals with the lineage of a child born to a woman whose husband died (iddah of death). The matn states that the child's lineage is established if born within two years from the time of death. Zufar, a Hanafi scholar, objects: if the child is born after the completion of the death iddah (which is four months and ten days for an adult woman) at six months, lineage is not established. His reasoning is that the law definitively ends the iddah by the months, similar to the case of a minor who acknowledged her iddah's completion. The commentator responds that the death iddah can also end by giving birth (another means), unlike the minor, who is not expected to be pregnant. Therefore, the absolute two-year period is valid for an adult woman. The passage then notes that this ruling applies to an adult woman, not a minor. It also discusses a condition: the woman must not have acknowledged the completion of her iddah, as acknowledgment removes the possibility of lineage. There is a brief discussion about a discrepancy in al-Bada'i' regarding a condition, but it is resolved. The final part mentions that the ruling for a menopausal woman (ayyisah) in death iddah is the same as for a woman who menstruates: her iddah is by months unless she gives birth, and the lineage ruling follows accordingly.

Key Terms
عدة الوفاة (iddat al-wafah): The waiting period for a widow: four months and ten days for an adult woman who is not pregnant.
زفر (Zufar): A prominent Hanafi jurist, a student of Abu Hanifa, who sometimes differed on subsidiary issues.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school establishes the lineage of a child born to a widow up to two years after the husband's death, considering the possibility of prolonged pregnancy.

Corrected Time Limits for Paternity After Divorce and Death
Commentary correcting paternity time limits with ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the corrected Hanafi time limits for establishing paternity in divorce and death cases, and explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf and Zufar's counterargument.

Core Idea
Paternity is established if birth occurs within nine months of divorce or ten months and ten days of death, not six.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq corrects a common error in the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The original text set a six-month limit from divorce, but the correct Hanafi position is nine months (or three months from the end of 'iddah). The author then expands to widows, where the limit is ten months and ten days according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), while Abu Yusuf personally extends it to two years. Zufar's objection is also addressed, and the conditions for these rulings are clarified.

Guided Path
1
Identify the error in the base text
The original matn said paternity is established if birth occurs within six months of divorce. The correct limit is nine months from divorce (or three months from the end of 'iddah). Understand why: the shortest possible pregnancy is six months, so birth before six months proves conception before divorce; birth at or after six months could be from after divorce, so the law allows a full nine-month term to cover the maximum pregnancy duration.
The correction replaces 'six' with 'nine' in the matn, or equivalently, measures from the end of 'iddah (three months) instead of divorce.
2
Apply the correction to the death case
For a widow who does not admit pregnancy or the end of her 'iddah, the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) rule: if she gives birth within ten months and ten days of death, paternity is established. This is because the 'iddah of death is four months and ten days, plus six months minimum gestation equals ten months and ten days. Abu Yusuf alone extends this to two years, based on the possibility of a hidden pregnancy. Conditions: the husband must have consummated, and she must not have admitted the end of 'iddah or claimed pregnancy.
If she admits the end of 'iddah, different rules apply (see later in the text).
3
Understand the conditions and exceptions
The author lists three restrictions: (1) the husband must have consummated the marriage (if not, paternity only within six months of divorce, as conception would be adulterous); (2) the woman must not have admitted the end of 'iddah (if she does, paternity depends on timing from her admission); (3) she must not have claimed pregnancy (if she does, she is treated as an adult woman, and paternity extends to two years for ba'in divorce or longer for raj'i).
The term 'murahiqah' (adolescent) is preferred over 'saghirah' (minor) because only an adolescent can conceive.
4
Analyze Zufar's objection and the Hanafi response
Zufar argues: for a widow, if she gives birth six months after the end of her 'iddah (by months), paternity is not established because the law has already ruled the end of 'iddah by months. The Hanafi response: the 'iddah can also end by delivery, so a pregnancy unknown at death can later establish paternity. Unlike a minor who cannot conceive, an adult can have a hidden pregnancy. Thus, the Hanafi position holds up to two years (as per Abu Yusuf) or ten months ten days (as per the two).
Practical difference: under Zufar, a child born 14 months after husband's death would not be attributed; under Hanafi, it is attributed if within two years (or ten months ten days according to the mufta bihi).

Book of Divorce, Chapter on Establishment of Lineage: By the end of her 'iddah, if she gives birth to him at less than six months from the time of divorce, the lineage is established; and if she gives birth at six months or more, it is not established. And the correct is to replace 'six' with 'nine' as in al-Mukhtasar, or to replace his saying 'from the time of divorce' with his saying 'from the time of the end of the 'iddah with three months', and both expressions are the same. The author restricted it to her being divorced because if her husband died and she did not admit pregnancy nor the end of the 'iddah, then according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), if she gives birth at less than ten months and ten days, the lineage is established because it becomes clear that it existed before the passage of the 'iddah of death; otherwise it is not established because it occurred after its passage. Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, correcting and detailing the ruling on paternity establishment. The author begins by correcting the matn: originally it said if a divorced woman gives birth less than six months from divorce, paternity is established; but the correct view is nine months from divorce, or alternatively three months from the end of the 'iddah. He then extends the ruling to the case of a widow who does not admit the end of her 'iddah nor pregnancy: according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), if she gives birth within ten months and ten days from death, paternity is established, otherwise not; according to Abu Yusuf alone, it extends to two years. The author then lists three restrictions: (1) the husband must have consummated the marriage, (2) the woman must not have admitted the end of the 'iddah, and (3) she must not have claimed pregnancy. If she claims pregnancy, she becomes like an adult woman: for a ba'in divorce, paternity is established up to two years; for a raj'i divorce, up to twenty-seven months for a minor, but for an adult it extends even longer. The author prefers the term 'murahiqah' (adolescent) over 'saghirah' (minor) because only an adolescent can give birth. Finally, he discusses the death case and Zufar's objection: Zufar argues that if a widow gives birth six months after the end of her 'iddah, paternity is not established because the 'iddah ended by months; the Hanafi response is that pregnancy is another way to end the 'iddah, unlike the minor who is not capable of pregnancy. The ruling is restricted to adult women; the minor's ruling was given earlier.

Key Terms
النسب (al-nasab): Legal lineage/paternity: the attribution of a child to the father.
العدة (al-iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband before remarriage.
المراهقة (al-murahiqah): An adolescent girl nearing or able to conceive; the preferred term used by the author.

C) No, because the original text said six months is the threshold, and the correction keeps it the same.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school corrects the common six-month limit to nine months from divorce and sets a ten-month-ten-day limit for widows, with the view of the two being the mufta bihi.

Paternity When a Woman Admits Her 'Iddah Has Ended
Ruling with proof: establishing paternity for the muqirrah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for establishing paternity when a woman admits her 'iddah has passed and apply them to a scenario.

Core Idea
If a woman admits her 'iddah ended but gives birth within six months, the admission is void and paternity is established -- but only if within two years of separation.

This lesson focuses on a specific scenario: a woman undergoing 'iddah (waiting period) from divorce or death who admits that her 'iddah has already ended. Later, she gives birth. Is the child attributed to the ex-husband? The Hanafi school has detailed rules balancing her admission against the possibility of conception during the 'iddah. We will unpack the conditions for paternity in such cases, as explained by the commentator of Kanz al-Daqa'iq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the woman and her admission
The woman is in 'iddah and she admits that her 'iddah has ended (al-muqirrah bi-madiha). The ruling on paternity depends on the timing of birth relative to her admission and the original separation.
She admits the 'iddah has passed, not necessarily that she is not pregnant.
2
Apply the six-month rule from admission
If she gives birth within six months of her admission, her admission is proven false (since a pregnancy that ends within six months must have begun before the 'iddah ended). Therefore, paternity is established. If six months or more, the birth could have occurred after the 'iddah, so paternity is not established.
The six-month period is based on the minimum duration of pregnancy.
3
Apply the two-year rule from separation
Even if the birth is within six months of admission, it must also be within two years from the time of separation (death or divorce) for paternity to be established. If more than two years have passed, paternity is not established regardless of the six-month rule.
The two-year period is the maximum possible duration of pregnancy according to the Hanafi school.
4
Consider the exception for revocable divorce
If the divorce was revocable (raj'i), and the woman gives birth within the 'iddah period (which can extend beyond two years in some views), paternity is established and the husband becomes a retractor. This overrides the two-year limit in revocable divorce.
Revocable divorce keeps the marriage tie intact during 'iddah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

كانت آيسة ولم تقر بانقضاء العدة فحكمها حكم ذوات الأقراء إذا جاءت لأقل من ستة أشهر من وقت الإقرار وإلا لا والمعتدة إن جحدت ولادتها بشهادة بها وغيرها كما في البدائع، وشمل ما إذا كانت من ذوات الأقراء أو من ذوات الأشهر لكن قيده في البدائع بأن تكون من ذوات الأقراء قال وأما إذا كانت من ذوات الأشهر فإن كانت آيسة أو صغيرة فحكمها في الوفاة ما هو حكمها في الطلاق وقد ذكرناه اهـ . وقيد بالأقل لأنها لو جاءت لاحتمال الحدوث بعده وهو المراد بقوله وإلا لا . وذكر في التبيين أن هذا إذا جاءت به لأقل من سنتين من وقت الفراق بالموت أو بالطلاق، وإن جاءت به لأكثر منهما لا يثبت وإن كان لأقل من ستة أشهر من وقت الإقرار كما إذا أقرت بعدما مضى من عدتها سنتان إلا شهرين فجاءت بولد بعد ثلاثة أشهر من وقت الإقرار لم يثبت نسبه منه لأن شرط ثبوته أن يكون لأقل من سنتين من وقت الفراق بالموت أو بالطلاق وبعده لا يثبت، وإن لم تقر بالانقضاء فمع الإقرار أولى إلا إذا كان الطلاق رجعياً فحينئذ يثبت ويكون مراجعاً على ما بينا من قبل بقي فيه إشكال وهو ما إذا أقرت بانقضاء عدتها ثم جاءت بولد لأقل من ستة أشهر من وقت الإقرار ولأقل من سنتين من وقت الفراق ينبغي أن لا يثبت نسبه إذا كانت المدة تحتمل ذلك بأن أقرت بعدما مضى سنة مثلاً ثم جاءت بولد لأقل من ستة أشهر من وقت الإقرار لأنه يحتمل أن عدتها انقضت في شهرين أو ثلاثة : بعد ثلاثة بولد إلى سنتين من وقت الطلاق ثبت نسبه وإن كانت صغيرة، فإما أن تقر بانقضاء العدة. أشهر أو لا تقر، فإن لم تقر فإما أن تسكت أو تقر بالحبل وقد تقدم بيان ذلك آنفاً وهو الذي ذكره في البدائع.. ومقتضاه أنها إذا لم تدع الانقضاء ولا الحبل أنه لا يثبت هنا إلا إذا جاءت به لأقل من تسعة أشهر كما في الطلاق، ويخالفه ما قدمه المؤلف بقوله قيد المصنف بكونها مطلقة الخ» وكذا قال الشارح الزيلعي الصغيرة إذا توفي عنها زوجها فإن أقرت بالحبل فهي كالكبيرة يثبت نسبه إلى سنتين لأن القول قولها في ذلك، وإن أقرت بانقضاء عدتها بعد أربعة أشهر وعشر ثم ولدت لستة أشهر لم يثبت النسب منه، وإن لم تدع حبلاً ولم تقر بانقضاء العدة فعندهما إن ولدت لأقل من عشرة أشهر وعشرة أيام يثبت النسب منه وإلا فلا، وعند أبي يوسف يثبت إلى سنتين. ثم ذكر بعده حكم الآيسة أنها إذا كانت معتدة عن وفاة فهي والتي من ذوات الأقراء سواء لأن عدة الوفاة تكون بالأشهر في حق كل واحدة منهما إذا لم تكن حاملاً. فصاعداً
His saying: 'But he restricted it in al-Bada'i to being...' He said in al-Nahr: I did not find this in al-Bada'i. I say: It seems it is from his copy, for I found it in the copy that I have as well. His saying: 'So her ruling in death is what her ruling in divorce is' meaning that if she is menopausal and did not admit the end of the 'iddah, her ruling is like that of women of menstrual cycles: if she comes with it at less than six months from the time of admission, [paternity is established]; otherwise not. And the woman in 'iddah if she denies her childbirth with testimony and other than that, as in al-Bada'i. And it includes whether she is of menstrual cycles or of months, but he restricted it in al-Bada'i to being of menstrual cycles. He said: 'As for if she is of months (dhawat al-ashhur), then if she is menopausal or minor, her ruling in death is what her ruling in divorce is, and we have mentioned it.' And it is restricted by 'less' because if she brings a child at more than two years from the time of death, his lineage is not established; thus in al-Bada'i. And I have not seen anyone explicitly stating two years, and it should be like the majority as previously mentioned in its parallel. His saying: 'And the one who admits its passage: for less than six months from the time of admission, otherwise not.' i.e., the lineage of the child of the woman in 'iddah who admits its passage is established if she brings the child at less than six months from the time of admission, because her falsehood becomes certain, so her admission is invalidated. And if she brings it at six months or more from the time of admission, it is not established, because we do not know the invalidation of her admission due to the possibility of its occurrence after it. And that is what is meant by his saying 'otherwise not'. And it is mentioned in al-Tabyin that this is if she brings it at less than two years from the time of separation by death or divorce; if she brings it at more than either, it is not established, even if it is less than six months from the time of admission. For example, if she admits after two years minus two months of her 'iddah have passed, then she brings a child after three months from the time of admission, his lineage from him is not established, because the condition for its establishment is that it be at less than two years from the time of separation by death or divorce, and after that it is not established. And if she does not admit the end, then with admission it is more so, except if the divorce is revocable, then it is established and he becomes a revoker, as we explained before. There remains a problem: if she admits the end of her 'iddah, then brings a child at less than six months from the time of admission and at less than two years from the time of separation, it would seem that his lineage should not be established if the period permits that, for example if she admits after a year has passed, then brings a child at less than six months from the time of admission, because it is possible that her 'iddah ended in two or three months: after three [months]? A child up to two years from the time of divorce, his lineage is established even if she is minor. Either she admits the end of the 'iddah [or not]. And if she does not admit, then either she remains silent or admits pregnancy, and the explanation of that has preceded recently. That is what is mentioned in al-Bada'i. And its implication is that if she does not claim the end nor pregnancy, then here it is not established unless she brings it at less than nine months as in divorce. And it contradicts what the author presented in his saying: 'The author restricted it to her being divorced...' And similarly said the commentator al-Zayla'i: If a minor's husband dies and she admits pregnancy, she is like an adult, paternity is established up to two years because her statement is accepted in that. And if she admits the end of her 'iddah after four months and ten days, then gives birth at six months, paternity from him is not established. And if she does not claim pregnancy nor admit the end of the 'iddah, then according to the two, if she gives birth at less than ten months and ten days, paternity is established from him; otherwise not. And according to Abu Yusuf, it is established up to two years. Then he mentioned afterward the ruling for the menopausal woman: that if she is in 'iddah from death, she and women of menstrual cycles are the same, because the 'iddah of death is by months for each of them if she is not pregnant.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the same chapter, focusing on two main issues: (1) the qualification from al-Bada'i that the earlier discussion about women who do not admit the end of 'iddah applies specifically to women of menstrual cycles (dhawat al-aqra'), not to women of months (dhawat al-ashhur) like the menopausal or minor, and (2) the rule for a woman who admits that her 'iddah has passed (muqirrah bi-madiha). For the muqirrah, if she gives birth within six months of her admission, paternity is established because her admission is proven false; if she gives birth at six months or more, it is not established because the child could have been conceived after the 'iddah. However, there is an overarching condition: the birth must occur within two years of the separation (divorce or death). If more than two years have passed, paternity is not established even if within six months of admission. An exception is revocable divorce: if she gives birth during the 'iddah period (within two years), paternity is established and the divorce is revoked (the husband becomes a retractor). The passage also resolves an apparent contradiction: for a minor widow who does not claim pregnancy nor admit the end of 'iddah, the ruling according to the two is as given earlier (within ten months and ten days), but according to Abu Yusuf, it extends to two years. Finally, the menopausal widow is treated like women of cycles for the death 'iddah.

Key Terms
المقرة بمضيها (al-muqirrah bi-madiha): The woman who admits that her 'iddah has passed (ended).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A woman in 'iddah from a final divorce admits her 'iddah has ended. She gives birth five months after her admission. The divorce occurred three years before the birth. Is the child's paternity established?
A) Yes, because she gave birth within six months of admission.
B) No, because the birth is more than two years from the divorce.
C) Yes, because both conditions seem to be met.
D) No, because she admitted the 'iddah ended.

Key Takeaway
For a woman who admits her 'iddah has ended, paternity is established only if birth occurs within six months of admission and within two years of separation; revocable divorce is an exception.

Women of Cycles vs. Women of Months and the Minor Widow Dispute
Expansion probe: clarifying the scope of earlier rulings and presenting intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the rulings for women of menstrual cycles and women of months in paternity cases, and state the intra-Hanafi dispute on the minor widow.

Core Idea
Paternity rules differ for women of cycles and women of months; for a minor widow who does not claim pregnancy, the two imams limit to 10 months 10 days, but Abu Yusuf extends to two years.

The commentary clarifies that earlier statements about women who do not admit the end of 'iddah apply only to women of menstrual cycles (dhawat al-aqra'), not to women of months (dhawat al-ashhur) like menopausal or minor women. For these, the ruling in death is the same as in divorce. Additionally, there is a known intra-Hanafi disagreement regarding the minor widow who neither claims pregnancy nor admits the end of 'iddah: according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two), her paternity is limited to 10 months 10 days; according to Abu Yusuf alone, it extends to two years.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of women
Women of menstrual cycles (dhawat al-aqra') have 'iddah by three cycles. Women of months (dhawat al-ashhur) have 'iddah by months: menopausal women and minor girls who do not menstruate.
The earlier discussion on paternity for those who do not admit the end of 'iddah was limited to women of cycles.
2
Apply the ruling for women of months in divorce and death
For a woman of months (e.g., menopausal) who does not admit the end of 'iddah, her ruling in death is the same as in divorce. This means the same time limits apply: paternity is established if birth within a certain period from separation, depending on whether she claims pregnancy or not.
The passage does not repeat the numbers here, but refers to the earlier discussion.
3
Analyze the minor widow scenario
A minor widow: if she admits pregnancy, she is like an adult, paternity up to two years. If she admits the end of 'iddah (e.g., after 4 months 10 days) and gives birth, paternity is not established under certain conditions (because her admission would be contradicted). If she neither claims pregnancy nor admits the end, there is a dispute.
The intra-Hanafi dispute: the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say paternity established only if birth within 10 months 10 days. Abu Yusuf (alone) says up to two years.
4
Resolve the dispute via tarjih
The commentator notes that the author of al-Bada'i and others follow the view of the two (10 months 10 days) for the minor widow, indicating that this is the relied-upon position.
However, in cases of necessity, Abu Yusuf's view may be followed.
