Conditional Divorce on Birth and Effect of Acknowledging Pregnancy
Ruling on conditional divorce tied to childbirth and woman's testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi intra-madhhab dispute on the sufficiency of a woman's testimony for conditional divorce and distinguish the effect of prior acknowledgment of pregnancy.

Core Idea
The husband's acknowledgment of pregnancy removes the need for witnesses for the wife's claim of birth per Abu Hanifa, but not per the Sahibayn.

In this passage, the author of al-Bahr al-Raiq discusses two scenarios involving conditional divorce tied to childbirth. The first scenario deals with whether the testimony of a single woman (midwife) suffices to establish the birth and trigger the divorce. The second scenario involves the husband's prior acknowledgment of pregnancy and how it affects the evidentiary requirements. We will examine the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and understand the underlying principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage first discusses conditional divorce without acknowledgment, then with acknowledgment. Note the difference in the ruling and evidence required.
2
Compare the positions in the first scenario
Abu Hanifa requires two men or one man and two women; the Sahibayn accept one woman's testimony. Understand the basis of each view.
3
Analyze the proofs (adilla) for each
The Sahibayn cite the hadith about women's testimony in unseen matters; Abu Hanifa argues that the claim of breaking the oath requires complete proof and that birth and divorce are separate.
4
Examine the effect of acknowledgment
When the husband acknowledges pregnancy, Abu Hanifa accepts the wife's word alone; the Sahibayn still require a midwife's testimony. Understand the reasoning behind this distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو علق طلاقها بولادتها وشهدت امرأة على الولادة لم تطلق) يعني لم يقع إلا بشهادة رجلين أو رجل وامرأتين عند أبي حنيفة. وقالا : تطلق لأن شهادتها حجة في ذلك قال عليه السلام «شهادة النساء جائزة فيما لا يطلع عليه الرجل ولأنها لما قبلت على الولادة تقبل فيما يبتني عليها وهو الطلاق. ولأبي حنيفة أنها ادعت الحنث فلا يثبت إلا بحجة تامة وهذا لأن شهادتهن ضرورية في الولادة فلا تظهر في حق الطلاق لأنه ينفك عنها وشرط في البدائع على قولهما أن تكون المرأة عدلة قيد بالطلاق لأن النسب يثبت بشهادتها وكذا ما هو من لوازمه من أمومية الولد لو كانت لو كانت أمة، وثبوت اللعان فيما إذا نفاه ووجوب الحد بنفيه إن لم يكن أهلاً للعان وليس مراده خصوص الطلاق بل كل ما لم يكن من لوازم الولادة فالعتاق كذلك قوله : ( وإن كان أقر بالحبل طلقت بلا شهادة أي بلا شهادة أحد أصلاً عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما تشترط شهادة القابلة لأنه لا بد من حجة لدعواها الحنث وشهادتها حجة فيه ما بيننا، وله أن الإقرار بالحبل إقرار بما يفضي إليه وهو الولادة ولأنه أقر بكونها مؤتمنة فيقبل قولها في رد الأمانة، وعلى هذا الخلاف لو كان الحبل ظاهراً، أما عندهما فظاهر لأنها مدعية فلا بد من إقامة البينة، وأما عنده فإن الطلاق تعلق بأمر كائن لا محالة فيقبل قولها فيه . والحاصل أن التعليق إن كان بما هو معلوم الوقوع بعده وعلمه من جهتها كما بحيضها وولادتها بعد الإقرار بحبلها أو ظهور حملها كان التزاماً لتصديقها عند إخبارها به واعترافاً بأنها
His statement: 'And if he suspended her divorce on her giving birth and a woman testified to the birth, she is not divorced' means it does not occur except by testimony of two men or one man and two women according to Abu Hanifa. The two said: She is divorced because her testimony is proof in that matter; the Prophet said 'Women's testimony is permissible in matters men cannot see' and because she is accepted concerning the birth, she is accepted concerning what depends on it, i.e., divorce. For Abu Hanifa, she claims breaking the oath, so it is not established except by complete proof, and because women's testimony is necessary for birth, it does not appear in the matter of divorce since divorce is separate from it. In al-Bada'i, it is conditioned on the woman being just ('adl) according to the two. And his statement: 'And if he acknowledged pregnancy, she is divorced without testimony', meaning without any testimony at all according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two, the testimony of the midwife is required because there must be proof for her claim of breaking the oath, and her testimony is proof in our view. For him, the acknowledgment of pregnancy is acknowledgment of what leads to it, i.e., birth, and because he acknowledged her as trustworthy, so her statement is accepted in returning the trust. This disagreement applies whether the pregnancy was apparent. According to the two, apparently she is claiming so she must produce evidence. According to him, the divorce is attached to something that will inevitably happen, so her statement is accepted. In summary, if the suspension is attached to something known to occur later, known from her side, like menstruation or birth after acknowledging pregnancy or visible pregnancy, it is a commitment to believe her when she informs and an admission that she...
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage continues the chapter on establishing lineage, focusing on conditional divorce. The author explains that if a man says, 'If you give birth, you are divorced,' and a woman (midwife) testifies to the birth, the divorce does not occur according to Abu Hanifa unless two men or one man and two women testify. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad disagree, saying her testimony suffices because it is a matter men cannot see, and accepting her for the birth means accepting her for the dependent divorce. Abu Hanifa argues that the woman is claiming the occurrence of the condition (the birth), which breaks the oath, and that requires full proof; women's testimony is only necessary for birth itself and does not extend to divorce. For Abu Hanifa, the husband's prior acknowledgment of pregnancy changes the rule: if he acknowledged she was pregnant, then her word alone is sufficient for the divorce without any witness. The Sahibayn still require a midwife's testimony in that case. The key principle is that if the husband knowingly attached divorce to an event that he knows from her side (like her menstruation or birth after he acknowledged pregnancy), then he is committed to accepting her word when she reports it.

Key Terms
الحنث (al-hinth): The breaking of an oath or condition; here, the occurrence of the birth that triggers the divorce.
الإقرار بالحبل (al-iqrār bil-ḥabal): Acknowledgment by the husband that his wife is pregnant.
العدالة (al-‘adālah): Justice or moral probity required in a witness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a husband acknowledges his wife's pregnancy but later she claims birth, what is required for the divorce to take effect?
A) Testimony of two men
B) Testimony of one midwife
C) Her word alone
D) Testimony of one man and two women

Key Takeaway
The sufficiency of evidence for conditional divorce turns on whether the husband acknowledged pregnancy: Abu Hanifa requires strong proof without acknowledgment but accepts the wife's word with acknowledgment; the Sahibayn accept a woman's testimony in both cases.

Testimony of a Woman on Birth for Suspended Divorce
Intra-Hanafi dispute on testimony for suspended divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the acceptance of a single woman's testimony for a suspended divorce and evaluate their respective proofs.

Core Idea
A single woman's testimony suffices for birth but not for divorce, according to Abu Hanifa.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on a subtle point: when a husband suspends divorce on the condition of his wife giving birth, does the testimony of a single woman (e.g., a midwife) prove that the divorce has occurred? Abu Hanifa insists on standard testimony, while his two foremost students differ. Understanding their arguments sharpens your ability to trace how a general principle (women's testimony in unseen matters) is delimited by the underlying rationale (necessity).

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling in the matn
The base text states that if one woman testifies to the birth under a suspended divorce, the divorce does not occur. Recognise that this is only the default position.
The matn sets the stage for the dispute.
2
Locate the two positions
Abu Hanifa holds that the divorce requires two men or a man and two women. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that one upright woman suffices because her testimony is accepted for birth and should extend to its consequence.
Note that the Sahibayn require the woman to be 'adl (upright).
3
Examine the Sahibayn's proof
They cite the hadith permitting women's testimony in matters men cannot see and use qiyas: if testimony is accepted for the cause (birth), it must be accepted for the effect (divorce).
This is a dalil naqli (hadith) combined with qiyas.
4
Analyse Abu Hanifa's response
Abu Hanifa counters that women's testimony is accepted only out of necessity for the birth itself; divorce is a separate claim (hanth) requiring complete proof. The birth is a physical fact, but divorce is a legal consequence that can be proven by other means.
The key distinction is necessity vs. general applicability.
5
Determine the practical difference
If only one woman testifies, Abu Hanifa says the wife remains married; the Sahibayn say she is divorced. This affects subsequent rulings like remarriage and iddah.
The thamarah al-khilaf is concrete: the marital status changes based on which view is followed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو علق طلاقها بولادتها وشهدت امرأة على الولادة لم تطلق) يعني لم يقع إلا بشهادة رجلين أو رجل وامرأتين عند أبي حنيفة. وقالا : تطلق لأن شهادتها حجة في ذلك قال عليه السلام «شهادة النساء جائزة فيما لا يطلع عليه الرجل ولأنها لما قبلت على الولادة تقبل فيما يبتني عليها وهو الطلاق. ولأبي حنيفة أنها ادعت الحنث فلا يثبت إلا بحجة تامة وهذا لأن شهادتهن ضرورية في الولادة فلا تظهر في حق الطلاق لأنه ينفك عنها وشرط في البدائع على قولهما أن تكون المرأة عدلة قيد بالطلاق لأن النسب يثبت بشهادتها وكذا ما هو من لوازمه من أمومية الولد لو كانت لو كانت أمة، وثبوت اللعان فيما إذا نفاه ووجوب الحد بنفيه إن لم يكن أهلاً للعان وليس مراده خصوص الطلاق بل كل ما لم يكن من لوازم الولادة فالعتاق كذلك
His saying: (And if he suspended her divorce upon her giving birth and a woman testified to the birth, she is not divorced) means it does not occur except with the testimony of two men or one man and two women according to Abu Hanifa. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: She is divorced because her testimony is a proof in that matter; the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'The testimony of women is permissible in matters that men cannot see.' And because when her testimony is accepted regarding the birth, it is accepted regarding what is built upon it, namely divorce. And for Abu Hanifa: She claimed the violation (of the oath) so it is not established except with complete proof, and this is because their testimony is necessary for the birth, so it does not appear with respect to divorce because it can be separated from it. And it is stipulated in al-Bada'i' according to their view that the woman be upright; he restricted it to divorce because paternity is established by her testimony, and likewise its necessary consequences like the motherhood of the child if she were a slave-girl, and the establishment of li'an if he denies it, and the obligation of hadd by his denial if he is not eligible for li'an. And his intention is not specifically divorce, but rather everything that is not a necessary consequence of birth, such as manumission, is likewise.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding a case where a husband suspends his wife's divorce on her giving birth. The matn states that if a woman (midwife or another) testifies to the birth, the divorce does not occur. Abu Hanifa explains that such testimony is insufficient for divorce; it requires two men or one man and two women. The two companions argue that the woman's testimony suffices because the Prophet allowed women's testimony in matters not accessible to men, and since testimony is accepted for birth, it should be accepted for its consequence (divorce). Abu Hanifa counters that divorce is a separate claim of violation (hanth) requiring complete proof; women's testimony is only necessary for the birth itself due to privacy, but divorce can be proven by other means. The commentator notes that according to the two companions, the testifying woman must be upright ('adl). He clarifies that the ruling extends beyond divorce to all matters that are not necessary consequences of birth, such as establishing paternity or li'an, but not to unrelated matters like manumission.

Key Terms
علق طلاقها (ʿallaqa ṭalāqahā): He suspended her divorce upon a condition (e.g., birth).
شهادتها (shahādatuhā): Testimony of a woman; in this context, of a midwife or witness to birth.
حنث (ḥanth): Violation of an oath; here it refers to the divorce becoming due upon fulfillment of the condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, how many witnesses are required for a divorce suspended on birth to take effect based on a woman's testimony about the birth?
A) One woman's testimony suffices.
B) Two men or one man and two women.
C) One man's testimony suffices.
D) No testimony is needed if the husband admits.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the acceptance of a woman's testimony for birth does not automatically extend to its legal consequences; the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn turns on whether divorce is a necessary consequence of birth.

Admission of Pregnancy and Its Effect on Suspended Divorce: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on evidentiary requirements for suspended divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Hanifa accepts the husband's admission of pregnancy as sufficient for a suspended divorce to occur, and contrast this with the Sahibayn's requirement of midwife testimony.

Core Idea
Admission of pregnancy implies trustworthiness and inevitability of birth, so the husband's admission alone triggers the suspended divorce according to Abu Hanifa.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the evidentiary requirements for a suspended divorce (ta'liq al-talaq) when the husband admits his wife's pregnancy. The core question: does the husband's admission suffice to prove that the condition (e.g., 'if you give birth, you are divorced') has occurred, or must the wife produce a midwife's testimony? Abu Hanifa takes the former position, while his two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, insist on external proof. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to distinguish between substantive rulings and procedural proof in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute (ikhtilaf)
The matn states: 'if he admitted to pregnancy, she is divorced without testimony.' This triggers a known ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn. Your task is to isolate the exact point of disagreement: what is the legal requirement for the wife to prove that the suspended divorce has taken effect?
The disagreement is not about the divorce itself, but about the proof needed when the husband disputes the occurrence of the condition.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his dalil (istihsan)
Abu Hanifa holds that the husband's admission of pregnancy suffices. His reasoning: admission of pregnancy is admission of what necessarily follows, i.e., birth. Moreover, by admitting pregnancy, the husband has effectively declared his wife trustworthy (amana) regarding her bodily states. This is an application of istihsan (juristic preference) overriding the general rule that a claimant must produce evidence.
The key principle: iqrār bi-l-ḥabl iqrār bimā yufḍī ilayhi (admission of pregnancy is admission of its result).
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their dalil
The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) require the testimony of a midwife (al-qābila). Their reasoning: the wife is a claimant (mudda'iyya) claiming the violation of the conditional divorce (al-ḥanth), and as a claimant she must produce evidence. The midwife's testimony is acceptable proof in such matters because she is a trusted expert.
The Sahibayn apply the general evidentiary rule: al-bayyina 'alā man idda'ā (proof is on the claimant).
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, if the husband admitted pregnancy, the wife's later claim of birth is accepted without further evidence, and the divorce takes effect. Under the Sahibayn, even if the husband admitted pregnancy, the wife must still produce a midwife to testify to the birth if the husband denies it. This affects when the divorce is considered to have occurred.
The dispute also applies when the pregnancy is visible: the Sahibayn still require a midwife; Abu Hanifa considers visible pregnancy as confirmation.
5
Apply the exception: cases where admission does not suffice
The commentator notes a crucial distinction: if the suspended condition (e.g., birth) was not preceded by an admission of pregnancy or visible pregnancy at the time of suspension, then the wife's word is not automatically accepted. In that case, proof (midwife testimony) is needed. Additionally, for establishing paternity (nasab), all agree that midwife testimony is required.
This shows that Abu Hanifa's ruling is limited to cases where pregnancy was known (by admission or appearance) at the time of suspension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وإن كان أقر بالحبل طلقت بلا شهادة أي بلا شهادة أحد أصلاً عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما تشترط شهادة القابلة لأنه لا بد من حجة لدعواها الحنث وشهادتها حجة فيه ما بيننا، وله أن الإقرار بالحبل إقرار بما يفضي إليه وهو الولادة ولأنه أقر بكونها مؤتمنة فيقبل قولها في رد الأمانة، وعلى هذا الخلاف لو كان الحبل ظاهراً، أما عندهما فظاهر لأنها مدعية فلا بد من إقامة البينة، وأما عنده فإن الطلاق تعلق بأمر كائن لا محالة فيقبل قولها فيه . والحاصل أن التعليق إن كان بما هو معلوم الوقوع بعده وعلمه من جهتها كما بحيضها وولادتها بعد الإقرار بحبلها أو ظهور حملها كان التزاماً لتصديقها عند إخبارها به واعترافاً بأنها مؤتمنة فيه وإن لم يكن كذلك وهو التعليق بولادتها قبل الاعتراف بحبل سابق ولا ظهور حبل حال التعليق لم يلتزم ذلك فيحتاج عند إنكاره إلى الحجة ولا خلاف أن النسب لا يثبت بدون شهادة القابلة؛ كذا في البدائع
His saying: (And if he admitted to pregnancy, she is divorced without testimony) i.e., without any witness at all according to Abu Hanifa. And according to the two (companions), the testimony of the midwife is required because there must be proof for her claim of violation (hanth), and her testimony is proof in that matter in our view. And for him (Abu Hanifa): admission of pregnancy is an admission of what leads to it, which is birth, and because he admitted that she is trustworthy, so her word is accepted in returning the trust. And upon this difference of opinion if the pregnancy was apparent. As for according to them, it is apparent because she is a claimant so she must produce evidence. And as for according to him, the divorce is suspended upon an inevitable matter, so her word is accepted in it. The upshot is: if the suspension is upon something whose occurrence is known afterwards and is known from her side, like her menses and her giving birth after admitting to her pregnancy or the appearance of her pregnancy, it is a commitment to believe her when she informs of it and an acknowledgment that she is trustworthy in it. And if it is not like that, i.e., the suspension upon her birth before acknowledging a previous pregnancy or appearance of pregnancy at the time of suspension, he did not commit to that, so upon his denial, he needs proof, and there is no disagreement that paternity is not established without the testimony of the midwife; thus in al-Bada'i'.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether the husband's admission of pregnancy suffices for the suspended divorce to occur. The matn says: if he admitted to pregnancy, she is divorced without any testimony. Abu Hanifa holds that the admission is enough because admitting pregnancy implies admitting birth will follow, and he has considered her trustworthy. The two companions require the testimony of the midwife because the wife is claiming the violation (hanth) and needs proof; the midwife's testimony is acceptable. The same difference applies even if the pregnancy is visible: the companions still require evidence, while Abu Hanifa says the divorce is suspended on an inevitable event (birth), so her word is accepted. The commentator summarizes: if the suspension was on an event that will definitely occur and is known from her (like menses or birth after admitting pregnancy), it implies trust; if not (suspension on birth without known pregnancy), then her word is not automatically accepted and proof is needed. For paternity, all agree that midwife testimony is required.

Key Terms
الإقرار بالحبل (al-iqrār bi-l-ḥabl): Husband's admission that his wife is pregnant.
القابلة (al-qābilah): Midwife; the woman who assists in childbirth and can testify to birth.
أمانة (amānah): Trust; here it refers to the wife's trustworthiness in reporting her own physical conditions like pregnancy and birth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), what is required for the wife's claim that the divorce condition has occurred if the husband denies the pregnancy?
A) Her own testimony suffices.
B) Testimony of a midwife is required.
C) Testimony of two men is required.
D) No evidence is needed if the husband admitted before.

Key Takeaway
The husband's admission of pregnancy creates a presumption that birth will occur, but the Sahibayn require midwife testimony for the wife's claim to be accepted in the absence of such admission.

Pregnancy Duration and the Principle of Attribution to Nearest Time
Rulings on pregnancy duration with proofs and applied case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on minimum (6 months) and maximum (2 years) pregnancy duration, derive them from Quran and Hadith, and apply the maxim of attributing events to the nearest time in paternity and divorce cases, recognizing its limitations.

Core Idea
Pregnancy minimum 6 months, maximum 2 years; events attributed to nearest time unless stronger evidence prevents it.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq establishes two foundational rulings: the minimum pregnancy duration is six months and the maximum is two years, supported by Quranic verses and a hadith. It then applies these rulings to a case involving a slave-girl, where paternity depends on whether birth occurs within six months of purchase. The author also addresses an internal objection regarding consistency with another Hanafi position and clarifies the legal maxim that events are attributed to the nearest possible time, but not when it would undermine established proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two temporal rulings and their proofs
Locate the statements in the matn: maximum two years (supported by Hadith of Aisha), minimum six months (derived from Quran 46:15 and 31:14).
The hadith 'shadow of a spindle' emphasizes the strictness of the two-year limit.
2
Understand the Quranic derivation for the minimum
Combine 30 months (gestation + weaning) minus 24 months (weaning alone in Quran 31:14) to get 6 months minimum gestation.
This is a straightforward arithmetic deduction accepted unanimously among Hanafi scholars.
3
Analyze the objection and its refutation
An objection claimed inconsistency with Abu Hanifa's view on breastfeeding duration. The author refutes it by explaining that the word 'thirty' can refer to different totals depending on context, and the derivation remains valid.
This shows internal scholarly critique and defense.
4
Apply the case: slave-girl married, divorced, then purchased
If birth occurs less than 6 months after purchase, child attributed to man (conceived before purchase in 'iddah). If 6 months or more, child belongs to slave-girl (milk yamin).
This applies the maxim 'yuḍāfu al-ḥādithu ilā aqrabi waqtihi' (event attributed to nearest time) but only when it does not contradict evidence.
5
Explore three additional examples applying the maxim
The passage lists: (1) saying 'one of you is thrice divorced' to two wives without specification, (2) conditional divorce upon pregnancy, (3) revocably divorced woman giving birth within 2 years. In each, the maxim does not apply because it would invalidate established rulings.
These examples illustrate the exception to the general rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وأكثر مدة الحمل (سنتان) لقول عائشة رضي عنها: الولد لا يبقى في البطن أكثر من سنتين ولو بظل مغزل. رواه الدارقطني والبيهقي. وهو لا يعرف إلا سماعاً. وظل المغزل مثل لقلته لأن ظله حالة الدوران أسرع زوالاً من سائر الظلال وهو على حذف المضاف تقديره ولو بقدر ظل مغزل. ويروى ولو بفلكة مغزل أي ولو بقدر دوران فلكة مغزل قوله : (وأقلها ستة أشهر لقوله تعالى وحمله وفصاله ثلاثون شهراً [الأحقاف : ١٥] ثم قال وفصاله في عامين ) [لقمان: ١٤] فيبقى للحمل ستة أشهر ؛ كذا في الهداية. وقد نقل في فتح القدير أنه لا خلاف للعلماء فيه وأورد على ما في الهداية أنه مخالف لما قرره لأبي حنيفة في الرضاع من أن هذه المدة مضروبة بتمامها لكل من الحمل والفصال غير أن المنقص قام في أحدهما الحمل وهو حديث عائشة عنها . قلنا : قدمنا هناك أنه غير صحيح لما يلزم من أنه يراد بلفظ الثلاثين في إطلاق واحد حقيقة ثلاثين وأربعة وعشرين باعتبار إضافتين فلعله رجع إلى الصحيح قوله : (فلو نكح أمة فطلقها فاشتراها فولدت لأقل من ستة أشهر منه أي من وقت الشراء قوله : (لزمه وإلا لا) أي وإن ولدت لتمام ستة أشهر أو لأكثر منها لا يلزمه لأن في الوجه الأول ولد المعتدة فإن العلوق سابق على الشراء، وفي الوجه الثاني ولد المملوكة لأنه يضاف الحادث إلى أقرب وقته حيث لم يتضمن إبطال ما كان ثابتاً بالدليل أو ترك العمل بالمقتضى، وبه اندفع ما أورد عليه كما علم في فتح القدير فلا بد من دعوته واقتصار الشارح على الأكثر في قوله «وإلا لا» ينبغي وقد صرح في فتح القدير بما ذكرناه وأطلق في الأمة فشمل المدخول بها وغيرها كما أطلق في الطلاق فشمل الرجعي والبائن الواحدة والثنتين وكل من الإطلاقين غير صحيح فإن كان بعد الدخول فلا فرق بين الرجعي والبائن إذا كان واحدة، وإن كان قبل الدخول ولأقل من سنتين منه، فالإيجاب على إبهامه ولا تتعين ضرتها للطلاق؛ ذكره في الزيادات. وثانيها ما لو قال لها إذا حبلت فأنت طالق فولدت لأقل من سنتين من وقت التعليق لا يقع الطلاق، وكذا لو كان هذا في تعليق العتاق بالحبل وثالثها المطلقة الرجعية إذا جاءت به لأقل من سنتين من وقت الطلاق لا يصير مراجعاً، ولو كانت الحوادث تضاف إلى أقرب الأوقات لثبتت هذه الأحكام أعني البيان والطلاق والرجعة قلنا الحوادث إنما تضاف إلى أقرب الأوقات إذا لم تتضمن إبطال ما كان ثابتاً بالدليل أو ترك العمل بالمقتضى أما إذا تضمن فلا فمتى عولت على ما قلنا ثم استقريت المسائل
His saying: (And the maximum period of pregnancy is two years) due to the saying of A'ishah (may Allah be pleased with her): 'The child does not remain in the womb more than two years, even if by a spindle's shadow.' Narrated by al-Daraqutni and al-Bayhaqi. And it is not known except by hearsay. 'Shadow of a spindle' is an expression for its shortness, because its shadow during rotation moves away faster than other shadows. It is with the deleted genitive, its appreciation: 'even if by the measure of a spindle's shadow.' And it is narrated: 'even if by the whorl of a spindle' i.e., even if by the measure of the rotation of a spindle's whorl. His saying: (And its minimum is six months) due to the saying of the Most High: 'And his gestation and weaning take thirty months' [Al-Ahqaf:15] then He said: 'And his weaning takes two years' [Luqman:14], so six months remain for pregnancy; thus in al-Hidayah. And it is transmitted in Fath al-Qadir that there is no disagreement among scholars about this. And an objection was raised against what is in al-Hidayah that it contradicts what was established for Abu Hanifa regarding suckling, that this period is fully multiplied for each of pregnancy and weaning, except that the reduction occurred in one of them, pregnancy, due to the hadith of A'ishah. We said: we presented there that it is not correct, because of the consequence that by the word 'thirty' in a single application, it means literally thirty and twenty-four considering two additions; perhaps he returned to the correct view. His saying: (So if he married a slave-girl and divorced her and then bought her and she gave birth in less than six months from it i.e., from the time of purchase) his saying: (it is binding on him, otherwise not) i.e., if she gave birth after full six months or more, it is not binding on him. Because in the first case, it is the child of the waiting woman ('iddah), for conception preceded the purchase. And in the second case, it is the child of the slave-girl, because the event is added to its nearest time when it does not entail nullifying what is established by evidence or abandoning acting upon the requirement. And with this, what was objected against it is repelled, as known in Fath al-Qadir, so his claim is necessary. And the commentator's restriction to the majority in his saying 'otherwise not' is appropriate. And it is explicitly stated in Fath al-Qadir what we mentioned. And he used absolute terms in the slave-girl, so it includes the one with whom he had intercourse and the one without, just as he used absolute terms in divorce, so it includes revocable and irrevocable, one and two. But each absolute term is incorrect. For if after consummation, there is no difference between revocable and irrevocable if it is one. And if before consummation, and less than two years from it, then the suspension is upon ambiguity and her co-wife is not specified for divorce; mentioned in al-Ziyadat. And the second (example): if he said to her: 'When you become pregnant, you are divorced' and she gave birth in less than two years from the time of suspension, the divorce does not occur. Similarly if this was in suspension of manumission upon pregnancy. And the third (example): the woman divorced revocably if she comes with a child in less than two years from the time of divorce, she does not become returned (to the marriage). And if events were added to the nearest times, these rulings would be established, namely specification, divorce, and return. We say: events are only added to the nearest times when it does not entail nullifying what is established by evidence or abandoning acting upon the requirement. So if it entails, then no. Whenever you rely on what we said and then review the cases (you will find consistency).
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage contains rulings on the minimum and maximum duration of pregnancy and an applied case. The matn first states that the maximum pregnancy duration is two years, supported by a hadith from A'ishah (radiyallahu 'anha) reported by al-Daraqutni and al-Bayhaqi. The commentator explains the expression 'shadow of a spindle' as a simile for extreme shortness. The minimum is six months, derived from combining the Quranic verses about gestation and weaning (30 months total) and weaning alone (2 years = 24 months), leaving 6 months for gestation. The commentator notes that this is unanimously accepted among scholars. He then addresses an objection regarding a possible inconsistency with Abu Hanifa's view on the period for breastfeeding, but refutes it by explaining that the 30 months can be interpreted as either 30 or 24 depending on context. The second part deals with a scenario: a man marries a slave-girl, divorces her, then buys her, and she gives birth. If the birth occurs less than 6 months after the purchase, the child is attributed to the man (as a child of the 'iddah period, conceived before purchase), so paternity is established. If 6 months or more, the child is considered her child as a slave-girl (since conception likely occurred after purchase), so paternity is not automatically established. The commentator explains the principle: events are attributed to their nearest possible time unless that would invalidate established evidence or abandon a necessary rule. He then lists three examples to demonstrate the application: (1) a man says to his two wives 'one of you is thrice divorced' without specification, then one gives birth less than 2 years later; the ambiguity is not resolved. (2) A man says 'when you become pregnant, you are divorced' and she gives birth less than 2 years later; divorce does not occur. (3) A revocably divorced woman gives birth less than 2 years after divorce; this does not constitute a return to marriage. The response to the objection clarifies that the principle of attributing events to the nearest time is not absolute; it is only applied when it does not contradict stronger evidence or necessary legal consequences.

Key Terms
الحمل (al-ḥaml): Pregnancy; the period a child is carried in the womb.
ظل مغزل (ẓill mighzal): A spindle's shadow; used to denote an extremely short period.
أقلها ستة أشهر (aqalluhā sittatu ashhur): Its minimum is six months; derived from Quranic calculations.
يضاف الحادث إلى أقرب وقته (yuḍāfu al-ḥādithu ilā aqrabi waqtihi): Legal principle: events are attributed to the nearest possible time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where a man marries a slave-girl, divorces her, then buys her and she gives birth, if the birth occurs 8 months after the purchase, what is the ruling regarding the child?
A) The child is attributed to the man as his own.
B) The child is the child of the slave-girl (milk yamin).
C) The child's paternity is suspended until further evidence.
D) The child is considered a foundling.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school determines pregnancy duration through Quranic and Hadith evidence, and applies the legal maxim with nuance, ensuring paternity rulings are just and consistent with stronger proofs.
